💼 About My Work Experience (Citibank Project — TCS)
▼

Currently I'm working in a Citi project. In this project, we are working on the conversion of legacy SAS scripts into Azure Databricks PySpark jobs.

📌 Previous Architecture (SAS-based):
Previously in SAS, data was extracted from SQL databases, business logic applied, and Excel reports generated. This was the existing legacy architecture.

Problems with the old approach:
  • ⚠️SAS processes ran sequentially, increasing overall execution time.
  • ⚠️Large Excel report generation slowed down the pipeline; downstream teams accessed these files inefficiently.
  • ⚠️Cost issues: SAS licensing and on-premise infrastructure maintenance overhead.
📌 New Architecture (Azure-based):

  • ✅Created a dedicated ingestion pipeline using Azure Data Factory to connect with SQL Server and load all source tables into ADLS Gen2 as raw data.
  • ✅The main Databricks PySpark job loads data from ADLS, applies business transformations using Spark, and writes transformed data back into ADLS.
  • ✅Azure Synapse Analytics is used for querying and analytical workloads; Power BI for reporting and dashboarding.
  • ✅Entire process orchestrated using ADF pipelines with dependency management, scheduled to run every Monday at 6 PM via ADF triggers.
  • ✅Notification emails sent through the configured monitoring and alerting mechanism on pipeline completion.
3
🏗️ Personal Project — NYC Taxi Lakehouse Analytics (Medallion Architecture)
▼

I worked on building an end-to-end Azure-based Lakehouse analytics platform using Medallion Architecture for NYC Taxi trip analytics.

🥉 Bronze Layer — Raw Ingestion:
Source data was publicly available NYC Taxi parquet datasets, dynamically ingested using Azure Data Factory pipelines from web/API sources into ADLS Gen2. Raw parquet files stored without transformation to preserve source fidelity.
🥈 Silver Layer — Transformation & Curation:
Used Azure Databricks with PySpark for data transformation: schema normalization, handling parquet schema drift across files, data quality validations, and business-rule validations. Curated datasets partitioned by pickup year and pickup month to optimize query performance.
🥇 Gold Layer — Business Marts (Delta Lake):
Built Delta Lake-based business marts for KPI reporting: daily KPI summaries, borough performance analytics, zone-level analytics, hourly trip pattern analysis, payment behavior analytics, and anomaly monitoring datasets. Delta format used for ACID transactions, transaction logging, and analytical optimization. Connected to Power BI for dashboards.
🔐 Security:
Implemented secure connectivity between Databricks and ADLS Gen2 using Azure Service Principal authentication and RBAC-based access control.
My Contribution:

Designed and implemented the ingestion and transformation architecture, developed dynamic ADF pipelines, built PySpark transformation logic in Databricks, handled schema inconsistencies in parquet datasets, implemented Silver-layer validation frameworks, created Gold-layer analytical marts using Delta Lake, and enabled BI connectivity through Power BI. Also responsible for optimizing the data model through partitioning strategies and designing reusable transformation logic for scalable processing.

⚡ Key Technical Challenges:

Challenge 1 — Schema Drift

Different parquet files had inconsistent datatypes like INT32, INT64, and DOUBLE for the same columns, causing Spark schema conversion failures. Solved by implementing dynamic file-level normalization logic where each parquet file was individually normalized and then unioned into a curated DataFrame.

Challenge 2 — Data Quality Validation

Some taxi records had abnormal durations, negative fares, refund trips, or inconsistent financial totals. Implemented business-rule validation frameworks and anomaly detection logic in the Silver layer to identify and flag suspicious records.

Challenge 3 — Analytical Optimization

Datasets were large and required efficient downstream querying. Optimized the Silver layer using partitioning strategies and built aggregated Gold marts in Delta Lake format for BI-ready analytics.

4
𖣑 Explain the architecture of an end-to-end ETL pipeline you have worked on.
▼

"I'll walk you through the Citibank project I'm currently working on at TCS — it's a real production pipeline."

Background: The client had legacy SAS scripts running on-prem that extracted data from SQL Server, applied business logic, and generated Excel reports. The problems were sequential processing, slow Excel generation, and high SAS licensing costs. We migrated the entire thing to Azure.

End-to-end pipeline flow
On-prem SQL Server → SHIR → ADF Copy Activity → ADLS Gen2 (Bronze) → Databricks PySpark → ADLS Gen2 (Silver/Gold) → Synapse Analytics → Power BI
  • Ingestion (ADF + SHIR): We set up a Self-Hosted Integration Runtime on the on-prem network to securely connect to SQL Server. ADF Copy Activities pull all source tables in parallel — not one by one — using a ForEach loop driven by a metadata config table in Azure SQL. This replaced the sequential SAS extraction.
  • Bronze layer (ADLS Gen2): Raw data lands here as Parquet files, no transformations. We preserve source fidelity — if something goes wrong downstream, we can always reprocess from Bronze without touching the source again.
  • Transformation (Databricks PySpark): This is where the actual business logic lives. We apply schema normalization, null handling, deduplication, business rule validation, and the equivalent of what SAS scripts used to do. Output is written as Delta Lake tables in Silver and Gold layers.
  • Serving (Synapse + Power BI): Synapse Serverless SQL creates views on top of Gold Delta tables. Power BI connects to these views for dashboards. This replaced the Excel report generation.
  • Orchestration (ADF): The entire pipeline is orchestrated by ADF — it runs the ingestion, then triggers the Databricks notebook via a Databricks activity, then sends a completion notification email. Scheduled every Monday at 6 PM via a schedule trigger.
  • Monitoring: Every run writes to a central audit table — rows in, rows out, start/end time, status. Azure Monitor alerts fire on any pipeline failure within 5 minutes.
💡
If they ask "what challenges did you face?" — mention the SHIR disk space issue (logs filling up, causing intermittent timeouts), and the schema drift in source tables (new columns appearing without notice). Both were real incidents we resolved.
5
🚨 What is the most common issue you face as a Data Engineer in day-to-day work?
▼

"In my experience across the Citi project and personal projects, the issues that come up most frequently aren't the exotic ones — they're the same categories of problems that show up in almost every data pipeline eventually."

1. Schema drift — the most silent killer:

Source systems add, rename, or change the type of a column without telling the data engineering team. The pipeline either fails at write time (if schema enforcement is enabled — the good outcome) or silently accepts the change and produces wrong data downstream (the bad outcome). On the Citi project, this happened regularly when the SAS scripts were updated to include new report columns. My fix was to add schema validation at the Bronze layer that detects new or missing columns and sends an alert before any transformation runs.

2. Data quality issues from the source:

Null values in non-nullable columns, negative amounts where only positives are valid, duplicate primary keys, date formats that change across files, truncated strings. These never show up in dev (where data is clean samples) and always show up in production (where data is messy reality). I've learned to add explicit assertions in the Silver layer — zero-null checks on join keys, range checks on numeric columns, uniqueness checks on primary keys — rather than trusting source data quality.

3. Pipeline scheduling and dependency issues:

Downstream pipeline runs before upstream finishes, or a trigger fires while the previous run is still active. On the Citi project, the Databricks transformation job depends on ADF completing the ingestion first — if the ADF run was delayed by a network blip and the Databricks trigger fired on schedule, it would process stale data. I handled this with ADF dependency chains (Databricks activity runs only after Copy Activities succeed) and tumbling window triggers with overlap protection.

4. Infrastructure issues — SHIR, network, credentials:

Self-hosted Integration Runtime disk filling up, expired service principal credentials, Key Vault access policy changes after an infra team rotation, firewall rules blocking a new server. These are the 2 AM alerts that have nothing to do with data or code. My counter is proactive monitoring: Azure Monitor alerts on SHIR disk usage, key expiry alerts via Key Vault, and a runbook documenting every infra dependency so the on-call engineer knows where to look.

5. The small things that compound:

Small files accumulating over weeks making queries slow. Databricks clusters left running over the weekend burning cost. A hardcoded date filter from initial development that breaks when the data crosses a year boundary. None of these are dramatic incidents — they're quiet degradation that only someone actively monitoring notices.

💡
"The most important career lesson from dealing with these: build for failure, not for the happy path. Every pipeline I write now assumes the source will send bad data, the infra will have hiccups, and the schedule will drift. Defensive engineering — validation, alerting, idempotency — is what separates a reliable pipeline from a fragile one."

General & System Design
Architecture patterns, scaling, cost optimization, debugging
1
What optimization techniques that you have applied in your day-to-day work so far in your four years of experience?
Optimization
▼

In my four years, the optimization techniques I applied depended on the layer I was working on.

  • Source / Ingestion layer: I avoided full loads wherever possible. In my project, we used watermark columns to extract only changed records since the last run — this alone reduced ingestion time significantly since we weren't pulling millions of unchanged rows every day.
  • ADF / Orchestration layer: I moved from sequential pipeline execution to parallel — using ForEach with a config-driven metadata table so multiple tables load simultaneously. I also parameterized pipelines so the same pipeline handles all tables instead of duplicating logic.
  • PySpark / Processing layer: I always push filters early and select only required columns — not SELECT *. I replaced Python UDFs with native Spark functions wherever possible since UDFs break Catalyst optimization. I also avoided unnecessary wide transformations like unnecessary groupBys and sorts, which cause expensive shuffles. Enabled AQE so Spark can dynamically adjust at runtime.
  • Storage layer: Switched from CSV to Parquet and Delta format — columnar storage, compressed, faster to scan. Applied partitioning by date columns so downstream queries only read relevant partitions. On the NYC Taxi project, I also ran OPTIMIZE with Z-ORDER on Gold layer Delta tables to compact small files and improve query performance.
  • Cost layer: Used spot instances for Databricks batch jobs, paused Synapse Dedicated Pool during off-hours, and set ADLS lifecycle policies to move older raw files to cheaper storage tiers automatically.
💡
If they ask for a specific example — mention the UDF replacement (30% runtime reduction) or the parallel ForEach ingestion (hours saved on sequential loads). Always close with a before/after number if you can.
2
How would you design a scalable system / data warehouse to handle millions of requests per day?
ArchitecturePattern
▼

So when I think about designing for millions of requests per day, I always start by asking — what kind of requests? Are they read-heavy analytical queries, or write-heavy ingestion? Because the answer changes the architecture completely.

In my current project context, we're dealing with high-volume data ingestion from on-prem SQL Server into ADLS Gen2, and then serving it for analytical reporting. Here's how I'd approach it end to end:

  • Ingestion layer: Use ADF with parallel Copy Activities — don't load tables one by one. Use a ForEach loop over a config table so all source tables load simultaneously. Set partition options on the source so ADF reads in parallel threads within each table too.
  • Storage layer: ADLS Gen2 with Hierarchical Namespace. Organize into Bronze / Silver / Gold. Compress everything as Parquet — it's columnar, much smaller, and faster to query than CSV. Partition data by date so downstream queries only scan what they need.
  • Processing layer: Databricks with auto-scaling clusters. For millions of records daily, the job needs to be distributed — PySpark handles that. Write output as Delta Lake tables so we get ACID transactions, and if something fails mid-run, we don't corrupt the target.
  • Serving layer: Synapse Dedicated Pool for BI dashboards where you need fast, consistent query performance. Use Hash distribution on the most-joined column — so when analysts run reports, data is already co-located. For ad-hoc queries, Synapse Serverless — pay per scan, no always-on cost.
  • Scalability specifically: Make sure nothing in the pipeline is a single-threaded bottleneck. Every step should be parallelizable. Use Databricks autoscaling so on heavy days the cluster grows, on light days it shrinks. Use ADF triggers with tumbling windows so even if one day's run is slow, the next day's doesn't back up.
💡
If they push further and ask "what if it needs to scale to billions?" — mention partitioning strategy review, Z-ordering in Delta Lake, and potentially moving to a streaming architecture with Event Hubs + Structured Streaming instead of daily batch.
3
How would you design a cost-optimized batch pipeline in Azure for large-scale daily processing?
Cost Optimization
▼

Cost optimization in Azure is something I actively think about on the project. The biggest cost levers in a data platform are compute, storage, and data transfer — and most teams overspend on compute because they set it up once and forget about it.

  • Databricks — biggest cost item: Never use all-purpose clusters for production jobs. Use job clusters — they spin up only for that specific run and auto-terminate when done. You're not paying for idle time. Also enable spot/low-priority VMs for batch workloads — up to 90% cheaper than on-demand. The job might occasionally get preempted but Databricks retries automatically.
  • Right-size before you scale: I always profile the job first — check Spark UI for spill, skew, and idle executors. Most slow jobs aren't slow because the cluster is too small, they're slow because of a data skew or an unnecessary shuffle. Fix the code first, then adjust cluster size.
  • ADF cost: Copy Activity is cheap. Data Flows (Mapping Data Flows) run Spark in the background and are significantly more expensive. For simple column mappings and filters, I use Copy Activity + a Databricks notebook instead of a Data Flow — same result, lower cost.
  • Storage cost: Compress everything to Parquet. Parquet + Snappy compression is typically 70-80% smaller than CSV. Set lifecycle policies on ADLS — raw Bronze files older than 30 days move to Cool tier, older than 90 days to Archive. You're not querying 6-month-old raw data daily.
  • Synapse Dedicated Pool: Pause it when not in use — this is a big one. You pay per DWU-hour. If your pipeline finishes at 8 AM and BI users stop at 6 PM, pause it at 6 PM via an ADF pipeline and resume it before the next morning run. That alone can cut the Synapse bill by 40%.
  • Monitor with Azure Cost Management: Tag every resource with environment and project. Set budget alerts. Review Azure Advisor recommendations monthly — it often flags underused resources you've forgotten about.
💡
A good line to say in interviews: "Cost optimization isn't a one-time task — I review cluster metrics and Azure Cost Management monthly. The biggest savings usually come from things that were set up correctly once and then never revisited as data volumes changed."
4
How would you design a metadata-driven ETL framework using Azure Data Factory? A client wants to ingest data from 100+ source systems with minimal manual effort.
▼

"This is exactly what I built on the Citi project — not 100 sources, but the same principle. The idea is to move all pipeline-specific configuration out of the ADF pipeline itself and into a config table. The pipeline becomes generic and config-driven."

Architecture:

  • Control table in Azure SQL: One row per source table with all the metadata needed to ingest it — source system, table name, watermark column, last load timestamp, target path, load mode, active flag, priority.
  • Master pipeline: Lookup → ForEach → Child Pipeline. The Lookup reads all active rows from the control table. ForEach iterates with parallel execution (up to 50 concurrent). Each iteration calls a generic child pipeline with the row's values as parameters.
  • Generic child pipeline: Accepts parameters (table name, watermark column, source type, target path, mode). Uses dynamic content expressions everywhere — source query, target path, linked service selection all driven by parameters.
  • Post-load: After each successful load, update the watermark timestamp in the control table using a Stored Procedure Activity — so the next run picks up only new/changed records.
🗄 SQL
-- Control table schema
CREATE TABLE etl_config (
    config_id       INT IDENTITY PRIMARY KEY,
    source_system   VARCHAR(50),    -- 'sql_server', 'oracle', 'api'
    table_name      VARCHAR(100),
    watermark_col   VARCHAR(50),
    last_load_date  DATETIME,
    target_path     VARCHAR(200),
    load_mode       VARCHAR(20),    -- 'incremental', 'full'
    is_active       BIT DEFAULT 1,
    priority        INT DEFAULT 1
);

-- Adding a new source = inserting one row
INSERT INTO etl_config VALUES (
    'sql_server', 'dim_product', 'modified_date',
    '1900-01-01', '/mnt/bronze/dim_product', 'incremental', 1, 1
);

For 100+ source systems specifically:

  • Use one Linked Service per source system type (not per table) — parameterized with server/database name. This keeps linked service count manageable.
  • Group sources by priority in the control table — mission-critical tables run first, reference data runs last.
  • Add error handling: wrap the child pipeline in a Try-Catch equivalent (success/failure paths). Failed tables are logged to an error table, not stopping the entire batch.
  • Build a monitoring dashboard in Power BI on top of the audit table — operations team can see what ran, when, how many rows, any failures — without touching ADF.
💡
"The key insight: the pipeline is infrastructure, the config table is the business logic. Engineers maintain the pipeline once. Adding a new source table is a data entry task, not an engineering task."
5
How do you design a pipeline that guarantees exactly-once processing? What are the trade-offs between idempotency and deduplication?
▼

"Exactly-once is one of the hardest guarantees in distributed systems — because networks fail, jobs restart, and sources sometimes send the same data twice. The practical approach is not to achieve true exactly-once delivery (which is extremely hard), but to make every step idempotent — meaning running it twice gives the same result as running it once."

How I design for exactly-once:

  • Idempotent writes using MERGE: Instead of blindly appending, I MERGE on the natural key — if the record already exists, update it; if not, insert it. Running the same batch 10 times gives the same result as running it once.
  • Checkpointing for streaming: Structured Streaming checkpoints track which source offsets have been processed. If the job restarts, it resumes exactly where it left off — no reprocessing, no skipping.
  • Watermark columns for batch: Track the last successfully processed timestamp. On next run, only process records newer than the watermark. Even if the job reruns the same window, the watermark ensures no duplicates land in the output.
  • Partition overwrite for idempotent batch writes: Instead of append, overwrite the specific partition being processed. Rerunning the same date's partition always produces the same result.
🐍 PySpark
# Idempotent write — MERGE pattern
from delta.tables import DeltaTable

def idempotent_write(batch_df, batch_id):
    delta_table = DeltaTable.forPath(spark, "/mnt/delta/orders")
    delta_table.alias("t").merge(
        batch_df.alias("s"),
        "t.order_id = s.order_id"
    ).whenMatchedUpdateAll() \
     .whenNotMatchedInsertAll() \
     .execute()

# Partition overwrite — idempotent for batch date processing
spark.conf.set("spark.sql.sources.partitionOverwriteMode", "dynamic")
df.write.format("delta") \
  .partitionBy("process_date") \
  .mode("overwrite") \
  .save("/mnt/delta/orders")

Idempotency vs Deduplication — the trade-off:

  • Idempotency — designing the write operation so re-running it doesn't create new records. The source of truth prevents duplicates at write time. Simple, elegant, works well when you control the write. Downside: requires a reliable natural key to MERGE on.
  • Deduplication — detecting and removing duplicates after they've been written. Flexible — works even without a natural key (can dedup on composite columns or hash). Downside: more expensive (requires scanning and comparing records), and duplicates exist temporarily until the dedup job runs. Also stateful in streaming — maintaining a "seen events" state store has memory implications.
  • When to use which: If I control the write layer → idempotency first (MERGE). If the source inherently sends duplicates and I can't prevent them at write → deduplication layer in Silver. In most production pipelines I use both — idempotent writes as the primary defence, plus a dedup check in the Silver transformation as a safety net.
💡
"Exactly-once is a spectrum, not a binary. At-most-once (may lose data), at-least-once (may duplicate), exactly-once (neither). In practice, I design for at-least-once delivery with idempotent processing — which gives the same end result as exactly-once but is much simpler to implement reliably."
6
How do you handle schema inference vs predefined schema in PySpark? Which is better in production?
▼

"Schema inference means Spark reads a sample of your data and guesses the schema. Predefined schema means you tell Spark exactly what the schema is upfront. In development, inference is convenient. In production, it's a risk."

Why schema inference is dangerous in production:

  • To infer schema, Spark reads the entire file (or a large sample) before processing — adding overhead on large datasets.
  • Inference can be wrong — a column with mostly integers but one string value gets inferred as string. Or a column that's null in today's file gets inferred as a different type than yesterday's file, causing a schema mismatch at write time.
  • On CSV files, every column is inferred as string by default unless you enable full scan — which is even more expensive.
  • Schema can silently change across runs — a source adds a column or changes a type, and your pipeline accepts it without alerting you.
🐍 PySpark
from pyspark.sql.types import (
    StructType, StructField,
    StringType, IntegerType, DoubleType, TimestampType
)

# ── Schema inference — development only ───────────────
df = spark.read.option("inferSchema", "true").csv("/mnt/bronze/orders.csv")
# Spark scans the file to guess types — slow and unreliable at scale

# ── Predefined schema — production standard ────────────
schema = StructType([
    StructField("order_id",    StringType(),    nullable=False),
    StructField("customer_id", StringType(),    nullable=False),
    StructField("amount",      DoubleType(),    nullable=True),
    StructField("order_date",  TimestampType(), nullable=True),
    StructField("status",      StringType(),    nullable=True),
])

df = spark.read.schema(schema).csv("/mnt/bronze/orders.csv")
# Spark uses the schema directly — no scan needed, no guessing

# ── Schema validation — detect drift ──────────────────
expected_fields = {f.name: f.dataType for f in schema.fields}
actual_fields   = {f.name: f.dataType for f in df.schema.fields}

new_cols     = set(actual_fields) - set(expected_fields)
missing_cols = set(expected_fields) - set(actual_fields)

if new_cols or missing_cols:
    raise ValueError(
        f"Schema drift detected! New: {new_cols}, Missing: {missing_cols}"
    )

# ── Reading Parquet — schema stored in file metadata ──
# Parquet already stores schema — no inferSchema needed
# But still validate against expected schema before processing
df = spark.read.format("parquet").load("/mnt/bronze/orders/")
assert set(expected_fields.keys()).issubset(set(df.columns)), "Missing expected columns"
💡
"My rule: inferSchema=True in a Jupyter notebook, never in a production pipeline. Always define the schema explicitly, and always validate incoming schema against expected schema at the Bronze layer. Schema drift should be a caught exception, not a silent corruption."
7
What are the differences between horizontal and vertical scaling? When would you use each?
Fundamentals
▼

Vertical scaling (Scale Up) means adding more CPU, RAM, or disk to the same machine. Simpler — no code changes — but has a hard ceiling and creates a single point of failure.

Horizontal scaling (Scale Out) means adding more machines or nodes. Requires distributed systems thinking but offers virtually unlimited ceiling.

  • Use vertical scaling for relational DBs that are hard to shard, or for quick wins on legacy systems.
  • Use horizontal scaling for Spark jobs, Kafka brokers, ADLS workloads — anywhere data partitions well across nodes.
8
How would you process incremental data without creating duplicates (Idempotency)?
Pattern
▼

An idempotent pipeline produces the same result whether it runs once or ten times. This is critical because pipelines fail and need to re-run.

  • Watermark table: Store the last processed timestamp or ID. Each run reads only rows newer than the watermark. After a successful load, update the watermark.
  • Delta Lake MERGE (UPSERT): Match on a unique business key — update if the row exists, insert if new. Naturally handles duplicates from re-runs.
  • Partition overwrite: Write new data to a date partition, overwrite that partition on re-run. Avoids duplicates by design when partitioned by ingestion date.
  • Dedup in PySpark: df.dropDuplicates(["id", "updated_at"]) before writing.
9
A pipeline completed successfully but produced incorrect data. How would you debug it?
Debugging
▼

This is the hardest class of bug — the system thinks everything succeeded. Work through these steps:

  1. Narrow down the layer. Check Bronze → Silver → Gold sequentially. At which layer does data first look wrong?
  2. Check transformation logic. Are joins on the wrong key? Is a GROUP BY missing a column? Are window functions using the right partition/order?
  3. Check data quality at source. Was the source data itself corrupt, late-arriving, or duplicated?
  4. Check watermark/filter logic. Did the incremental filter cut off valid records or include old ones twice?
  5. Check date/timezone issues. UTC vs local time bugs are extremely common.
  6. Enable row counts + checksums. Add audit columns at each layer — row_count, checksum, load_timestamp — so discrepancies become visible.
  7. Delta Lake time travel: SELECT * FROM table VERSION AS OF N to compare data before and after a specific run.
10
A data pipeline is failing intermittently in production. How would you debug and fix it?
Debugging
▼

"Intermittent failures are actually harder to debug than consistent failures — because by the time you look at it, it's working again. So the first thing I do is resist the urge to immediately restart the pipeline and call it done. I want to understand why it failed before I fix anything."

Step 1 — Reproduce and capture:

  • Check ADF Monitor first — look at the exact activity that failed, the error message, and the timestamp. Screenshot it or copy the JSON error before rerunning — these details disappear once you restart.
  • Note the pattern — is it failing at the same time every day? On Mondays only? When data volume is higher? That pattern tells you a lot.

Step 2 — Narrow down the layer:

  • Is it an infrastructure issue? — SHIR disconnected, disk full on the VM, network timeout. I've had this on my project — the SHIR VM's disk filled up with diagnostic logs and the Copy Activity started timing out intermittently. Fixed by adding log rotation and a disk usage alert in Azure Monitor.
  • Is it a data issue? — a source table sent an unexpected null in a non-nullable column, or a new column appeared that broke the schema. This is why I always add schema validation at Bronze ingestion.
  • Is it a resource issue? — Databricks executor OOM on certain days when data volume spikes. Check Spark UI — look at task duration variance and spill to disk.
  • Is it a dependency issue? — upstream pipeline didn't finish, so the data wasn't ready. ADF pipeline dependencies should handle this, but sometimes trigger timing is off.

Step 3 — Fix and prevent:

  • Fix the root cause, not just the symptom. If it OOM'd because Monday has 3x more data than other days, the fix isn't "restart it" — it's either increase executor memory for the job cluster, or add salting to fix the data skew that's causing one executor to take all the load.
  • Add retry policies on the failing activity — 3 retries with 5-minute intervals handles most transient network issues without human intervention.
  • Add an alert so next time it fails, I know within 5 minutes instead of when a business user calls at 9 AM.
💡
Good phrase for the interview: "My rule is — don't just fix the failure, fix the detection. After every production incident, I ask: why didn't our monitoring catch this before someone noticed? Then I add the alert that would have caught it."
11
Your pipeline is stuck in "Queued" state. What are the first 3 things you check?
DebuggingScenario
▼

"Queued usually means the pipeline is waiting for resources or permission to run, not that it's actually failing. I check these three things, in this order."

1. Cluster availability / resource contention: In Databricks, a queued job often means the cluster is starting up, all available spot instances are taken, or the cluster pool is exhausted. Check the cluster Events tab for startup errors or spot eviction notices. In ADF, check if the Integration Runtime is online — if SHIR is offline, activities queue indefinitely. I check the cluster event log for messages like "waiting for instance" or "pool exhausted.

2. Integration Runtime status (for ADF): If it's an ADF pipeline using a Self-Hosted IR, I check whether the SHIR machine is online and has free capacity. SHIR has a limited number of concurrent jobs it can run — if too many pipelines are queued on the same SHIR, new ones wait in line. I check the IR's node status in the ADF Studio Manage tab.

3. Concurrency limits: Both ADF and Databricks have concurrency caps — ADF has a limit on concurrent pipeline runs per subscription/factory, and Databricks workspaces have job concurrency limits. If too many other pipelines are running simultaneously, new ones queue until a slot frees up. I check how many other pipelines or jobs are currently active.

4. Concurrency limits: In ADF, check if the pipeline concurrency setting is 1 and another instance is already running. In Synapse, check workload management — if all concurrency slots are consumed, new queries queue. Check sys.dm_pdw_waits and sys.dm_pdw_exec_requests for queued requests and what resources they are waiting for.

5. Trigger or dependency issue: Is the pipeline waiting for a predecessor to complete? In ADF, check pipeline dependencies. Is a tumbling window trigger still processing historical windows one at a time? In Databricks, check if the job is waiting on a preceding task in a multi-task job that has not yet completed.

  • If it's none of these — I also check for a circular or unresolved dependency in the trigger or pipeline dependency chain, which can cause a pipeline to wait indefinitely for a condition that will never be satisfied.
💡
"My mental shortcut: Queued = waiting for something, not broken. I check compute capacity, IR/connector capacity, then concurrency limits — in that order, because that's roughly the order of how often each one is actually the cause."
12
You received duplicate records in streaming data — how do you handle deduplication?
Streaming
▼

Duplicates in streaming are actually very common — they happen because of network retries, producer restarts, or exactly-once delivery not being guaranteed end to end. So I treat deduplication as something I have to design for, not something I hope won't happen.

First, I identify what makes a record unique — usually it's a combination of event_id + event_timestamp. Without a natural unique key, you can't reliably deduplicate.

Then I choose the approach based on the use case:

  • Structured Streaming with watermark (most common): Spark's built-in deduplication — you define a watermark on the event time column, then call dropDuplicates(["event_id"]). Spark keeps a state store of event IDs it's seen within the watermark window. Any duplicate arriving within that window gets dropped automatically. Events arriving after the watermark are assumed late and dropped too — so I set the watermark generously, like 10 to 30 minutes depending on how late data can realistically arrive.
    🐍 PySpark
    df.withWatermark("event_time", "10 minutes") \
  .dropDuplicates(["event_id"]) \
  .writeStream \
  .format("delta") \
  .option("checkpointLocation", "/mnt/checkpoints/events") \
  .start("/mnt/delta/events")
  • Delta Lake MERGE in foreachBatch (my preferred for production): Instead of dropDuplicates, in each micro-batch I do a MERGE into the Delta target table — if the event_id already exists, update it; if not, insert it. This is naturally idempotent — running the same batch twice gives the same result. I use this when duplicates can come hours later, beyond what a watermark window would cover.
    🐍 PySpark
    def upsert_to_delta(batch_df, batch_id):
    batch_df.createOrReplaceTempView("updates")
    batch_df._jdf.sparkSession().sql("""
        MERGE INTO events_delta t
        USING updates s ON t.event_id = s.event_id
        WHEN MATCHED THEN UPDATE SET *
        WHEN NOT MATCHED THEN INSERT *
    """)

df.writeStream \
  .foreachBatch(upsert_to_delta) \
  .option("checkpointLocation", "/mnt/checkpoints/events") \
  .start()
  • External state store (Redis/Cosmos DB): When the deduplication window needs to be days or weeks — beyond what Spark's in-memory state can hold — I check an external key-value store. Before processing each event, check if event_id exists in Redis. If yes, skip it. If no, process and write the ID to Redis with a TTL. This works across streaming restarts too, since Spark's internal state resets but Redis persists.
💡
A good thing to mention: "Checkpointing is non-negotiable with streaming deduplication — without a checkpoint, if the job restarts it loses its state and will reprocess everything, potentially inserting duplicates it already dropped. Always set a checkpoint location on a reliable storage like ADLS."
13
Multiple applications depend on the same dataset, but each requires different refresh frequencies. How would you design the data architecture?
DesignScenario
▼

This is actually a very real problem. In my Citi project, the same Silver layer data is consumed by a Power BI dashboard that refreshes daily, a Synapse analytical query that runs weekly, and an ops team report that needs near real-time numbers. So I've thought about this carefully.

The answer is — don't try to serve everyone from the same layer at the same frequency. Instead, separate the concerns using the Medallion Architecture pattern.

  • Bronze layer — raw, append-only, as frequent as the source allows: This is your single source of truth. Everything lands here. You never delete from Bronze. If a source can push every 15 minutes, Bronze gets updated every 15 minutes — but downstream consumers don't touch Bronze directly.
  • Silver layer — cleaned, deduplicated, refreshed at a moderate frequency: This is where you do schema normalization, null handling, deduplication. Refresh this based on the fastest consumer's need — if someone needs hourly data, Silver runs hourly. This layer is shared across all consumers.
  • Gold layer — purpose-built per consumer, each with its own refresh schedule: This is where the frequency split happens. You create separate Gold tables or views for each application:
    • Power BI dashboard → Gold table refreshed daily at 6 AM via ADF scheduled trigger
    • Ops team report → Gold table refreshed every hour via tumbling window trigger
    • Weekly analytics → Gold table refreshed every Sunday via schedule trigger
  • Each Gold table reads from the same Silver layer — so the cleaning logic is written once. Only the aggregation, refresh schedule, and output format differ per consumer.
  • Use Delta Lake for all layers: Because multiple pipelines running at different schedules on the same data can cause read/write conflicts. Delta's ACID guarantees mean a Power BI query reading the daily Gold table won't see half-written data from the hourly pipeline updating it at the same time.
💡
If they ask "what if a consumer needs real-time?" — introduce Structured Streaming from the Bronze layer into a separate streaming Gold table, while batch consumers continue reading from their scheduled Gold tables. The Silver layer stays shared between both.
14
Describe how you ensure data quality and validation in your ETL process.
Data QualityBest Practice
▼

"Data quality is not something I add at the end — I bake it into every layer of the pipeline from day one. The earlier you catch a bad record, the cheaper it is to fix."

At the Bronze layer — structural checks:

  • Schema validation — compare incoming schema against the expected schema before writing. If a new column appears or a column type changes, the pipeline alerts instead of silently accepting it.
  • Row count check — assert that the incoming data is not empty. Zero rows almost always means something broke upstream, not that the source genuinely had no data today.
  • File arrival check — in ADF, use an event-based trigger or a Get Metadata activity to verify the source file actually landed before the pipeline proceeds.

At the Silver layer — business rule validation:

  • Null checks on critical columns — primary keys, foreign keys, and any column used in joins must have zero nulls. I assert this explicitly and fail loudly if violated.
  • Uniqueness check — no duplicate primary keys in the output. If I find duplicates, I log them to a separate bad records table and investigate rather than just dropping them silently.
  • Range validation — amounts should be greater than or equal to zero, dates should fall within a valid range, status columns should only contain allowed values.
  • Referential integrity — every foreign key should have a matching record in the dimension table. Orphaned fact records mean a join key problem somewhere upstream.

How I implement this in PySpark:

🐍 PySpark
def validate(df, table_name):
    checks = {
        "not_empty":     df.count() > 0,
        "no_null_id":    df.filter(col("customer_id").isNull()).count() == 0,
        "no_neg_amount": df.filter(col("amount") < 0).count() == 0,
        "no_dup_keys":   df.count() == df.dropDuplicates(["customer_id"]).count()
    }
    failed = [k for k, v in checks.items() if not v]
    if failed:
        raise ValueError(f"{table_name} failed: {failed}")

At the Gold layer — reconciliation:

  • Row count reconciliation — compare total rows loaded today against yesterday's count. If today's count is less than 80% of yesterday's without a business reason, alert the team.
  • Aggregate reconciliation — sum of revenue in Gold should match sum of revenue in Silver after transformations. Any mismatch means the transformation logic has a bug.

Audit logging: Every pipeline run writes a row to a central audit table — pipeline name, run ID, rows in, rows out, status, error message, start and end time. I built a simple Power BI dashboard on top of this so the team has visibility without needing to dig into ADF Monitor every day.

💡
Good line to say: "My rule is — never write bad data silently. Either fix it, route it to a dead-letter path with the reason logged, or fail loudly. Silent bad data is the worst outcome because nobody knows it's wrong until a business decision is made on it."
15
Your job runtime increased from 1 hour to 4 hours — what is your root-cause analysis approach?
DebuggingPerformance
▼

The first thing I do is not panic and not randomly restart the job. A 4x slowdown has a reason, and I want to find it before I touch anything.

Step 1 — Check if the data volume changed:

  • Go to the audit table and compare today's row count with the last few days. If data volume tripled, the slowdown may be proportional and expected — but still worth investigating whether the job scales linearly or has a bottleneck.
  • If volume is the same as before, something else changed — code, cluster config, or data distribution.

Step 2 — Open Spark UI and find the bottleneck stage:

  • Go to the Stages tab. Look for the stage that's taking the most time. Click into it and check:
    • Task duration variance — if most tasks finish in 2 seconds but one takes 40 minutes, that's data skew. One executor is getting all the heavy rows.
    • Shuffle read/write size — if this jumped significantly compared to before, a join or groupBy is shuffling much more data than it used to.
    • Spill to disk — non-zero spill means executors ran out of memory and started writing intermediate data to disk. Disk is 100x slower than RAM.
    • GC time — high garbage collection time means JVM is struggling with memory pressure.

Step 3 — Check what changed recently:

  • Was there a code change or a new transformation added? Check Git history.
  • Did the cluster configuration change — smaller node type, fewer workers, different DBR version?
  • Did a new data skew appear — like a new customer with millions of transactions that's now concentrating on one executor?
  • Did a cached DataFrame get evicted because another job ran on the same cluster and consumed the memory?

Step 4 — Fix based on what you found:

  • Data skew → add salting on the skewed key, or enable AQE skew join optimization (spark.sql.adaptive.skewJoin.enabled=true)
  • Shuffle explosion → check if a broadcast join is now possible, reduce shuffle partitions if data volume is actually smaller, push filters earlier
  • Spill to disk → increase executor memory, reduce partition size, or fix the skew that's causing one partition to be huge
  • Cluster config change → restore the original config or right-size properly based on the new data volume
💡
I always compare two Spark UI runs side by side — the last good run and the slow run. The difference in stage timings, shuffle sizes, and task counts usually points directly to the problem within 10 minutes.
16
Explain the difference between batch processing, micro-batch processing, and continuous streaming in Spark.
Spark Internals
▼

"These three represent different points on the latency-vs-throughput spectrum, and Spark actually supports all three, though in different ways."

  • Batch processing: The traditional model — you process a finite, complete dataset all at once. The job has a clear start and end. On the Citi project, our weekly SAS-to-Databricks pipeline is batch — it runs every Monday at 6 PM, processes that week's data, and finishes. Latency doesn't matter here; throughput and correctness do. We use regular DataFrame reads and writes for this.
  • Micro-batch processing (Structured Streaming default mode): This is Spark's main streaming model. Instead of processing one record at a time, Spark waits a small interval, then processes whatever arrived as a small batch — typically every few seconds. Each micro-batch goes through the full Spark engine: Catalyst optimization, task scheduling, the works. Latency is in the seconds range, not milliseconds, but you get all the reliability guarantees — exactly-once processing, fault tolerance through checkpointing, and the same DataFrame API as batch.
    • 🐍 PySpark
    df.writeStream \
  .format("delta") \
  .trigger(processingTime="10 seconds") \
  .option("checkpointLocation", "/mnt/checkpoints/stream") \
  .start("/mnt/delta/events")
  • Continuous processing (experimental in Spark): This is true record-by-record streaming — sub-millisecond latency, no micro-batching at all. It's still marked experimental in Spark and has significant limitations — only certain sources/sinks are supported, and it sacrifices some of the exactly-once guarantees that micro-batch gives you. I haven't used this in production; for genuinely low-latency needs, most teams reach for something like Kafka Streams or Flink instead.

How I'd decide which to use:

  • Daily/weekly reports, large historical reprocessing → Batch
  • Near-real-time dashboards, fraud alerts within seconds, event-driven pipelines → Micro-batch (Structured Streaming) — this covers 95% of "real-time" use cases I've actually needed
  • True sub-second latency requirements (rare) → look outside Spark, or accept the experimental risk of Continuous Processing
💡
"In practice, when people say they need 'real-time' in a Spark context, micro-batch with a short trigger interval — like every few seconds — almost always meets the actual business need. True millisecond-level continuous processing is rarely what the business actually requires once you dig into the SLA."
17
How do you implement parallel processing in Python for ETL workloads? How would you structure a reusable Python ETL framework?
▼

"In ETL workloads, most tasks are I/O-bound — connecting to a database, reading from an API, uploading to ADLS. Python's GIL doesn't block I/O operations, so threading gives real parallelism here. For CPU-bound heavy transformation, I rely on Spark rather than Python multiprocessing."

Parallel ingestion using ThreadPoolExecutor:

🐍 Python
from concurrent.futures import ThreadPoolExecutor, as_completed
import logging

logger = logging.getLogger(__name__)

# Config-driven table list
tables = [
    {"name": "orders",    "watermark_col": "updated_at"},
    {"name": "customers", "watermark_col": "modified_date"},
    {"name": "products",  "watermark_col": "updated_at"},
    {"name": "inventory", "watermark_col": "last_sync"},
]

def ingest_table(config: dict) -> dict:
    table = config["name"]
    try:
        df = read_from_sql(table, config["watermark_col"])
        write_to_adls(df, f"/mnt/bronze/{table}")
        return {"table": table, "status": "success", "rows": df.count()}
    except Exception as e:
        logger.error(f"{table} failed: {e}")
        return {"table": table, "status": "failed", "error": str(e)}

# Run all tables in parallel — max 5 at a time
results = []
with ThreadPoolExecutor(max_workers=5) as executor:
    futures = {executor.submit(ingest_table, t): t for t in tables}
    for future in as_completed(futures):
        results.append(future.result())

# Audit results
failed = [r for r in results if r["status"] == "failed"]
if failed:
    raise Exception(f"Failed tables: {[r['table'] for r in failed]}")

Reusable Python ETL framework structure:

🐍 Python
"""
etl_framework/
├── readers/
│   ├── base_reader.py       # abstract BaseReader class
│   ├── sql_reader.py        # SqlReader(BaseReader)
│   ├── adls_reader.py       # AdlsReader(BaseReader)
│   └── api_reader.py        # ApiReader(BaseReader)
├── transformers/
│   ├── base_transformer.py  # abstract BaseTransformer
│   ├── dedup.py             # DedupTransformer
│   ├── schema_validator.py  # SchemaValidator
│   └── type_caster.py       # TypeCaster
├── writers/
│   ├── base_writer.py       # abstract BaseWriter
│   ├── delta_writer.py      # DeltaWriter(BaseWriter) — MERGE
│   └── adls_writer.py       # AdlsWriter(BaseWriter)
├── utils/
│   ├── logger.py            # structured logging
│   ├── audit.py             # write audit rows to SQL
│   └── config.py            # load JSON/YAML config
└── pipeline.py              # orchestrates: read → validate → transform → write → audit
"""

# pipeline.py — generic, config-driven
class Pipeline:
    def __init__(self, config: dict):
        self.config = config
        self.reader      = self._get_reader()
        self.transformer = self._get_transformer()
        self.writer      = self._get_writer()

    def run(self):
        logger.info(f"Starting pipeline: {self.config['name']}")
        df = self.reader.read()
        df = self.transformer.transform(df)
        self.writer.write(df)
        audit.log(self.config["name"], df.count(), "success")

    def _get_reader(self):
        source = self.config["source"]["type"]
        if source == "sql":   return SqlReader(self.config["source"])
        if source == "adls":  return AdlsReader(self.config["source"])
        if source == "api":   return ApiReader(self.config["source"])
        raise ValueError(f"Unknown source type: {source}")

# Run from config — adding a new table = new JSON file, zero code change
config = {
    "name": "orders_ingestion",
    "source": {"type": "sql", "table": "orders", "watermark_col": "updated_at"},
    "target": {"type": "delta", "path": "/mnt/delta/silver/orders", "mode": "merge", "pk": "order_id"}
}
Pipeline(config).run()
💡
"The goal of a reusable framework is: adding a new source table is a config change, not a code change. One pipeline.py handles 1 table and 100 tables identically. That's what I built on the Citi project — onboarding a new table went from 2 days to under 2 hours."
☁️
Azure Data Factory
Integration runtime, pipelines, parameterization, error handling
1
What is Azure Data Factory? Explain its core components.
OverviewCore Concept
▼

Azure Data Factory is a cloud-based data integration service that allows you to create, schedule, and orchestrate data pipelines. Think of it as a pipeline builder — it connects to your source systems, moves the data, optionally transforms it, and loads it to your destination.

In simple terms — ADF is the orchestrator. It doesn't process big data itself, but it coordinates all the tools that do.

In my project, we use ADF to pull data from on-prem SQL Server via SHIR, land it in ADLS Gen2, trigger Databricks notebooks for transformation, and load results into Synapse — all automated and scheduled.

Core Components:

  • Pipeline: A logical grouping of activities that together perform a task. A pipeline is what you schedule and monitor. Think of it as the workflow definition — it says "first do this, then do that, and if it fails, do this instead."
  • Activity: A single step inside a pipeline. ADF has three types of activities:
    • Data movement — Copy Activity (moves data from source to sink)
    • Data transformation — Databricks notebook, Data Flow, Stored Procedure, HDInsight etc.
    • Control activities — ForEach, If Condition, Until, Wait, Set Variable — used to add logic and flow control inside a pipeline
  • Dataset: A named reference to the data you want to use — it points to a specific table, file, or folder inside a linked service. It defines the structure (schema) and location of the data. Example: a dataset pointing to orders table inside an Azure SQL linked service.
  • Linked Service: The connection string — it tells ADF how to connect to an external system like SQL Server, ADLS Gen2, Databricks, Synapse, Blob Storage etc. It stores the credentials (or references Key Vault for secrets). One linked service can be reused across many datasets and activities.
  • Integration Runtime (IR): The actual compute that ADF uses to execute activities and move data. Three types:
    • Azure IR — cloud-to-cloud data movement, managed by Microsoft, no setup needed
    • Self-hosted IR (SHIR) — installed on your on-premise machine or VM. Used when the source is behind a firewall or on-prem. On the Citi project, we use SHIR to connect to the on-prem SQL Server.
    • Azure-SSIS IR — used to run existing SSIS packages in the cloud
  • Trigger: Defines when a pipeline runs. Types:
    • Schedule trigger — runs at a fixed time/frequency (e.g., every Monday at 6 PM — which is what we use on Citi)
    • Tumbling window trigger — schedule-based but processes data in fixed non-overlapping time windows, supports retry and dependency between windows
    • Event-based trigger — fires when a file arrives in ADLS or a blob storage container
    • Manual trigger — triggered on demand
  • Data Flow (Mapping Data Flow): A visually designed transformation engine inside ADF. It runs on a Spark cluster in the background. Used when you want to do transformations — joins, aggregations, derived columns, pivots — without writing code. I use this for simple transformations; for complex ones I prefer Databricks notebooks since they're faster, cheaper, and easier to test.
  • Parameters & Variables: Parameters are passed into a pipeline at runtime (from outside). Variables are used inside the pipeline to store and update values during execution. Together they make pipelines reusable — one pipeline can handle 50 tables by just changing the parameter value.
📌
Quick summary to say in interview: "Pipeline is the workflow. Activity is each step. Dataset is what data to use. Linked Service is how to connect. Integration Runtime is the compute that does the actual work. Trigger is when it runs."
💡
A common follow-up is "what's the difference between parameters and variables?" — Parameters are set from outside when the pipeline is triggered (read-only inside the pipeline). Variables are created and updated inside the pipeline during execution. Think of parameters as inputs, variables as scratchpad.
2
ADLS Gen2 vs Azure Blob Storage — what are the key differences?
Storage
▼

Both are Azure object storage, but ADLS Gen2 = Blob Storage with Hierarchical Namespace (HNS) enabled. That one feature creates substantial differences:

  • Namespace: Blob = flat (folders are just key prefixes, no real directories). ADLS Gen2 = true hierarchical file system — directories are first-class objects. Rename a "folder" of 1 million files: Blob O(n), ADLS Gen2 O(1).
  • Atomic operations: ADLS Gen2 supports atomic rename and delete at directory level — critical for Spark job commits. Blob Storage requires listing and updating every blob, which is non-atomic and slow.
  • POSIX ACLs: ADLS Gen2 supports POSIX-style ACLs at file and directory level (read/write/execute per user/group). Blob Storage only has RBAC at container level.
  • Performance: ADLS Gen2 is optimized for big data analytics workloads (Hadoop/Spark compatible). Significantly higher throughput for parallel reads/writes.
  • Protocol: ADLS Gen2 supports both the DFS endpoint (abfss://) for analytics and the Blob endpoint (wasbs://) for backward compatibility.
  • Cost: ADLS Gen2 costs slightly more due to namespace metadata storage, but the performance gains far outweigh this for analytics.
📌
Rule: Use Blob Storage for simple file hosting, CDN origin, or backup. Use ADLS Gen2 for any analytics workload — Spark, ADF, Databricks, Synapse. Always enable HNS at account creation; it cannot be added later.
3
What is the difference between Copy Activity and Data Flow in ADF?
Core Concept
▼

Copy Activity is a lightweight, high-speed data movement tool. It moves data from source to sink with minimal transformation — column renaming, basic type casting. It does not spin up a Spark cluster; it runs on the Integration Runtime directly. Very fast, very cheap. Use it for ingestion — landing raw files in ADLS.

Data Flow (Mapping Data Flow) is a visual, code-free transformation engine backed by Apache Spark. You can join, aggregate, pivot, derive columns, parse JSON, and split streams — all via a drag-and-drop UI. ADF compiles your Data Flow into Spark code and runs it on an auto-created cluster.

💸
Data Flow is significantly more expensive — it spins up Spark compute. Only use it when transformation complexity justifies it. For simple copy-and-land, always use Copy Activity.
4
What different activities are you using in Azure Data Factory? Explain each.
ADFCore Concept
▼

"In ADF, activities are the individual steps inside a pipeline. They fall into three categories — data movement, transformation, and control flow. Let me walk through what I actually use on the Citi project."

Data Movement:

  • Copy Activity: The most used activity. Copies data from a source to a sink. I use this to pull tables from on-prem SQL Server into ADLS Gen2 as Parquet. It supports parallelism within a single table through partition options, and I can configure retry policies directly on it.

Transformation:

  • Databricks Notebook Activity: Triggers a specific Databricks notebook and waits for it to complete. I pass parameters to it — like the pipeline run date or the table name — so the same notebook can handle different scenarios without hardcoding values.
  • Stored Procedure Activity: Executes a SQL stored procedure in Azure SQL or Synapse. I use this for post-load operations — like updating a watermark table after ingestion, or running a TRUNCATE and reload on a summary table.
  • Mapping Data Flow: Visual transformation engine — joins, aggregations, derived columns — all without code. Runs on a Spark cluster in the background. I use this for simple transformations where spinning up a Databricks cluster would be overkill, but for complex business logic I prefer Databricks notebooks — more control, easier to test, cheaper.

Control Flow:

  • Lookup Activity: Reads from a source — usually a config/metadata table in Azure SQL — and returns the result to the pipeline. I use this at the start of every pipeline to fetch the list of tables to process, the watermark values, and any configuration parameters. This is what makes pipelines metadata-driven.
  • ForEach Activity: Iterates over a collection — usually the output from a Lookup — and runs an inner pipeline or activity for each item. I set it to run in parallel (up to 50 concurrent iterations) so all tables load simultaneously instead of one by one.
  • If Condition Activity: Branches the pipeline based on a true/false expression. I use this to check if a source file arrived before proceeding — if the file exists, continue; if not, send an alert and stop.
  • Set Variable / Append Variable: Stores values during pipeline execution. I use Set Variable to capture the current run date, row counts, or status flags that I later write to an audit table.
  • Wait Activity: Pauses the pipeline for a specified number of seconds. Rarely used, but helpful when you're waiting for an upstream system to finish before your pipeline reads from it.
  • Web Activity: Calls any REST API endpoint. I've used this to send Teams notifications when a critical pipeline finishes or fails — just a POST to the Teams webhook URL with a message payload.
  • Execute Pipeline Activity: Calls another pipeline from within the current one. I use this to break large pipelines into smaller modular child pipelines — ingestion pipeline, transformation pipeline, notification pipeline — and the parent pipeline orchestrates all three in sequence.
💡
The combination I use most: Lookup → ForEach → Copy Activity inside ForEach. This is the metadata-driven pattern that loads all tables in parallel from a single config table. It replaced what used to be 50 individual hardcoded pipelines.
X
What is the difference between Lookup Activity and Get Metadata Activity in ADF?
▼

"Both activities read information before the main pipeline logic runs — but they serve completely different purposes."

Lookup Activity: Reads actual data — rows and column values — from a source (SQL table, file, stored procedure result). Returns a result set that you use to drive downstream logic. Think of it as running a SELECT query and using the result inside the pipeline.

  • Returns: firstRow (single row) or value array (multiple rows, up to 5000)
  • Common uses: Get watermark value, get list of tables from a config table, get a count of rows to validate before processing, fetch a config parameter stored in SQL.
🗄 SQL
# Lookup output usage in dynamic content
# Get watermark from control table
@activity('LookupWatermark').output.firstRow.last_load_date

# Loop through table list from config table
@activity('LookupTables').output.value  # returns array of rows
# Each item in ForEach:
@item().table_name
@item().watermark_col

Get Metadata Activity: Reads metadata about a file or folder — not the actual data content. Used to inspect properties like file existence, size, last modified date, or list of child items in a folder. Think of it as ls -la for your ADLS container.

  • Returns: field values like exists, size, lastModified, childItems, itemType, contentMD5
  • Common uses: Check if a file arrived before triggering the pipeline, get count of files in a landing folder, verify a file isn't empty (size > 0), dynamically list all files in a partition folder.
🗄 SQL
# Get Metadata output usage
# Check file exists before copying
@activity('CheckFile').output.exists

# File size > 0 validation
@greater(activity('GetFileInfo').output.size, 0)

# List all files in a folder
@activity('ListLandingFiles').output.childItems
# Returns: [{"name":"orders_jan.csv","type":"File"}, ...]
Quick comparison
Lookup Activity
• Reads actual data rows
• Works on SQL, files, APIs
• Returns row values
• Use for: watermarks, config, table lists
• Max 5000 rows returned
Get Metadata Activity
• Reads file/folder properties
• Works on ADLS, Blob, SFTP
• Returns metadata (size, exists...)
• Use for: file arrival checks, folder listing
• No data content returned
💡
Common pattern combining both: Get Metadata checks if today's file arrived and is non-empty → If Condition gates on exists = true AND size > 0 → Lookup gets the watermark → Copy Activity runs the extraction. This prevents pipelines from running on missing or empty source files.
X
How do you implement parameterized pipelines and datasets in ADF?
▼

"Parameterization is what turns a hardcoded, single-purpose pipeline into a reusable generic one. The goal is to have one pipeline that can handle 50 tables, not 50 pipelines for 50 tables."

Pipeline Parameters: Defined on the pipeline itself, passed in at trigger time or by a parent pipeline. Read-only inside the pipeline — they're inputs, not scratchpad values.

🗄 SQL
# Pipeline parameter definition (in ADF JSON)
{
  "parameters": {
    "tableName":      {"type": "string"},
    "watermarkCol":   {"type": "string"},
    "targetPath":     {"type": "string"},
    "loadMode":       {"type": "string", "defaultValue": "append"}
  }
}

# Reference inside the pipeline with dynamic content
@pipeline().parameters.tableName
@pipeline().parameters.watermarkCol
@pipeline().parameters.targetPath

# Used in a Copy Activity source query:
SELECT *
FROM @{pipeline().parameters.tableName}
WHERE @{pipeline().parameters.watermarkCol}
  > '@{activity('LookupWatermark').output.firstRow.last_load_date}'

Parameterized Datasets: Instead of creating one dataset per table, create one dataset with parameters for table name, schema, file path etc. The pipeline passes the parameter value to the dataset at runtime.

🗄 SQL
# Parameterized dataset definition
{
  "parameters": {
    "tableName": {"type": "string"},
    "schemaName": {"type": "string", "defaultValue": "dbo"}
  },
  "typeProperties": {
    "schema": "@dataset().schemaName",
    "table":  "@dataset().tableName"
  }
}

# In Copy Activity — pass parameters to the dataset
# "dataset": {
#   "referenceName": "ds_sql_parameterized",
#   "type": "DatasetReference",
#   "parameters": {
#     "tableName":  {"value": "@pipeline().parameters.tableName"},
#     "schemaName": {"value": "dbo"}
#   }
# }

The full metadata-driven pattern I use on Citi project:

  • Config table in Azure SQL: stores table_name, watermark_col, target_path, load_mode, is_active per row.
  • Master pipeline: Lookup activity reads all active rows → ForEach activity loops in parallel → calls generic child pipeline with row values as parameters.
  • Generic child pipeline: accepts parameters, uses parameterized linked service and dataset, Copy Activity query built entirely from dynamic content expressions.
  • Result: adding a new table = one row in the config table. Zero pipeline changes. Zero new datasets.
🗄 SQL
# ForEach item reference — used inside the child pipeline call
{
  "tableName":    "@item().table_name",
  "watermarkCol": "@item().watermark_col",
  "targetPath":   "@item().target_path",
  "loadMode":     "@item().load_mode"
}

# Variables vs Parameters — quick distinction:
# Parameters: set from OUTSIDE at run time — read-only inside pipeline
# Variables:  created INSIDE the pipeline — can be updated during execution
#             via Set Variable activity — useful for accumulating row counts,
#             tracking loop state, building dynamic strings step by step
💡
"The combination of Lookup → ForEach → parameterized child pipeline is the single most powerful pattern in ADF. Once you build it, you never write a new pipeline for a new source table again — it's just a config row. I built this on the Citi project and it reduced new table onboarding from 2 days to under 2 hours."
5
What is your approach to designing a fault-tolerant, scalable ETL/ELT pipeline in Azure? What tools and tradeoffs?
Architecture
▼

Core design principles:

  • Idempotency: Every pipeline run must be safe to re-run. Use watermarks, MERGE, or partition overwrite — never append-only without dedup.
  • Modularity: Break pipelines into discrete stages (ingest, validate, transform, load). Each stage independently testable and restartable. Failure in Gold layer shouldn't require re-ingesting Bronze.
  • Retry + dead-letter: Configure retry policies (3 attempts, exponential backoff) on every activity. Route bad records to a dead-letter path in ADLS, not pipeline failure.
  • Checkpointing: For streaming — mandatory. For batch — use ADF pipeline activity dependencies to resume from last successful stage, not restart from scratch.
  • Observability: Every pipeline writes audit logs (start/end time, row counts, status, error). Azure Monitor alerts on failure. Log Analytics for pattern analysis.

Tool choices and tradeoffs:

  • ADF (orchestration): Managed, UI-driven, easy CI/CD. Tradeoff: limited complex logic — move that to Databricks notebooks.
  • Databricks (transformation): Powerful, scalable Spark. Tradeoff: more expensive than ADF Data Flows; requires Spark expertise.
  • Delta Lake (storage format): ACID, time travel, schema enforcement. Tradeoff: overhead vs raw Parquet for very simple pipelines.
  • Synapse (serving): Excellent for BI workloads and multi-user concurrency. Tradeoff: DWU cost is always-on; Serverless SQL is cheaper for ad-hoc but slower for repeated queries.
6
Under what networking conditions must you use a Self-Hosted Integration Runtime (SHIR) instead of Azure IR?
Networking
▼

The Integration Runtime (IR) is the compute engine that moves data in ADF. Azure IR is Microsoft-managed, lives in the cloud, and works for cloud-to-cloud transfers. It requires sources reachable over the public internet.

Use Self-Hosted IR (SHIR) — a Windows agent installed on a machine in your own network — when:

  • Data source is on-premises (SQL Server, Oracle, SAP) behind a corporate firewall.
  • Source is in a private VNet with no public endpoint and no peering to ADF's managed VNet.
  • You need to copy data between two private networks.
  • Source requires a local driver (ODBC, Oracle client) installed on the IR machine.
✅
The SHIR machine initiates outbound connections to ADF's cloud service over port 443 — no inbound firewall rules required.
7
How do you implement a Watermark-based incremental load in ADF to avoid re-processing old data?
▼

"A watermark is a stored timestamp that tracks 'how far we've successfully processed.' Every run only loads data newer than the watermark, and after a successful run the watermark is updated. This is my default approach for any source table that has a reliable updated_at or modified_date column."

Step 1 — Create the watermark control table:

🐍 SQL
CREATE TABLE dbo.watermark_control (
    table_name      VARCHAR(100) PRIMARY KEY,
    last_load_date  DATETIME,
    last_run_status VARCHAR(20));

INSERT INTO dbo.watermark_control VALUES ('orders', '1900-01-01', 'success');

Step 2 — ADF pipeline design:

  • Activity 1 — Lookup (Get Watermark): Query the control table to get last_load_date for the current table.
  • Activity 2 — Copy Activity (Incremental Extract): Source query uses the watermark as a filter. Sink writes to ADLS as Parquet/Delta.
  • Activity 3 — Stored Procedure (Update Watermark): On successful copy, update last_load_date to the current run timestamp. This only runs if Activity 2 succeeds — use the success dependency arrow in ADF.
🐍 SQL
-- Lookup query (Activity 1)
SELECT last_load_date
FROM dbo.watermark_control
WHERE table_name = '@{pipeline().parameters.tableName}'

-- Copy Activity source query (Activity 2)
-- Uses dynamic content expressions
SELECT *
FROM orders
WHERE updated_at > '@{activity('LookupWatermark').output.firstRow.last_load_date}'
AND   updated_at <= '@{utcNow()}'

-- Stored Procedure (Activity 3 — runs only on success)
UPDATE dbo.watermark_control
SET last_load_date  = '@{utcNow()}',
    last_run_status = 'success'
WHERE table_name = '@{pipeline().parameters.tableName}'

Key design decisions:

  • Use a closed-open window: Filter > last_load_date AND <= current_run_time. This avoids the edge case of records inserted exactly at the boundary being missed or double-counted.
  • Store the current run time before extraction starts: Not after — if new records arrive during the extraction window, they'll be captured in the next run because the watermark was set to the extraction start time, not end time.
  • Only update watermark on success: If the copy fails partway, the watermark stays at the last successful value. The next run will re-extract from the last successful point — some re-processing, but no data loss.
  • Source must have a reliable timestamp: Watermark-based CDC only works if the source column is always populated and accurately reflects when the row was last modified. If the source has rows with NULL updated_at, they'll never be captured incrementally — handle these separately with a one-time full load.

A watermark is a "last processed" marker — usually a timestamp or integer ID. Each run fetches only records newer than this marker.

  1. Create a watermark table in Azure SQL: CREATE TABLE watermark (table_name VARCHAR, last_value DATETIME)
  2. Lookup Activity — read the current watermark value for the target table.
  3. Lookup Activity — read the current MAX value in source: SELECT MAX(updated_at) FROM orders
  4. Copy Activity — source query uses both values: WHERE updated_at > old_wm AND updated_at <= new_max
  5. Stored Procedure Activity — after successful copy, update the watermark to the new max value.
💡
"This is the most robust incremental pattern for ADF. The watermark table is your pipeline's memory — it's what allows a failed run to safely restart from exactly the right point without human intervention."
8
How do you implement CDC (Change Data Capture) in ADF?
▼

"CDC is about capturing only the rows that changed — inserts, updates, and deletes — since the last extraction, instead of pulling the full table every time. In ADF, I implement CDC in two ways depending on what the source system supports."

Approach 1 — Watermark-based CDC (most common):

The source table has an updated_at or modified_date column. I store the last successfully loaded timestamp in a control table and use it as a filter on the next run — pulling only records newer than that watermark.

🐍 SQL
-- Control table
CREATE TABLE watermark (
    table_name    VARCHAR(100) PRIMARY KEY,
    last_load_date DATETIME
);

-- Incremental query in ADF Copy Activity source
SELECT * FROM orders
WHERE updated_at > '@{activity('GetWatermark').output.firstRow.last_load_date}'
AND   updated_at <= '@{utcNow()}'

-- After successful load — update watermark
UPDATE watermark
SET last_load_date = '@{utcNow()}'
WHERE table_name = 'orders'

ADF pipeline flow: Lookup (get watermark) → Copy Activity (incremental extract using watermark filter) → Stored Procedure (update watermark on success).

Approach 2 — Native CDC using SQL Server Change Tracking / CT:

SQL Server has built-in CDC and Change Tracking features. ADF's Copy Activity natively supports these as a source — you enable CDC on the source table and ADF automatically queries the change tables to get inserts, updates, and deletes without you writing any SQL filter logic.

  • Change Tracking (CT): Lightweight — tracks which rows changed and the type of change (INSERT/UPDATE/DELETE), but not the old values. Enough for most ETL use cases.
  • CDC (full): Tracks the actual before and after values for every change. More overhead on the source, but you can see what a column changed from and to — useful for audit trails.

Approach 3 — Soft deletes:

Source system marks deleted rows with an is_deleted = 1 flag and an updated_at timestamp instead of physically deleting them. The watermark-based extraction picks these up — and in the Silver layer, I use MERGE to apply the delete flag to the target table.

💡
"Watermark-based CDC is my default choice — it works on any source with a reliable timestamp column and requires no special source system setup. Native SQL Server CDC/CT is better when you also need to capture deletes, since a watermark can't detect a physically deleted row."
9
Explain how parameterization and dynamic content are handled in ADF pipelines.
▼

"Parameterization is what turns a hardcoded pipeline into a reusable one. Instead of building one pipeline per table, I build one pipeline with parameters — and pass different values at runtime."

Parameters vs Variables:

  • Parameters — set from outside at trigger time or by a parent pipeline. Read-only inside the pipeline. Think of them as inputs.
  • Variables — defined inside the pipeline, can be updated during execution using Set Variable activity. Think of them as a scratchpad.

Dynamic content (expressions): ADF uses an expression language with @ syntax inside any field that has a dynamic content option.

🐍 Python
# Common ADF expressions

# Reference a pipeline parameter
@pipeline().parameters.tableName

# Reference a variable
@variables('lastWatermark')

# Current UTC time
@utcNow()
@formatDateTime(utcNow(), 'yyyy-MM-dd')

# Concat strings dynamically
@concat('/mnt/bronze/', pipeline().parameters.tableName, '/', formatDateTime(utcNow(), 'yyyy/MM/dd'))

# Conditional expression
@if(equals(pipeline().parameters.mode, 'full'), 'overwrite', 'append')

# Reference Lookup activity output
@activity('LookupWatermark').output.firstRow.last_load_date

# ForEach item reference
@item().table_name
@item().watermark_col

Metadata-driven pattern I use on Citi project:

  • A SQL config table stores: table name, watermark column, target path, load mode, active flag.
  • Lookup Activity reads all active rows from the config table.
  • ForEach Activity loops through rows with parallel execution.
  • Inner Copy Activity uses @item().table_name, @item().watermark_col etc. — fully dynamic, zero hardcoding.
  • Adding a new table = inserting one row into the config table. No pipeline changes.

Parameterization levels:

  • Pipeline parameters: Defined at pipeline level. Passed when triggering via schedule trigger, event trigger, or Execute Pipeline activity.
  • Dataset parameters: Make dataset properties dynamic — table name, file path, container name. Reference with @dataset().paramName.
  • Linked Service parameters: Parameterize connection strings so one linked service handles multiple environments (dev/prod).
  • Global parameters: Set once at ADF level, available across all pipelines — useful for environment-level config like storage account names.
  • ForEach + parameterized pipeline: Build a config-driven framework — store table names in a control config, loop with ForEach, call a generic pipeline per table with the table name as a parameter.
💡
"Dynamic content is ADF's equivalent of f-strings in Python. Once you start using it with a config table, you realise you never need to build a new pipeline for a new table again — just a new config row."
10
Describe how error handling and retry mechanisms work in ADF.
Reliability
▼
  • Activity retry: Each activity has configurable retry count (default 0) and retry interval. ADF re-runs the failed activity N times before marking it failed. Use for transient failures like network blips and throttling.
  • On Failure path: Each activity has Success, Failure, and Completion output paths. Connect activities on the Failure path to trigger cleanup, send alerts (Web Activity to Teams webhook), or log errors to a SQL table.
  • Timeout: Set timeouts on long-running activities to prevent hanging pipelines from consuming resources indefinitely.
  • Fault tolerance in Copy Activity: Skip incompatible rows and log them to a fault file in ADLS instead of failing the entire copy. Configure under Copy Activity → Settings → Fault tolerance.
11
How would you securely migrate on-premises SQL Server data into ADLS Gen2?
SecurityMigration
▼
  1. Install SHIR on a machine in the on-prem network that can reach the SQL Server.
  2. Create a Linked Service in ADF pointing to on-prem SQL Server via the SHIR. Store the password in Azure Key Vault — never hardcode credentials.
  3. Use Managed Identity for ADF to authenticate to ADLS Gen2 — no storage account keys needed.
  4. Configure a Copy Activity with the on-prem SQL linked service as source and ADLS Gen2 as sink. Use Parquet format for storage efficiency.
  5. Ensure encryption in transit: SQL Server uses TLS, ADLS uses HTTPS, SHIR to ADF cloud uses TLS over port 443.
  6. Implement incremental load using watermarks or SQL Server Change Tracking to avoid moving the full dataset on every run.
  7. For high-security requirements, use ExpressRoute or VPN for private connectivity instead of public internet traversal.
12
How do you handle schema drift in ADF?
Schema Drift
▼

Schema drift happens when source systems add, remove, or rename columns without telling you. In ADF, you handle it at two levels:

In Mapping Data Flow (the right place to handle drift):

  • Enable "Allow schema drift" on the Source transformation — ADF will accept any columns from the source, even ones not in the schema definition.
  • Enable "Infer drifted column types" — ADF will auto-detect the data type of new columns.
  • Use "Auto mapping" on the Sink transformation — ADF automatically maps source columns to sink, including new drifted ones.
  • Use drifted column patterns (regex-based column selectors) in Derived Column transforms to apply logic to all columns matching a pattern, e.g., all columns ending in _id.

In Copy Activity: Copy Activity doesn't transform, so schema drift at copy time is handled by writing raw data as-is (landing in ADLS as JSON/Parquet with all columns). Downstream Databricks handles the schema reconciliation.
