Best practice for production: Don't silently accept all drift. Add a schema validation step that compares incoming schema to expected schema — alert on changes so the team can decide consciously whether to accept or reject them.

13
How do you manage Linked Services and Datasets in ADF at scale?
Best Practice
▼
  • Parameterize everything: A single linked service per technology (one for Azure SQL, one for ADLS), parameterized with server, database, and container name. Datasets also parameterized with table name, file path, schema. This means one linked service handles 100 tables, not 100 linked services.
  • Store credentials in Key Vault: Linked services should reference Key Vault secrets, never hardcode connection strings. Use Managed Identity for Azure resources (ADLS, Azure SQL) — no secrets at all.
  • Use Integration Runtime appropriately: One SHIR per on-prem network zone. Azure IR for all cloud-to-cloud. Don't create a new IR for every pipeline.
  • Naming convention: ls_[source]_[env] (e.g., ls_sqlserver_prod), ds_[source]_[format] (e.g., ds_adls_parquet). Consistent naming makes maintenance at scale manageable.
  • Git integration: Store all ADF artifacts (pipelines, linked services, datasets, triggers) in Azure DevOps or GitHub. Use ADF's native Git integration. Never edit directly in Production — always go through dev → test → prod via CI/CD.
  • ARM template deployment: ADF publishes a ARMTemplateForFactory.json that CI/CD pipelines use to deploy across environments, with environment-specific parameter files replacing dev values with prod values.
14
How do you deploy ADF across environments (Dev → Test → Prod)?
CI/CD
▼
  1. Connect ADF to Git (Azure DevOps or GitHub): All changes made in ADF Studio are committed to a feature branch. Dev ADF is the only one connected to Git.
  2. Collaboration branch: PRs merge into main/adf_publish. ADF auto-generates ARM templates on publish.
  3. CI Pipeline (Azure DevOps): On merge to main, validate ARM templates, run unit tests on pipeline logic if applicable.
  4. CD Pipeline: Deploy ARM template to Test ADF with test-specific parameter file (test storage accounts, test SQL DBs). After approval, deploy to Prod ADF with prod parameter file.
  5. Environment-specific parameters: The parameter file overrides linked service connection strings, Key Vault references, storage paths, and integration runtime names per environment.
  6. Triggers: Stop triggers before deployment, deploy, restart triggers. Use the PrePostDeploymentScript.ps1 that Microsoft provides for this.
  7. Never manually edit Prod: All changes flow through Git → Dev → Test → Prod pipeline. Any hotfix goes through a hotfix branch, not direct Prod edits.
15
How do you set up CI/CD for Azure Data Factory using Git integration and ARM templates?
▼

"CI/CD for ADF ensures that no one edits Production directly — every change goes through code review, automated validation, and a controlled deployment process. ADF makes this straightforward through native Git integration and ARM template publishing."

Step 1 — Git Integration (Dev ADF only):

  • Connect only the Dev ADF to a Git repository (Azure DevOps or GitHub) — Test and Prod are never connected to Git directly.
  • All ADF artifacts — pipelines, datasets, linked services, triggers — are stored as JSON files in the repo. Every change made in the ADF Studio UI is committed to a feature branch.
  • Developers work in feature branches, raise PRs to the main/collaboration branch, and changes are reviewed like any code change.

Step 2 — Publishing (generates ARM templates):

  • When code is merged to the collaboration branch and the publish button is clicked in Dev ADF, ADF auto-generates ARM templates in the adf_publish branch — ARMTemplateForFactory.json and ARMTemplateParametersForFactory.json.
  • These ARM templates are the deployable artifact — they contain the complete definition of all ADF objects.

Step 3 — CI Pipeline (Azure DevOps):

  • Triggered on merge to main — validates the ARM templates are well-formed, runs any unit tests on transformation logic.
  • Publishes the ARM template as a build artifact ready for deployment.

Step 4 — CD Pipeline (Azure DevOps):

🐍 JSON
# Azure DevOps CD pipeline — simplified
stages:
  - stage: DeployTest
    jobs:
      - job: StopTriggers
        steps:
          - task: AzurePowerShell@5
            inputs:
              ScriptPath: PrePostDeploymentScript.ps1  # stop triggers before deploy
              ScriptArguments: -armTemplate $(armTemplate) -ResourceGroupName $(rg_test)

      - job: DeployARM
        steps:
          - task: AzureResourceManagerTemplateDeployment@3
            inputs:
              deploymentScope: Resource Group
              resourceGroupName: $(rg_test)
              templateLocation: Linked artifact
              csmFile: ARMTemplateForFactory.json
              csmParametersFile: ARMTemplateParameters_test.json  # test-specific values

      - job: StartTriggers
        steps:
          - task: AzurePowerShell@5  # restart triggers after deploy

  - stage: DeployProd
    dependsOn: DeployTest
    condition: succeeded()
    jobs:
      # Same steps, uses ARMTemplateParameters_prod.json

Environment-specific parameter files:

🐍 JSON
{
  "parameters": {
    "AzureSqlServer_connectionString": {
      "value": "Server=prod-sql.database.windows.net;..."
    },
    "AzureDataLakeStorage_accountKey": {
      "value": "@Microsoft.KeyVault(SecretUri=https://prod-kv.../secrets/adls-key)"
    },
    "AzureIntegrationRuntime_name": {
      "value": "SHIR-PROD"
    }
  }
}

The parameter file replaces dev values (dev SQL server, dev ADLS, dev IR) with prod values — same ARM template, different parameter file per environment.

  • Stop triggers before deploy, restart after — Microsoft provides a PrePostDeploymentScript.ps1 exactly for this. Never skip this step — deploying while a trigger is active can corrupt the deployment.
  • Never edit Prod directly — even for urgent hotfixes, go through a hotfix branch → Dev ADF → publish → CD pipeline. The extra 10 minutes is worth the audit trail and safety.
💡
"The core principle: ADF artifacts are code, not config. They belong in Git, they get reviewed in PRs, and they get deployed through a pipeline — not by clicking publish directly in Production ADF Studio."
📊
Azure Synapse Analytics
Distribution, HNS, Serverless vs Dedicated, workload management, security
1
Why Synapse? What problems does it solve?
Architecture
▼

Earlier organizations used SQL Data Warehouse, Azure Data Factory, HDInsight, Spark Clusters as separate services.

Synapse unified them into a Single Platform, reducing Data movement, Infrastructure management & Development Complexity.

Performance Optimization in Synapse:

  • Use Hash distribution
  • Reduce data movement
  • Use Partitioning
  • Use Parquet files
  • Avoid Select *
  • Use Replicated tables for dimensions
  • Use CTAS

Security in Synapse:

  • Azure RBAC → Managed Identity
  • Azure Key Vault → Private End Points
  • Row-level Security → Column level Security
2
What is Azure Synapse Analytics?
Overview
▼

It is a unified analytics platform that combines data warehousing, big data analytics & SQL querying over Data Lake.

It supports: SQL Analytics, Lakehouse querying, data warehousing, Power BI serving layer, ELT Pipeline.

Think of it as SQL engine for analytics at scale.

Components inside Synapse Workspace:

  • SQL Pools → Query engine
  • Spark Pools → Big data Processing
  • Pipelines → Orchestration
  • Studio UI → development interface
  • Integration → Power BI Connection
3
types of SQL Pools & Open Rowset & External Tables
SQL Pools
▼

1. Dedicated SQL Pool — it uses MPP architecture (massively parallel processing).

  • Here users pre-allocate compute resources using Data Warehousing Units (DWUs)
  • Requires manual scaling (up/down) and can be paused to stop billing for compute while retaining data
  • Best for star schema model, fact tables, large aggregations

2. Serverless SQL Pool (Lakehouse query engine)

  • It is an on-demand pool that automatically scales compute resources without user intervention, allowing queries directly against data lake files in ADLS
  • It uses Distributed Query Processing (DQP) engine that breaks queries into tasks executed on backend nodes, scaling automatically based on workload
  • Best for querying Parquet in ADLS, exploratory analytics, lakehouse queries & ad-hoc SQL analysis

OPENROWSET — it's a Temporary SQL method to query files directly from Data Lake without creating metadata objects. Used for ad-hoc queries, schema inspection, quick file exploration, one-time analysis.

External Table — it's a re-usable table definition referencing data lake files inside Serverless SQL Pool. Used for repeated queries, shared datasets, reporting layers, BI integration, prod analytics.

4
Distribution Strategies
Distribution
▼

It is a technique used to spread table rows across compute nodes for parallel processing. There are 3 types in Dedicated SQL Pool:

  • Hash Distribution: Rows with same column go to same node.
    Ex: CREATE TABLE fact_sales WITH (Distribution = Hash(Cust_id))
    Used for — large fact tables, star schema fact tables, frequent joins on specific column.
  • Round Robin: Rows distributed randomly across nodes.
    Ex: (Distribution = ROUND_ROBIN)
    Used when — Temp staging tables, initial ingestion tables, no clear join column exists. Fast to load, slower for joins.
  • Replicate Distribution: Entire table copied to every compute node.
    Ex: (Distribution = REPLICATE)
    Used when — table is small, frequently joined, dimension lookup table (5000 dims & 5b facts). Speeds up joins dramatically.
5
Synapse Storage Table Types
Storage
▼

1. Heap Table: It is a table without any index, where data is stored unsorted & written directly as inserted.
Ex: CREATE TABLE Staging_Sales (Sale_id INT, amount FLOAT) WITH (HEAP)

  • Rows are inserted directly into storage without index maintenance & without ordering — results in very fast data ingestion
  • Recommended for: Staging tables, temp datasets, intermediate ELT layers, frequent batch loads, ADF ingestion targets
  • Advantages: Fastest insert speed, minimal storage overhead, simple structure, ideal for temporary usage

2. Clustered Columnstore Index (CCI): It stores table data col-by-col instead of row-by-row, optimized for large scale analytics.
Ex: CREATE TABLE FACT_Sales (customer_id INT, Sale_date Date, revenue FLOAT) WITH (CLUSTERED COLUMNSTORE INDEX)

  • Recommended for: Fact tables, large datasets, agg workloads, star schema WH tables
  • Advantages: high compression ratio, faster aggregations, reduced storage use, parallel scan performance, MPP optimization

3. Clustered Index Table: It stores rows sorted based on a key column, improving lookup performance.
Ex: CREATE TABLE dim_customers (customer_id INT, Cust_name VARCHAR(100)) WITH (CLUSTERED INDEX(customer_id))

  • Sorted order improves lookup speed, join speed & filter performance
  • Recommended for: Dim tables, lookup tables, small-medium datasets, reference datasets
  • Advantages: Fast point lookups, efficient joins, sorted storage structure, better filtering performance
6
What is PolyBase? How does it compare to COPY INTO?
Data Loading
▼

PolyBase: It is a technology that enables Azure Synapse & SQL Server to efficiently query & load data from external server to efficiently query & load data from external storage systems using SQL, making large-scale data ingestion faster & more scalable.

  • It requires creating External Data Source, fileformat & External Table as separate objects before loading. It is an older approach.

COPY INTO: Is a modern & preferred approach. A single T-SQL statement handles everything. Supports more file formats, more authentication methods & handles bad rows.

🗄 SQL
COPY INTO dbo.orders FROM 'abfss://mystacc...../orders/*.parquet'
WITH (FILE_TYPE = 'PARQUET', CREDENTIAL = ('Managed Identity'));
7
When would you choose Hash distribution over Round-Robin or Replicated tables in a Synapse Dedicated Pool?
Distribution
▼

Synapse Dedicated Pool distributes data across 60 distributions (physical storage units). How data lands across those distributions determines query performance.

  • Hash distribution: Rows with the same hash column value always land on the same distribution. Use on large fact tables (millions+ rows) frequently joined on the same column (e.g., customer_id). When two hash-distributed tables are joined on the same column, no data movement is required — a massive performance gain.
  • Round-Robin: Rows distributed evenly across all 60 distributions sequentially. Very fast writes. Use for staging tables, temp tables, or tables without a clear join key. Bad for query performance — joins always require data movement.
  • Replicated: A full copy of the table on every compute node. Use only for small dimension tables (<2GB). Eliminates all shuffle on joins with fact tables. Avoid for large tables — 60x storage cost and slow writes on updates.
💡
Rule of thumb: large table + frequent joins → Hash on join key. Small dimension table → Replicate. Staging area → Round-Robin.
8
What is the Hierarchical Namespace (HNS) in ADLS Gen2 and how does it optimize big data operations?
Fundamentals
▼

Regular Azure Blob Storage is a flat namespace — "folders" are just prefixes in blob names. Operations like renaming or deleting a "folder" require enumerating and updating every blob with that prefix — O(n) operations at scale.

ADLS Gen2 with HNS enabled creates a true hierarchical file system like HDFS. Directories are first-class objects with their own metadata. This enables:

  • Atomic rename: Renaming a directory is a single metadata operation — O(1). Critical for Spark commit operations, which write to a temp location then rename to final.
  • Atomic delete: Delete an entire directory in one operation rather than looping through every file individually.
  • POSIX-compliant ACLs: Set read, write, and execute permissions at the directory level, not just file level.
  • Performance at scale: Dramatically faster directory-level operations when you have millions of files.
⚠️
Always enable HNS when creating storage accounts for data lake workloads. It cannot be enabled after account creation.
9
Explain Synapse Serverless SQL Pools versus Dedicated SQL Pools — when do you use each?
Architecture
▼

Serverless SQL Pool: No provisioned compute. Query files directly in ADLS (Parquet, CSV, JSON, Delta) using T-SQL with OPENROWSET or external tables. Pay per TB scanned. Great for ad-hoc exploration, data discovery, and building views for Power BI on top of the data lake. Limitation: no indexes or materialized aggregates — every query scans files.

Dedicated SQL Pool: You provision DWUs — dedicated compute and columnar storage (CCI). Supports indexes, statistics, materialized views, and workload management. Pay per DWU-hour. Best for repeated, complex BI queries with multi-user concurrency and SLA requirements.

📌
Common pattern: Serverless SQL for the Silver lake layer (ad-hoc, flexible exploration) | Dedicated Pool for the Gold layer (performance-critical BI dashboards).
10
What is workload management in Synapse and why is it important?
Performance
▼

Without workload management, a single heavy query can starve all other users of DWU resources. Synapse workload management has three components:

  • Workload groups: Define resource pools with min/max DWU percentages — e.g., ETL jobs get 50%, dashboards get 30%, ad-hoc gets 20%.
  • Workload classifiers: Route specific users, labels, or query patterns to a workload group — e.g., etl_user → ETL group; label 'dashboard' → Dashboard group.
  • Workload importance: HIGH/MEDIUM/LOW priority within the queue. High-importance queries run first when resources are contested.
11
How do you secure data in Azure Synapse Analytics?
Security
▼
  • Network: Deploy Synapse in a managed VNet. Use Private Endpoints to block public internet exposure. Apply Synapse Firewall rules to whitelist trusted IP ranges.
  • Authentication: Azure AD integration with AAD users and groups — avoid SQL logins where possible. Use Service Principals for automated pipelines.
  • Authorization: RBAC at the workspace level (Owner, Contributor, Reader). SQL-level roles. Column-Level Security and Row-Level Security for fine-grained access.
  • Dynamic Data Masking (DDM): Mask sensitive columns (SSN, credit card) for unauthorized users at query time. Data is not changed on disk — masking is applied at query time only.
  • Encryption: Data at rest encrypted with Azure-managed keys or Customer-Managed Keys (CMK) in Key Vault. All data in transit uses TLS.
  • Auditing: Enable Synapse auditing to write audit logs to a Storage Account or Log Analytics for compliance and forensic investigation.
🔥
Azure Databricks
OOM troubleshooting, monitoring, schema management, ETL frameworks
1
Databricks Architecture
ArchitectureInternals
▼

Databricks is a cloud-based unified analytics platform that combines Data Engineering, Data Science, ML & Business Intelligence Into a single environment. It is widely used for large-scale data processing and advanced analytics.

Databricks follows a two-layer architecture — the control plane managed by Databricks, while the Compute/Data Plane is hosted in the cloud environment (AWS, Azure, or Google Cloud)

Managed Spark + Delta Lake + Lakehouse Platform + Governance + Enterprise Tooling

Databricks Architecture Two-Plane Model
Control Plane — Databricks-managed
• Workspace UI
• Job Scheduler & Orchestrator
• Notebook & IDE
• Unity Catalog (governance)
• IAM & Access Control
Compute/Data Plane — cloud account
• Spark Clusters (VMs)
• ADLS Gen2 / S3 / GCS
• Delta Lake tables
• Virtual Network (VNet)
• Key Vault / Secrets
Control Plane: The Control Plane is responsible for managing user access, workspaces, job scheduling, and metadata storage. It operates as Software as a Service (SaaS) and is fully managed by Databricks.
Workspace UI: Provides an interface to manage notebooks, clusters, jobs, and assets. Organizes projects and permissions within Databricks.
Job Scheduler & Orchestrator: Responsible for scheduling, triggering and monitoring workflows and jobs.
Notebook & IDE: Development environment used to write SQL, Python, PySpark, Scala and R code.
Unity Catalog: Centralized governance layer used for managing data access, permissions and metadata.
IAM & Access Control: Manages user identities and permissions. Handles authentication, authorization and workspace-level security.
Data Plane: The Compute Plane is where actual data processing and storage happens. Unlike the Control Plane (managed by Databricks), the Compute Plane is hosted inside the customer’s cloud environment.
Spark Clusters (VMs): Compute resources where Spark jobs and notebooks execute.
ADLS Gen2 / S3 / GCS: Cloud storage layer used for storing raw, processed and curated data.
Delta Lake Tables: Transactional storage layer that provides ACID properties, versioning and optimized analytics.
Virtual Network (VNet): Provides network isolation and secure communication between resources.
Key Vault / Secrets: Stores credentials, connection strings, keys and sensitive configuration securely.
  • Cluster types: All-purpose clusters (interactive notebooks, development — always running, expensive) vs Job clusters (auto-created for a specific job run, auto-terminated after — cheaper, recommended for production).
  • Cluster modes: Standard (single-user), High Concurrency (multi-user, SQL focused), Single Node (driver only, no workers — for small local jobs).
  • Databricks Runtime (DBR): A packaged version of Spark + optimised libraries (Delta, MLflow, etc.). Each cluster picks a DBR version. Always use the latest LTS version in production for stability.
  • Repos: Native Git integration — connect notebooks directly to Azure DevOps or GitHub. Enables version control without leaving the workspace.
  • Workflows (Jobs): Multi-task job orchestration built into Databricks — chain notebooks, Python scripts, JAR files with dependencies. Alternative to ADF for Databricks-only pipelines.
Unity Catalog — Databricks' data governance layer

Unity Catalog is a centralised metadata and governance layer that sits across all Databricks workspaces in an account. Before Unity Catalog, every workspace had its own Hive metastore — isolated, hard to share, no cross-workspace governance. Unity Catalog solves that.

Three-level namespace
Catalog → Schema (Database) → Table / View / Volume
Ex: citi_catalog.silver.customer_transactions
  • Centralised access control: Grant/revoke permissions on catalogs, schemas, tables, and even individual columns — all from one place, applied across every workspace.
  • Column-level security & row filters: Mask PII columns for unauthorised users. Apply row-level filters so different teams only see their own data — all enforced at query time, no copies needed.
  • Data lineage: Unity Catalog automatically tracks which notebook/job read or wrote each table, and which columns were used. Useful for impact analysis — "if I change this column, what breaks?"
  • Audit logs: Every query, every data access is logged. Who read what, when, from which cluster — exported to ADLS or Azure Monitor.
  • External locations & storage credentials: Register your ADLS paths as External Locations with a Storage Credential (service principal or managed identity). Tables referencing these paths inherit governance automatically — no manual access management per cluster.
💡
In an interview: "Unity Catalog replaced the per-workspace Hive metastore with a single governance layer — one place for permissions, lineage, auditing, and data discovery across all workspaces and all clouds. It's what makes Databricks enterprise-ready for compliance requirements like GDPR and HIPAA."
2
Explain the Small File Problem. How does Auto-Compaction in Databricks solve this?
PerformanceDelta Lake
▼

"The small file problem is one of the most common performance issues in data lakes and it sneaks up on you slowly."

What is the small file problem?

Every time a Spark job writes data, it creates one file per partition. If your pipeline runs every 15 minutes and writes 10 files each time, after one day you have 960 files. After a month, tens of thousands of files — each tiny, maybe a few KB or MB.

This causes two problems:

  • Read performance degrades: When a query scans the table, Spark creates one task per file. Thousands of tiny files = thousands of tasks with huge scheduling overhead and barely any actual data per task. The job spends more time managing tasks than processing data.
  • Driver memory pressure: The driver has to track metadata for every file. Tens of thousands of files → massive metadata load on the driver → slowdowns and sometimes OOM on the driver.

How Auto-Compaction solves it:

Auto-Compaction is a Databricks Delta Lake feature that automatically compacts small files into larger ones after every write operation — without you having to schedule anything manually.

  • After a write completes, Databricks checks if the resulting files are smaller than a target size (default 128MB).
  • If they are, it immediately runs a background compaction job on just the newly written files — merging them into fewer, larger files.
  • It only compacts the files from the recent write, not the entire table — so it's fast and doesn't block other operations.
-- Enable Auto-Compaction on a Delta table
ALTER TABLE orders
SET TBLPROPERTIES ('delta.autoOptimize.autoCompact' = 'true');

-- Optimize Write: enables optimized writes (larger files on write during ingestion)
SET spark.databricks.delta.optimizeWrite.enabled = true; 
-- Auto Compact: enables automatic compaction (merges small files into larger ones)
SET spark.databricks.delta.autoCompact.enabled = true

Auto-Compaction vs manual OPTIMIZE:

  • Auto-Compaction — runs automatically after writes, handles recent files only, no scheduling needed. Good for streaming or frequent batch pipelines. It automatically merges small files into larger ones in the background.
  • OPTIMIZE — manually triggered, compacts the entire table or specific partitions, produces larger target file sizes, also supports Z-ORDER. Use this for weekly/monthly maintenance on large tables.
💡
On the NYC Taxi project, our hourly pipeline was creating hundreds of tiny files daily. Enabling Auto-Compaction reduced our Gold layer file count from ~8000 files to under 200 within a week — and query times on those tables dropped noticeably.
3
A Databricks job is failing with Out-of-Memory (OOM) errors. How would you troubleshoot it?
Troubleshooting
▼
  1. Identify where OOM occurs: Driver OOM → you're collecting too much data to the driver (collect(), toPandas(), large broadcast). Executor OOM → individual tasks are too memory-heavy.
  2. Check Spark UI: Go to Stages → look for tasks with very large shuffle read/write or skewed task sizes.
  3. Fix data skew: Add salting for skewed joins, or enable AQE (spark.sql.adaptive.enabled=true).
  4. Increase shuffle partitions: Default is 200. For large data, increase to 400–1000 to reduce per-partition size.
  5. Upgrade cluster node type to increase executor memory, or increase spark.executor.memory in cluster config.
  6. Reduce data being processed: Push filters earlier. Use select() to drop unused columns before joins.
  7. Replace Python UDFs with native Spark functions — UDFs serialize data to a Python process, consuming extra memory.
  8. Avoid collect() on large DataFrames. Sample or aggregate first before pulling data to the driver.
4
How do you monitor and troubleshoot failed jobs in Azure Databricks?
Monitoring
▼
  • Databricks Jobs UI: Workflows → Jobs → click the failed run to see task-level status and error messages.
  • Cluster logs: Check driver stdout/stderr for Python exceptions. Check event log for cluster-level issues (node eviction, OOM kill).
  • Spark UI: Access via Cluster → Spark UI. Check failed stages, task duration distribution, shuffle stats, and GC time.
  • Log Analytics integration: Configure Databricks to ship logs to Azure Log Analytics. Query with KQL to identify patterns across multiple failed runs.
  • Alert on failure: In Databricks Jobs, set email or webhook alerts on failure. Integrate with Azure Monitor for SLA tracking.
5
What is the difference between schema inference and predefined schema in PySpark? Which is better in production?
Schema
▼

Schema inference means Spark reads a sample of the data and auto-detects column names and types. Convenient in development, but problematic in production:

  • Expensive: requires an extra scan of the data before processing begins.
  • Unreliable: inferSchema on CSV might read "123" as integer, but rows later with "123abc" fail or produce wrong types.
  • Non-deterministic: schema can silently change if source data changes, breaking downstream code without any obvious error.

Predefined schema (StructType) — you explicitly declare every column name and type before reading:

schema = StructType([
    StructField("order_id", IntegerType(), False),
    StructField("amount", DoubleType(), True),
    StructField("created_at", TimestampType(), True)
])
df = spark.read.schema(schema).json("path/to/data")
✅
Always use predefined schema in production. It's faster, predictable, and forces you to explicitly handle schema changes — which is exactly what you want in a controlled pipeline.
6
How would you build a reusable Python ETL framework in Databricks?
Architecture
▼
  • Config-driven: Store pipeline configs in JSON, YAML, or a control SQL table. Code reads config at runtime — add a new pipeline by adding a config row, not writing new code.
  • Separate concerns: Organize into readers/, transformers/, writers/, and utils/ (logging, audit, schema validation).
  • Base classes: Abstract base class for Reader, Transformer, Writer with standard read(), transform(df), write(df) interfaces.
  • Audit logging: Every pipeline run writes to an audit table — pipeline name, run ID, start/end time, rows read/written, status, error message.
  • Package as a wheel: Build as a Python package, publish to Databricks repos, install on clusters with %pip install.
  • Unit tests: Use pytest + chispa (PySpark DataFrame equality testing). Run in CI/CD pipeline (GitHub Actions / Azure DevOps).
7
How do you handle Schema Drift using ADF and Databricks?
Schema Management
▼

Schema drift = source adds/removes/renames columns unexpectedly, breaking your pipeline.

In ADF Data Flow: Enable "Allow schema drift" on the source transformation. ADF will pass through unknown columns. Use "Auto mapping" in the sink.

In Databricks:

  • Read with mergeSchema = True for Parquet — Spark merges schemas across files with different columns.
  • For Delta: use mergeSchema = true on write. Delta handles additive changes automatically; destructive changes (column removal) need explicit ALTER TABLE DROP COLUMN.
  • Add a validation step that compares the incoming schema to the expected schema and sends an alert before writing rather than silently dropping new columns.
📋
For Power BI downstream: use a view layer in Synapse or Databricks SQL that selects only the stable columns BI depends on — insulating reports from upstream schema changes.
8
How do you manage access control and governance in a shared Databricks workspace?
▼

"In a shared workspace — where multiple teams, environments, and use cases co-exist — governance isn't optional. Without it, you end up with data sprawl, accidental overwrites, and compliance failures. I manage it at three layers."

1. Identity & Authentication:

  • All users authenticate via Azure Active Directory (AAD) — no local Databricks passwords in production. This gives you single sign-on and centralized identity management.
  • Service principals (not personal accounts) are used for automated pipelines and ADF connections. A pipeline running as "salman@company.com" is a problem if that person leaves the company — service principals don't.

2. Unity Catalog — the main governance layer:

  • Three-level namespace: catalog.schema.table — e.g., citi_prod.silver.customer_transactions. Each level has its own permissions.
  • GRANT/REVOKE at any level: Grant SELECT on a specific table to an analyst group. Grant ALL PRIVILEGES on a schema to the data engineering team. Deny access to PII tables for everyone except the compliance team.
  • Column-level security: Mask or restrict specific columns (like SSN, credit card) per user group — the table is visible but sensitive columns return masked values.
  • Row-level security: Apply row filters so a regional team only sees their region's data, even when querying the same shared table.
  • Data lineage: Unity Catalog auto-tracks which notebook/job read or wrote each table — critical for impact analysis and compliance audits.

3. Cluster & compute access:

  • Cluster policies restrict what cluster configurations users can create — preventing analysts from spinning up 64-node clusters by accident.
  • Separate cluster pools per team — data engineering gets job clusters, analysts get SQL Warehouses, ML gets GPU clusters. Each pool has its own budget and access control.
  • Table ACLs on SQL Warehouses — analysts can only query what they're explicitly granted, not browse the entire metastore.
💡
"My rule for shared workspaces: least privilege by default. Start with no access and grant explicitly — don't start with broad access and try to revoke it later. The second approach always leaves gaps."
9
Your Databricks cost is too high — what strategies would you use to reduce it?
▼

"Databricks cost has two main components — DBU (Databricks Units, based on cluster type and size) and the underlying cloud VM cost. I attack both."

1. Cluster strategy — biggest impact:

  • Job clusters over all-purpose clusters: All-purpose clusters run 24/7 and bill every minute even when idle. Job clusters spin up for a specific run and auto-terminate when done. For production batch pipelines, this is non-negotiable.
  • Spot/preemptible instances for workers: Worker nodes on spot VMs cost 60–90% less than on-demand. Databricks handles spot preemption with automatic retries. I keep only the driver on on-demand.
  • Auto-termination: Set idle termination to 15–30 minutes on any all-purpose cluster used for development. An idle 10-node cluster left running overnight burns money for nothing.
  • Right-sizing: Profile the job in Spark UI first — check executor utilization, spill, and skew. Most jobs run on clusters twice as large as they need to be because nobody revisited the config after initial setup.

2. Cluster pools:

  • Cluster pools keep a warm set of VMs ready — so job clusters start in 30 seconds instead of 5 minutes, without paying for a running cluster in between runs. Ideal for pipelines that trigger frequently throughout the day.

3. Photon engine:

  • Photon is Databricks' vectorized query engine — it runs SQL and DataFrame operations 2–4x faster than standard Spark on the same hardware. Jobs finish faster, cluster uptime is shorter, total cost drops. Worth enabling on SQL-heavy workloads even though Photon DBUs are priced slightly higher per hour.

4. Storage optimization:

  • Small file problems inflate read times and inflate the cluster time needed. Regular OPTIMIZE + VACUUM on Delta tables reduces both scan time and storage cost.
  • ADLS lifecycle policies — move Bronze files older than 30 days to Cool tier, 90 days to Archive. Raw data you never query shouldn't sit on hot storage.

5. Monitor and attribute cost:

  • Tag every cluster with team, project, and environment tags. Use Databricks cost dashboards + Azure Cost Management to see which team, pipeline, or job is driving spend. You can't optimize what you can't measure.
  • Set budget alerts — email the team lead when a workspace exceeds a weekly threshold. Nobody reduces cost without visibility into what they're spending.
💡
"The single highest-impact change is almost always switching from all-purpose to job clusters with spot workers. I've seen Databricks bills drop 40–60% from that one change alone, before touching anything else."
10
How do you optimize a slow Databricks job? End-to-end approach.
PerformanceTuning
▼

Work through these layers in order — fix the cheapest issue first:

  1. Check Spark UI first: Go to Stages → find the bottleneck stage. Look at: task duration variance (skew?), shuffle read/write size (too much data?), spill to disk (OOM pressure?), GC time (memory tuning needed?).
  2. Fix data skew: If one task takes 10x longer than others, you have skew. Enable AQE (spark.sql.adaptive.enabled=true). For severe skew, use salting on the join key.
  3. Reduce shuffle: Increase shuffle partitions (spark.sql.shuffle.partitions) so each partition is smaller. Use broadcast join for small tables (<200MB). Push filters before joins to reduce data volume.
  4. Fix the small files problem: If reading thousands of tiny files, use OPTIMIZE (Delta) or repartition() before writing. Read files are also a performance issue — merge them first.
  5. Upgrade cluster or right-size: If spill is high → more memory per node. If task parallelism is low → more cores. Profile first, don't just throw bigger hardware at it.
  6. Use Delta Lake: If reading Parquet, switch to Delta — data skipping, Z-ordering, and statistics dramatically reduce scan size.
  7. Cache strategically: Cache DataFrames used in multiple actions. Unpersist when done.
  8. Check cluster config: Is autoscaling enabled? Is the cluster too small for the data volume on that day? Check job run history for cluster size vs data volume correlation.
11
How do you control Azure costs across your data platform?
Cost Optimization
▼
  • Databricks: Use spot instances for batch (90% cheaper). Use job clusters (auto-terminate) not all-purpose clusters (idle billing). Enable auto-termination (15–30 min idle). Use cluster pools for faster spin-up without idle cost. Right-size — profile job metrics and pick smallest fitting cluster.
  • Synapse Dedicated Pool: Pause when not in use (ADF pipeline or Azure Automation). Scale down DWU during off-peak. Use Serverless SQL Pool for ad-hoc exploration — pay per TB scanned, not per hour.
  • ADLS Gen2: Lifecycle policies — move data to Cool tier after 30 days, Archive after 90 days. Delete processed staging files daily. Compress data (Parquet + Snappy saves 70–80% vs CSV).
  • ADF: Minimize Data Flow usage (Spark cost). Prefer Copy Activity for simple ingestion. Use IR wisely — don't run SHIR on over-provisioned VMs.
  • Monitoring: Set up Azure Cost Management budgets and alerts. Tag all resources with project and environment tags. Review Azure Advisor recommendations monthly.
  • Reserved Instances: For always-on resources (ADF IR, certain Databricks clusters), commit to 1-year Reserved Instance — up to 40% savings.
12
How do you ensure data quality and monitoring in production pipelines?
Data Quality
▼

Data Quality Checks (at each layer):

  • Schema validation: Compare incoming schema to expected schema before writing. Alert on mismatch — don't silently drop columns.
  • Row count checks: Assert that output rows are within an expected range (e.g., ±10% of yesterday's count). Zero rows is almost always a bug.
  • Null checks: Assert that critical columns (primary keys, join keys, foreign keys) have zero nulls.
  • Uniqueness checks: Assert no duplicate primary keys in the target table after load.
  • Referential integrity: Assert that all foreign keys have matching records in dimension tables (no orphaned fact records).
  • Business rule validation: Amount >= 0, dates are in valid range, status is in allowed set of values.

Tools in Azure:

  • Great Expectations or Deequ (Databricks): Programmatic data quality rule engines that generate quality reports.
  • Azure Monitor + Log Analytics: Ship custom metrics (row counts, null counts, error counts) from pipelines. Set alert rules — trigger email/Teams/PagerDuty when a metric breaches threshold.
  • ADF Monitor: Pipeline run history, activity-level error messages, built-in alerts on pipeline failure.
  • Audit table: Write pipeline run metadata (pipeline name, run ID, start/end, rows in/out, status, error) to a central audit SQL table. Build a Power BI dashboard on top for visibility.
13
Describe a production issue you handled end-to-end.
Scenario
▼

"Our nightly ADF pipeline that loaded source tables from on-prem SQL Server into ADLS Gen2 started failing intermittently — specifically on some Mondays. Other days it ran fine, which made it harder to diagnose.

I checked ADF Monitor and found the Copy Activity was timing out on the SHIR connection. I SSH'd into the SHIR machine and found the disk was at 98% capacity — weeks of ADF diagnostic logs had accumulated with no rotation policy in place. I cleared the logs, freed the disk, and manually re-triggered the pipeline. It completed in 35 minutes.

For the fix: I added a Windows Scheduled Task on the SHIR VM to rotate logs weekly, configured an Azure Monitor alert to trigger at 80% disk usage (giving us reaction time before it became critical), and added a retry policy of 3 attempts with 5-minute intervals on the Copy Activity so transient issues don't cause immediate failure.

Since those changes, the alert has fired twice — both times the team resolved it proactively before it impacted any pipeline run. Zero recurrence of the failure itself."

💡
The prevention steps — monitoring alert + log rotation + retry policy — are what show engineering maturity. Fix + prevent, not just fix.
14
A job works in Dev but fails in Production — how would you debug it?
Debugging
▼

This is one of the most common real-world scenarios. Check these differences between Dev and Prod:

  1. Data volume: Dev usually has a sample (1000 rows). Prod has millions. OOM, shuffle spill, and skew issues only appear at scale. Check Spark UI for executor failures and spill.
  2. Cluster config: Is Prod cluster the same node type and size as Dev? Different Databricks Runtime version? Different Spark config settings?
  3. Credentials / permissions: Does the Prod service principal/Managed Identity have access to the Prod storage account, Key Vault, and SQL DB? Check IAM roles.
  4. Network / firewall: Does Prod have stricter firewall rules? Is the Prod SHIR reachable from the on-prem source? Is a private endpoint blocking a connection that was public in Dev?
  5. Data quality differences: Prod data has edge cases Dev data doesn't — nulls in required fields, unexpected character encodings, dates in non-standard formats that break parsing.
  6. Environment-specific config: Is the pipeline reading the right parameter file? Is it pointing to the Prod Key Vault secret (not Dev)? Check environment variable resolution.
  7. Dependencies: Does Prod have a library version mismatch? Is the Python wheel version on the Prod cluster the same as Dev?
💡
Replicate Prod in Dev: run the job on a Prod data sample (not Dev data) on a Dev cluster with Prod-sized cluster config. This narrows down whether it's a data issue or an environment issue.
⚡
PySpark
Skew, UDFs, partitioning, memory, AQE, streaming, nested JSON
1
How is Apache Spark different from MapReduce? List the key differences.
▼

"MapReduce was Hadoop's original processing engine — Spark was built to fix its performance limitations. The core difference is where intermediate data lives: MapReduce always writes to disk between steps, Spark keeps it in memory."

Spark vs MapReduce
⚡ Apache Spark
• In-memory processing
• Up to 100x faster
• Lazy evaluation + DAG
• Supports batch + streaming
• ML, SQL, Graph, Streaming
• DataFrame / SQL API
• Scala, Python, Java, R
• Disk used only for spill
• Cache data across steps
🐘 MapReduce
• Disk-based (HDFS) between steps
• Much slower — I/O bound
• Linear Map → Reduce flow
• Batch processing only
• Only Map and Reduce ops
• Low-level Java API
• Java only (originally)
• Every step writes to disk
• No caching across steps
  • Processing model: MapReduce has two fixed steps — Map (transform) and Reduce (aggregate), with a mandatory disk write in between. Spark builds a full DAG of transformations and optimizes the entire plan before executing — no mandatory disk writes between steps.
  • Speed: Spark is up to 100x faster for iterative algorithms (like machine learning) because it keeps intermediate results in memory. MapReduce reads and writes HDFS between every step — for a 10-step job, that's 10 disk reads and 10 disk writes.
  • Ease of use: MapReduce requires writing verbose Java code. Spark provides high-level DataFrame API and SQL, and supports Python, Scala, Java, and R.
  • Streaming: MapReduce is batch-only. Spark supports Structured Streaming natively — the same codebase handles batch and streaming.
  • When would you use MapReduce today? Almost never for new development. It's legacy technology — most Hadoop clusters have migrated to Spark. MapReduce still exists for specific Hadoop ecosystem tools (Hive on MR) or very constrained memory environments where you can't fit data in memory at all.
💡
"The simplest way I explain it: MapReduce is like writing to a whiteboard, photographing it, erasing it, then reading the photo before the next step — every single time. Spark just keeps the data on the whiteboard the whole time."
2
Spark Architecture — Driver, Executors, Cluster Manager, DAG, Lazy Evaluation
Architecture
▼

PySpark is the Python API for Apache Spark, used to process large-scale data across distributed systems.

Spark follows a master–worker design. A Spark application runs a driver program on one node and executor processes on many worker nodes. The driver hosts the main function and creates the SparkContext (or SparkSession ), which is the entry point to Spark. The driver’s SparkContext connects to a cluster manager (e.g. YARN, Kubernetes, or Spark’s standalone manager) to acquire resources. The cluster manager allocates executors on worker machines; each executor is a JVM process that will run tasks and cache data partitions.

Driver
Maintains SparkContext, builds the DAG, runs the DAG Scheduler and Task Scheduler. Your app's JVM process. Sends serialised tasks to executors.
Executor
JVM on a worker node. Runs tasks in a thread pool, stores cached partitions in Block Manager, sends heartbeat every 10s to the Driver.
DAG Scheduler
Converts RDD/DataFrame lineage into a DAG of stages. Splits on shuffle boundaries. Submits stage-ready TaskSets to the Task Scheduler.
Task = 1 Partition
One task processes exactly one partition on one executor thread. Parallelism = min(available cores, number of partitions).

Core components:

  • Driver: The JVM process that runs your Spark application's main() PySpark Code. It creates the SparkContext, builds the DAG (Directed Acyclic Graph) of transformations, divides the DAG into stages and tasks, and schedules tasks on executors. it coordinates work across the cluster. The driver has a finite memory — never collect() large data to it.
  • Executor: Worker JVM processes that run tasks. Each executor has a fixed number of CPU cores (tasks run in parallel = cores) and memory (execution + storage). Executors live for the lifetime of the application.
  • Cluster Manager: Allocates resources (executors) to the Spark application. It Manages worker nodes & it doesn't execute tasks. In Databricks: YARN (legacy) or Kubernetes. In standalone: Spark's built-in manager. The driver requests resources from the cluster manager.
  • DAG (Directed Acyclic Graph): Spark builds a logical plan of transformations lazily. When an action (count(), write(), collect()) is triggered, the Catalyst optimizer converts the logical plan into a physical plan, splits it into stages at shuffle boundaries, and each stage into tasks (one task per partition).
  • RDD → DataFrame → Dataset: RDDs are the low-level distributed data structure. DataFrames are RDDs with schema — the Catalyst optimizer can reason about them. Always use DataFrames/SQL in production, not raw RDDs.
  • Lazy evaluation: Transformations (filter(), join(), select()) don't execute immediately. They build the DAG. Only actions trigger actual computation. This allows Catalyst to optimize the full plan before executing anything.
    • Instead of executing the logic/transformations & generating the output immediately, spark stores the instructions in a DAG to execute them when an action is triggered. This allows Spark to optimize the execution plan across multiple transformations before running anything.

      By not executing the transformations immediately, we avoid bringing the entire dataframe into memory at once, which can lead to OOM errors. Instead, Spark processes data in partitions and only when necessary, allowing it to handle large datasets efficiently.
3
Memory Management in Spark
MemoryInternals
▼

Spark runs on JVM and every executor has a fixed memory container. Spark divides that memory into regions — each with a specific job. Think of it like a restaurant kitchen with dedicated stations.

Executor container — 4 GB total (example)
Reserved memory ~300 MB fixed
Spark's internal objects (SparkContext, metadata). You cannot touch this. Kitchen: the fridge and utilities — always reserved.
User memory ~40% of remaining
Your Python objects, custom data structures, UDF variables. Spark doesn't manage this — it's yours. Kitchen: your personal prep table.
Spark (unified) memory — 60% of remaining shared pool
Execution memory
Shuffle, sort, join, aggregation. Spills to disk when full. The cooking station.
Storage memory
Cached DataFrames. Evicted when execution needs space. The warming shelf.
These two share the same pool and borrow from each other dynamically — this is called Unified Memory Model (default since Spark 1.6).
Overhead memory 10% of executor memory (min 384 MB)
Off-heap: JVM threads, Python worker processes, network buffers, GC structures. Driver also has this. Kitchen: ventilation and plumbing — invisible but critical.

What happens during a join? (real example)

  • Spark reads two DataFrames and starts shuffling them by join key — this uses execution memory.
  • If you called df.cache() earlier, that cached data sits in storage memory.
  • If execution memory fills up, Spark spills to disk (slow but doesn't crash).
  • If storage memory is needed by execution, Spark evicts the cache automatically.
  • If even the overhead fills up → OOM error — the executor dies.
⚠️
Common traps: calling toPandas() or collect() sends everything to driver memory (not executor) — driver has no execution/storage split, just a JVM heap. Overflow it and the entire application crashes, not just one task.
4
Catalyst Optimizer & Tungsten Engine
Internals
▼

Catalyst Optimizer is Spark's intelligent query optimizer — it automatically rewrites our PySpark code into the fastest possible execution plan. Catalyst decides how to execute it efficiently. It works in 4 phases:

  1. Analysis (Unresolved → Analyzed Logical Plan): Spark first reads the code but doesn't yet know details about columns or tables — this is the unresolved logical plan. Example: df.select("salary") — Spark asks "does column salary exist?" Only structure is understood here. Then in the analyzed phase, Spark checks metadata — does the column exist, what data type is it, where is the table stored. This is where Spark catches "column not found" errors.
  2. Logical Optimization: This is the main intelligence of Catalyst. It applies rule-based optimizations — predicate pushdown (move filters as early as possible), projection pruning (drop unused columns early), constant folding (1+1 → 2), subquery elimination. For example, df.filter(df.age>18).select("name") gets internally rewritten as "select name AFTER filtering rows" instead of "select name from ALL rows THEN filter" — this reduces computation dramatically.
  3. Physical Planning: Spark generates several physical plans from the logical plan and picks the most efficient one based on cost — chooses join strategy (broadcast vs sort-merge vs hash), decides partition count, selects sort algorithms.
  4. Code Generation: Generates optimized JVM bytecode for each physical operator — eliminates virtual function calls, enables CPU-level optimizations.
⚡
Catalyst Optimizer is Spark SQL's query optimization engine that transforms logical query plans into efficient physical execution plans using rule-based and cost-based optimization techniques. We can view the execution plan anytime using df.explain(True).

Tungsten Engine is Spark's memory and CPU optimization layer, operating below Catalyst — Catalyst decides the best plan, Tungsten executes that plan extremely fast.

  • Off-heap memory management: Without Tungsten, Spark stores data as Row → Java Object → JVM memory. With Tungsten, it stores Row → Binary Format → off-heap memory, directly in system memory outside the JVM. This bypasses JVM garbage collection and is much faster to process.
  • CPU optimization: Tungsten re-arranges data internally so the CPU reads faster — this improves cache locality, enables vectorised processing, and improves instruction efficiency.
  • Whole-stage code generation: Normally Spark executes operations step by step — filter() → select() → groupBy() runs as Step 1 → Step 2 → Step 3 separately. With Tungsten, Spark combines everything into a single compiled machine code block — this is called Whole-Stage Code Generation. Instead of one function call per operator, it's one optimized loop for the entire pipeline.
  • Binary row format: Stores data in compact binary format in memory — smaller footprint, faster serialization than Java objects.
💡
Catalyst makes our SQL/DataFrame code correct and logically optimal — Tungsten makes the physical execution fast at the memory/CPU level. Result: less waiting, faster execution. Together they're why Spark DataFrames are much faster than raw RDDs.
5
cache() vs persist()?
Memory
▼

cache() is one of the optimization technique used to improve the performance of Spark applications. It is used to cache the intermediate results of the transformation so that other transformations can run on top of cached dataframes. These will perform faster.

cache() is shorthand for persist(StorageLevel.MEMORY_AND_DISK). It tries to store the DataFrame in memory and spills to disk if memory is insufficient.

persist() is the full API — choose the storage level explicitly:

  • MEMORY_ONLY: Fastest. If it doesn't fit, partitions are dropped and recomputed on access — no disk fallback.
  • MEMORY_AND_DISK: Default of cache(). Spills excess partitions to disk instead of recomputing. Safe for most use cases.
  • DISK_ONLY: Never uses memory. Use when the DataFrame is too large to fit in memory AND you want to avoid OOM errors. Slower but prevents crashes for extremely large intermediate results.
  • MEMORY_AND_DISK_SER: Serializes objects (smaller footprint) at cost of CPU for ser/deser.
  • Other types are: MEMORY_SER, DISK_SER, MEMORY_AND_DISK_2, MEMORY_ONLY_2, MEMORY_ONLY_SER etc.,
⚠️
Always call unpersist() when done to free resources. Never cache a DataFrame used only once — it's pointless overhead.
6
Adaptive Query Execution (AQE)
Optimization
▼

Traditional Spark optimization happens at query parse time based on statistics that may be stale or missing. AQE (Spark 3.0+) re-optimizes the query plan at runtime using actual shuffle statistics.

AQE is an optimization technique in Spark that makes the use of runtime statistics to choose the most efficient query execution plan, which is enabled by default.

  • Dynamic coalescing of shuffle partitions: If data is small, AQE merges small partitions → fewer tasks, less overhead.
  • Dynamic switch to broadcast join: If at runtime one side turns out small enough to broadcast, AQE switches from sort-merge to broadcast hash join mid-query.
  • Dynamic skew join handling: AQE detects skewed partitions (5x larger than median) and splits them into parallel sub-tasks.

Enable: spark.conf.set("spark.sql.adaptive.enabled", "true"). In Databricks, AQE is enabled by default from DBR 7.3+.

7
Data Skewness in a PySpark cluster
Performance
▼

Data skew occurs when data is not evenly distributed across partitions. One partition may have 10 million rows while others have 10,000 — that one task takes 100x longer and becomes the bottleneck of the entire stage.

  • Salting (manual): Append a random integer (0 to N-1) to the join key on the skewed side. Explode the small side N times with each salt value. Join on the salted composite key. Drop the salt column after joining. This distributes one hot key across N partitions.
  • AQE: Enable spark.sql.adaptive.enabled=true and spark.sql.adaptive.skewJoin.enabled=true. Spark 3.x automatically detects and splits skewed partitions at runtime — no code changes needed.
  • Broadcast join: If the smaller side is <~200MB, broadcast it to eliminate the shuffle entirely.
  • Repartition on a composite key that distributes more evenly before the join.
8
How do you optimize a slow-running Spark job?
PerformanceTuning
▼

"Whenever I see a slow Spark job, I don't just throw a bigger cluster at it. I follow a systematic approach to find the actual root cause first."

Step 1 — Open Spark UI: This is always my first stop. I go to the Stages tab, find the stage that's taking the longest, and look at three things:

  • Task duration variance — if most tasks finish in 2 seconds but one takes 40 minutes, that's data skew. One executor is getting all the heavy data.
  • Shuffle read/write size — if it's huge, there's an expensive join or groupBy causing massive data movement across executors.
  • Spill to disk — if non-zero, executors are running out of memory and writing to disk. Disk is 100x slower than RAM.

Step 2 — Fix based on what I find:

  • Data skew: I add salting — append a random number to the skewed key, groupBy on the salted key, then aggregate again to remove the salt. Or I enable AQE skew join hint: spark.sql.adaptive.skewJoin.enabled=true. AQE handles it automatically at runtime.
  • Too much shuffle: Push filters as early as possible so less data moves around. Use broadcast join if one side is small — df1.join(broadcast(df2), "id"). This eliminates the shuffle completely for that join.
  • Spill to disk: Either increase executor memory, reduce the number of concurrent tasks per executor, or fix the skew that's causing one partition to be too large.
  • UDFs: Replace Python UDFs with native Spark functions like regexp_replace, to_date, coalesce. UDFs break Catalyst optimization and serialize row by row between JVM and Python — very slow.
  • Enable AQE: spark.sql.adaptive.enabled=true — this lets Spark dynamically adjust shuffle partitions and join strategies at runtime based on actual data. Low effort, high impact.
  • Cache strategically: If the same DataFrame is used multiple times in a job, cache it after the first transformation. Without caching, Spark recomputes it from scratch every time it's referenced.
💡
I always compare Spark UI from the last good run vs the slow run side by side. The difference in stage timings and shuffle sizes usually points to the problem within 10 minutes — without guessing.
9
Join two DataFrames with different schemas using row numbers
PySparkWindow Function
▼

When two DataFrames have completely different columns and no natural join key, the only way to join them side-by-side is by their position — row 1 of DF1 pairs with row 1 of DF2. You do that by assigning row numbers to both using a window function, then joining on that row number.

df1: name, age  |  df2: city, salary — no common key between them
🐍 PySpark
from pyspark.sql.functions import row_number, monotonically_increasing_id
from pyspark.sql.window import Window

# Assign row numbers to both DataFrames
# monotonically_increasing_id gives a unique but not sequential id
# row_number over that gives clean 1,2,3... sequence

window = Window.orderBy("_id")

df1_rn = (df1.withColumn("_id", monotonically_increasing_id())
             .withColumn("rn", row_number().over(window)).drop("_id"))

df2_rn = (df2.withColumn("_id", monotonically_increasing_id())
             .withColumn("rn", row_number().over(window)).drop("_id"))

# Join on row number, then drop the helper column
result = (df1_rn.join(df2_rn, on="rn", how="inner").drop("rn"))
result.show()
⚠️
Row-number joins assume both DataFrames have a meaningful row order and the same number of rows. If the order isn't defined by a specific column, results can be non-deterministic across runs — Spark doesn't guarantee row order without an explicit orderBy. In production, always have a real join key. This pattern is mainly useful for positional merging of structured flat files.
10
Explain the difference between Vectorized UDF (pandas_udf) and standard UDF. Which is more efficient?
Performance
▼

Standard UDF: A Python function registered to run row-by-row. Spark cannot optimize it — it's a black box. Each row is serialized from JVM → Python process → processed → serialized back. This overhead is enormous, and UDFs disable Catalyst optimizations like predicate pushdown.

Vectorized UDF (pandas_udf): Data is batched into Pandas Series using Apache Arrow for zero-copy serialization. Much faster — typically 3-10x compared to row-at-a-time UDFs.

🐍 PySpark
from pyspark.sql.functions import pandas_udf
import pandas as pd

@pandas_udf("double")
def multiply_by_two(s: pd.Series) -> pd.Series:
    return s * 2

df.withColumn("result", multiply_by_two(col("amount")))
🏆
Performance ranking: Native Spark functions > Vectorized pandas_udf > Row-at-a-time UDF. Always prefer native functions — they're fully optimized by the Catalyst engine.
11
Partitioning vs Bucketing — when do you use each?
▼

"Both are ways to physically organize data for performance — but they solve different problems and work at different levels."

Partitioning: Splits data into separate folders/directories based on a column's values. When you query with a filter on the partition column, Spark skips entire folders that don't match — this is called partition pruning.

  • Best for low cardinality columns — date, region, country, status. A column with 365 values = 365 folders, which is fine. A column with 10 million values = 10 million folders (the small file problem).
  • Most effective for range queries and time-based filtering — "give me all orders from January 2024."
  • Partition columns should be the ones you filter on most frequently in WHERE clauses.
🐍 PySpark
# Partitioning — creates folder structure on disk
df.write.format("delta") \
  .partitionBy("year", "month") \
  .save("/mnt/delta/orders")
# Creates: /year=2024/month=01/, /year=2024/month=02/ etc.

# Query skips all other year/month folders — very fast
spark.sql("SELECT * FROM orders WHERE year=2024 AND month=01")

Bucketing: Distributes data into a fixed number of files (buckets) based on a hash of the bucket column. All rows with the same hash value go into the same bucket file — so when two tables are bucketed on the same column, joining them doesn't require a shuffle because matching rows are already co-located.

  • Best for high cardinality columns used in frequent joins — customer_id, order_id, product_id.
  • Most effective for join performance — if two large tables are both bucketed on the same join key with the same number of buckets, Spark skips the shuffle entirely.
  • Bucketing is a write-time decision — the data must be bucketed when written. You can't retroactively bucket existing data without rewriting it.
  • Bucketing only works with Hive/Spark tables (not with raw file paths) and the table metadata must be registered in the metastore.
🐍 PySpark
# Bucketing — distributes data by hash of customer_id into 64 buckets
df.write.format("parquet") \
  .bucketBy(64, "customer_id") \
  .sortBy("customer_id") \
  .saveAsTable("orders_bucketed")

# Joining two bucketed tables on the same key — no shuffle needed
orders    = spark.table("orders_bucketed")      # bucketed on customer_id
customers = spark.table("customers_bucketed")   # bucketed on customer_id, same 64 buckets

# Spark detects matching bucket structure — eliminates the shuffle stage
result = orders.join(customers, "customer_id")
Quick comparison
Partitioning
• Folder-level split
• Low cardinality columns
• Best for filter/range queries
• Works on any file format
• Partition pruning benefit
• Use: date, region, status
Bucketing
• Hash-based file split
• High cardinality columns
• Best for join performance
• Requires Hive metastore
• Shuffle elimination benefit
• Use: customer_id, order_id
💡
"Use both together for optimal performance: partition by date (for time-range filters) and bucket by customer_id (for join performance). In practice on Databricks and Delta Lake, Z-Ordering has largely replaced bucketing for most use cases — same join performance benefit without the metastore dependency."
12
What are the different read and write modes in Spark?
▼

Write Modes (batch):

  • append: Adds new data to the existing table/path. Existing data is untouched. Most common for incremental loads.
  • overwrite: Deletes all existing data and replaces with the new data. Use for full refreshes or when reprocessing a partition.
  • ignore: If data already exists at the target path, do nothing — silently skip the write. Useful for idempotent pipelines where you want first-write-wins behaviour.
  • error / errorIfExists (default): Throws an error if data already exists at the path. Protects against accidental overwrites.
🐍 PySpark
df.write.format("delta").mode("append").save("/mnt/delta/orders")
df.write.format("delta").mode("overwrite").save("/mnt/delta/orders")
df.write.format("delta").mode("ignore").save("/mnt/delta/orders")
df.write.format("delta").mode("error").save("/mnt/delta/orders")

# Partition overwrite — overwrite only specific partitions, not entire table
spark.conf.set("spark.sql.sources.partitionOverwriteMode", "dynamic")
df.write.format("delta").mode("overwrite").save("/mnt/delta/orders")
# Only partitions present in df get overwritten — others stay untouched

Streaming output modes:

  • append: Only new rows added since the last trigger are written. Most common. Cannot be used with aggregations (unless watermark is defined).
  • complete: The entire result table is written every trigger. Used with aggregations — e.g., running count of events per category. Expensive on large datasets.
  • update: Only rows that changed since last trigger are written. More efficient than complete for aggregations — only updated group rows are output.
🐍 PySpark
# Streaming write modes
df.writeStream.outputMode("append").format("delta").start(path)
df.writeStream.outputMode("complete").format("delta").start(path)
df.writeStream.outputMode("update").format("delta").start(path)

Read modes (for handling corrupt records):

  • PERMISSIVE (default): Corrupt records are set to null and put in a _corrupt_record column. Processing continues.
  • DROPMALFORMED: Silently drops corrupt records. Processing continues with clean records only.
  • FAILFAST: Throws an exception immediately when a corrupt record is encountered. Stops the job. Use in strict quality-controlled pipelines.
🐍 PySpark
df = (spark.read.format("csv")
  .option("header", "true")
  .option("mode", "PERMISSIVE")          # or DROPMALFORMED or FAILFAST
  .option("columnNameOfCorruptRecord", "_corrupt_record")
  .load("/mnt/bronze/orders.csv")
)

# Check what came in corrupt
corrupt = df.filter(col("_corrupt_record").isNotNull())
print(f"Corrupt records: {corrupt.count()}")
💡
In production I use PERMISSIVE with a corrupt record check after reading — this lets the pipeline continue but gives me visibility into bad records. FAILFAST is too strict for data engineering where source quality varies, and DROPMALFORMED is too silent.
13
Explain Broadcast Hash Joins. What is the default threshold size in Spark?
Optimization
▼

In a regular join, Spark must shuffle both tables across the network so matching keys land on the same executor. In a Broadcast Hash Join, the smaller table is fully copied ("broadcast") to every executor — no shuffle needed.

Default auto-broadcast threshold: 10MB (spark.sql.autoBroadcastJoinThreshold = 10485760). If the smaller table is under 10MB, Spark automatically broadcasts it.

🐍 PySpark
from pyspark.sql.functions import broadcast
df_large.join(broadcast(df_small), "key")
⚠️
Broadcasting a 500MB table to 100 executors = 50GB total memory consumed. Increase the threshold carefully. Use the explicit broadcast() hint when Spark's size estimate is off.
14
What is Spark Spill? How do you identify it in the Spark UI and resolve it?
Performance
▼

Spill occurs when a Spark task's in-memory data exceeds available executor memory. Spark writes excess to disk and reads it back when needed — disk I/O is 100-1000x slower than RAM.

Identify: Spark UI → Stages tab → look for non-zero Shuffle Spill (Memory) and Shuffle Spill (Disk) columns. Also check Stage detail → Task metrics for high "Disk bytes spilled".

  • Increase executor memory or upgrade node type.
  • Increase spark.sql.shuffle.partitions so each partition is smaller.
  • Fix data skew with salting or AQE — skewed tasks spill because they receive disproportionate amounts of data.
  • Push filters earlier to reduce data volume before expensive shuffle operations.
15
Coalesce vs. repartition?
Performance
▼

Thousands of tiny files require a metadata call per file and Spark creates one task per file — 10,000 files = 10,000 tasks = scheduler and storage service overwhelm.

  • coalesce(N): Reduces partition count without a full shuffle. Very efficient. Output may be slightly uneven. Use when reducing files before writing and balanced output is not critical.
  • repartition(N): is used to Increase/Decrease partitions. Causes a full shuffle — redistributes data evenly across N partitions. More expensive but produces balanced, evenly-sized output files.
🐍 PySpark
df.coalesce(10).write.parquet("output/")      # cheap, slightly uneven
df.repartition(10).write.parquet("output/")   # expensive, perfectly even
💡
In Delta Lake: use the OPTIMIZE command which compacts small files into larger ones (default ~1GB) via bin-packing. Schedule nightly alongside Auto-Compaction for real-time mitigation.
16
Write PySpark code to process and flatten nested JSON data.
Code
▼

Key functions: explode() for arrays (drops null rows), explode_outer() keeps null rows, col("struct.field") accesses nested struct fields.

🐍 PySpark
from pyspark.sql.functions import col, explode_outer

# Read nested JSON
df = spark.read.option("multiline", "true").json("path/to/nested.json")

# Step 1: Explode the orders array (keep rows with null arrays)
df_orders = df.select(
    col("user_id"),
    col("name"),
    explode_outer(col("orders")).alias("order")
)

# Step 2: Access struct fields and explode nested items array
df_items = df_orders.select(
    col("user_id"),
    col("name"),
    col("order.order_id"),
    explode_outer(col("order.items")).alias("item")
)

# Step 3: Flatten struct fields into top-level columns
df_flat = df_items.select(
    col("user_id"),
    col("name"),
    col("order_id"),
    col("item.product"),
    col("item.qty")
)
df_flat.show()
17
How do you implement parallel processing in Python for ETL workloads? How do you structure a reusable Python ETL framework?
Python ETL
▼

Parallel processing options in Python:

  • multiprocessing.Pool: True parallelism — bypasses Python's GIL. Use for CPU-bound tasks (data transformation, compression). Each process gets its own memory — no shared state issues.
  • concurrent.futures.ThreadPoolExecutor: Thread-based parallelism. Best for I/O-bound tasks (API calls, file reads, DB connections) where most time is waiting, not computing. GIL is released during I/O.
  • asyncio: Single-threaded async. Best for high-concurrency I/O (hundreds of API calls simultaneously) without thread overhead.
🐍 PySpark
from concurrent.futures import ThreadPoolExecutor, as_completed

tables = ["orders", "customers", "products", "inventory"]

def process_table(table_name):
    """Ingest one table — called in parallel"""
    df = read_from_source(table_name)
    df_clean = transform(df)
    write_to_adls(df_clean, f"/mnt/silver/{table_name}")
    return f"{table_name}: {len(df_clean)} rows"

# Process 4 tables in parallel (I/O bound → ThreadPool)
with ThreadPoolExecutor(max_workers=4) as executor:
    futures = {executor.submit(process_table, t): t for t in tables}
    for future in as_completed(futures):
        print(future.result())

Reusable ETL Framework structure:

  • config/ — pipeline configs as JSON/YAML (source, target, transformations, schedule)
  • readers/ — BaseReader abstract class + concrete: SqlReader, CsvReader, ApiReader, AdlsReader
  • transformers/ — BaseTransformer + concrete: DedupTransformer, SchemaValidator, TypeCaster
  • writers/ — BaseWriter + concrete: DeltaWriter, SqlWriter, AdlsWriter
  • utils/ — logger, audit_logger, secret_manager, retry_decorator
  • pipeline.py — orchestrates: read → validate → transform → write → audit_log
18
Partitioning strategies and how they impact performance
Partitioning
▼

Physical partitioning (on disk): Splitting data into directories based on column values — partitionBy("year","month"). Queries filtering on those columns skip irrelevant directories (partition pruning). Performance is excellent for the partition columns; no benefit for other columns.

Choosing partition columns: Low cardinality (date, region, status) — good. High cardinality (user_id, order_id) — bad (millions of tiny directories = small files problem, driver overwhelmed by metadata).

In-memory partitioning (Spark): Controls how data is split across executor tasks during processing.

  • spark.sql.shuffle.partitions: Number of partitions after a shuffle (default 200). Too few → large partitions → spill/OOM. Too many → tiny partitions → task overhead. Rule of thumb: target 100-200MB per partition.
  • repartition(n, col): Full shuffle to redistribute evenly, optionally on a column for co-location before a join.
  • coalesce(n): Reduce partition count without shuffle — cheap but potentially uneven.

Synapse/SQL Server table distribution: Hash (join performance), Round-Robin (staging/loading), Replicated (small dimension tables). This is a physical partitioning decision that affects every query permanently — choose carefully at table creation.

💡
Good partitioning = the right columns pruned at query time + balanced partition sizes in memory. Bad partitioning = either too few (skew/OOM) or too many (small files/overhead).
📀
Delta Lake
Row-level deletes, compaction, data skipping, Z-ordering, skew joins
1
Explain the architecture of Delta Lake and how it solves data lake reliability challenges.
Delta LakeCore Concept
▼

Before Delta Lake, a regular data lake (just Parquet files on ADLS) had serious problems — no ACID transactions, no schema enforcement, and no way to update or delete individual records. If your pipeline failed mid-write, you'd have partial corrupted data with no way to recover cleanly. Delta Lake was built specifically to fix these problems.

What Delta Lake actually is: It's a storage layer that sits on top of your existing cloud storage (ADLS, S3). Your data is still stored as Parquet files — Delta doesn't change that. What Delta adds is a transaction log called the _delta_log folder, which is the key to everything.

Delta table structure on ADLS:
/mnt/delta/orders/
   _delta_log/ ← transaction log (JSON + checkpoint files)
   part-0001.parquet
   part-0002.parquet
   part-0003.parquet

The transaction log (_delta_log): Every operation — write, update, delete, schema change — is recorded as a JSON entry in the transaction log. The log says which files were added, which were removed, and what the operation was. This is how Delta provides all its guarantees.

How Delta solves the reliability challenges:

  • ACID transactions: Because every write is first recorded in the transaction log atomically, a reader either sees the complete write or nothing at all — never a half-written state. If a Spark job fails mid-write, the transaction log has no commit entry for that operation, so readers ignore those partial files completely.

    Atomicity: Transaction either fully succeeds or fully fails. If a Spark job fails while writing, no commit entry is created in the transaction log. Table remains unchanged.

    Consistency: Table always stays in a valid state. Schema enforcement prevents corrupt writes.

    Isolation: Multiple jobs can write simultaneously. Delta uses Optimistic Concurrency Control. If a conflict is detected, the transaction fails and retry is required.

    Durability: Once a commit is written to _delta_log, data is permanent. Even if the cluster crashes.

  • Schema enforcement: Delta rejects writes that don't match the table schema. If your pipeline suddenly sends a string into a column that expects an integer, it fails loudly at write time — not silently corrupting the data and causing a wrong report 3 days later.
  • Schema evolution: When you genuinely need to add a new column, Delta supports it with mergeSchema=true — it updates the schema definition in the transaction log without rewriting existing files.
  • Time travel: Since the transaction log records every version, you can query the table as it looked at any previous point: SELECT * FROM orders VERSION AS OF 5 or TIMESTAMP AS OF '2024-01-15'. I've used this on the Citi project to recover a Gold table after a bad transformation run — just pointed the pipeline back to the last good version.
  • Upserts with MERGE: Plain Parquet has no concept of update or delete — you'd have to rewrite entire partitions. Delta supports MERGE INTO natively — match on a key, update if found, insert if not. This makes SCD Type 2 and incremental loads much cleaner.
  • Data skipping: Delta automatically stores min/max statistics per column for each file in the transaction log. When you run a filtered query, Delta checks these stats and skips files that can't possibly contain matching rows — without opening them. Z-ORDER further improves this by physically co-locating related rows in the same files.
🐍 PySpark
# MERGE example — upsert pattern
from delta.tables import DeltaTable

delta_table = DeltaTable.forPath(spark, "/mnt/delta/orders")

delta_table.alias("target").merge(
    updates_df.alias("source"),
    "target.order_id = source.order_id") \
    .whenMatchedUpdateAll() \
    .whenNotMatchedInsertAll() \
    .execute()
💡
Delta Lake turns your data lake into a data lakehouse — you get the scalability and low cost of cloud object storage, but with the reliability and consistency guarantees you'd normally only get from a data warehouse. The transaction log is what makes all of it possible.
2
Delta Table vs Delta Lake — what's the actual difference?
▼

"These two terms are often used interchangeably and it causes confusion. They're related but they describe different things — one is a framework, the other is a specific data object within it."

  • Delta Lake — the open-source storage framework. It's the technology, the protocol, the set of rules and capabilities. It defines how the transaction log works, how ACID transactions are implemented, how time travel is stored, how schema enforcement works. Delta Lake is what you install or enable — it's a library (delta-core) that adds capabilities on top of Apache Spark and cloud object storage (ADLS, S3, GCS).
  • Delta Table — a specific table stored using the Delta Lake format. It's the data object — a specific folder on ADLS that contains Parquet files + a _delta_log transaction log directory. When you write a DataFrame with .format("delta"), you create a Delta Table. A Delta Lake installation can manage thousands of Delta Tables.
Analogy: Delta Lake is like a database engine (e.g., SQL Server). A Delta Table is like a specific table inside that database engine (e.g., dbo.orders).

Another way: Delta Lake = the system. Delta Table = one object inside that system.
🐍 PySpark
# Delta Lake — the framework (library installed on the cluster)
# You configure it at the cluster or session level
spark = SparkSession.builder \
    .config("spark.sql.extensions", "io.delta.sql.DeltaSparkSessionExtension") \
    .config("spark.sql.catalog.spark_catalog", "org.apache.spark.sql.delta.catalog.DeltaCatalog") \
    .getOrCreate()

# Delta Table — a specific table created using the Delta Lake format
df.write.format("delta").save("/mnt/delta/orders")
# This created ONE Delta Table at /mnt/delta/orders

# You can have many Delta Tables managed by one Delta Lake setup
df.write.format("delta").save("/mnt/delta/customers")
df.write.format("delta").save("/mnt/delta/products")
# Three Delta Tables, all governed by the same Delta Lake framework
💡
"In interviews, if someone says 'we store our data in Delta Lake' — they mean their data platform uses the Delta Lake storage format. If they say 'query the Delta Table' — they mean a specific table at a specific path. The distinction matters when someone asks 'how many Delta Lakes do you have?' — the answer is one framework, many tables within it."
3
How do you implement Row Level Deletions in Delta Lake without rewriting the entire partition?
Delta LakeBest Practice
▼

"This is one of the things that makes Delta Lake genuinely different from plain Parquet. With Parquet, if you want to delete even a single row, you have to rewrite the entire file that contains it — which is expensive. Delta Lake gives you two approaches."

Approach 1 — Simple DELETE statement:

Delta supports SQL-style deletes directly:

🗄 SQL
