-- Delete a specific customer's records (e.g. GDPR request)
DELETE FROM orders
WHERE customer_id = 'C1001';

-- Or with a condition
DELETE FROM orders
WHERE order_date < '2022-01-01' AND status = 'cancelled';

Internally, Delta reads the affected files, removes the matching rows, and writes new Parquet files with those rows excluded. The old files are marked as removed in the transaction log but not immediately deleted from storage — that's handled by VACUUM later.

Approach 2 — Deletion Vectors (newer, more efficient):

This is the smarter approach introduced in newer Databricks runtimes. Instead of rewriting the entire file when you delete a row, Delta writes a small deletion vector — a bitmap file that just marks which rows in a file are deleted. The original Parquet file stays untouched.

  • When a query reads that file, it applies the deletion vector as a filter — marked rows are skipped.
  • The actual file is only rewritten when you run OPTIMIZE — which you'd run anyway as part of maintenance.
  • This makes individual row deletions much cheaper — you're writing a tiny bitmap instead of rewriting a 500MB Parquet file.
🗄 SQL
-- Enable deletion vectors on a table
ALTER TABLE orders
SET TBLPROPERTIES ('delta.enableDeletionVectors' = 'true');

-- Now DELETE is fast — just writes a bitmap
DELETE FROM orders WHERE customer_id = 'C1001';
💡
Real use case I'd mention: GDPR "right to be forgotten" requests. A customer asks to have all their data deleted. With deletion vectors, I can delete their rows across multiple Delta tables immediately without triggering expensive full file rewrites across the entire dataset.
4
How does the Medallion Architecture work, and what are its advantages?
ArchitectureCore Concept
▼

"Medallion Architecture is a layered approach to organizing data in a lakehouse — I've used it both on my NYC Taxi project and conceptually on the Citi project. It splits the pipeline into three progressively cleaner layers."

  • Bronze layer — raw, as-is: Data lands here exactly as it arrived from the source — no transformations, no cleaning. On my NYC Taxi project, this was raw parquet files ingested directly from the public dataset via ADF. We preserve source fidelity here — if something breaks downstream, we can always reprocess from Bronze without touching the source again.
  • Silver layer — cleaned and curated: Here I apply schema normalization, deduplication, null handling, type casting, and business rule validation. This is where I handled schema drift across NYC Taxi parquet files — different files had inconsistent INT32/INT64/DOUBLE types for the same column, so I normalized each file individually before unioning. Output is partitioned for performance — I partitioned by pickup year and month.
  • Gold layer — business-ready aggregates: This is where I build the final marts for reporting — daily KPI summaries, borough-level analytics, hourly trip patterns. Stored as Delta tables for ACID guarantees, and this is what Power BI connects to directly.

Advantages of this approach:

  • Incremental quality improvement: Each layer adds more structure and trust. You're never debugging "is this raw or processed" — the layer name tells you.
  • Reprocessing without re-ingestion: If my Silver transformation logic has a bug, I fix it and reprocess from Bronze — I don't need to hit the source system again, which is often slow or rate-limited.
  • Different audiences, different layers: Data engineers and auditors can go to Bronze for raw fidelity. Analysts work with Silver for ad-hoc exploration. BI tools and business users only touch Gold — fast, clean, pre-aggregated.
  • Cost and performance separation: Bronze can be cheap, infrequently-queried storage. Gold can be highly optimized — partitioned, Z-ordered, small number of large files — specifically for fast BI reads.
  • Clear audit trail: Since Bronze is untouched raw data, it acts as a natural point of reconciliation — I can always check Gold-layer totals against Bronze-layer raw counts to catch transformation bugs.
💡
"Medallion Architecture isn't a tool, it's a pattern — Bronze is your safety net, Silver is your trust layer, Gold is your business interface. I use it as the default structure for almost any lakehouse design."
5
How does Delta Lake support streaming and batch together? What is Lambda vs Kappa architecture?
▼

"This is one of Delta Lake's most powerful features — the same table can be written to by a batch job and read from by a streaming job simultaneously, with full consistency. This is called the Lakehouse architecture and it eliminates the need to maintain separate batch and streaming pipelines."

How it works — unified table for both:

  • A batch ADF pipeline runs nightly and appends records to a Delta table.
  • At the same time, a Structured Streaming job reads from the same Delta table using readStream — Delta's transaction log tells the stream exactly which new files to process since its last checkpoint. It never reads a file twice and never misses a new one.
  • ACID transactions ensure the stream reader never sees a partially written batch — it either sees the full committed batch or waits for it to complete.
🐍 PySpark
# Batch write — standard
df_batch.write.format("delta").mode("append").save("/mnt/delta/events")

# Stream read from the SAME table — works seamlessly
stream_df = (spark.readStream.format("delta").load("/mnt/delta/events"))

stream_df.writeStream \
  .format("delta") \
  .option("checkpointLocation", "/mnt/checkpoints/events") \
  .outputMode("append") \
  .start("/mnt/delta/events_processed")

Lambda Architecture (old way): Maintain two separate systems — a batch layer (Hadoop/Spark for historical accuracy) and a speed layer (Kafka/Storm for real-time). Results from both are merged at the serving layer. Problem: two codebases, two sets of bugs, two maintenance efforts. The batch and stream logic often diverge over time.

Kappa Architecture (simpler): Everything goes through streaming. Historical reprocessing is done by replaying the stream from the beginning. Simpler architecture — one codebase. Problem: reprocessing at scale is expensive, and batch jobs are often more efficient for large historical loads.

Lakehouse with Delta (best of both): One storage layer, one table, both batch and streaming read/write to it. No duplication, no divergence. This is what Databricks calls the Lakehouse pattern — and Delta's transaction log is what makes it possible.

💡
"In my projects, I use Delta as a unified table. ADF batch loads run nightly, Structured Streaming reads the same table for near-real-time use cases. Same data, same quality guarantees, one pipeline to maintain."
6
A streaming job is writing duplicate records to a Delta table. How would you prevent and resolve the issue?
ScenarioStreaming
▼

"First, I'd figure out why duplicates are happening before jumping to a fix — usually it's one of three causes: the job restarted and reprocessed already-written data, the checkpoint location was deleted or corrupted, or the source itself is sending duplicate events due to at-least-once delivery."

Step 1 — Check the checkpoint: Structured Streaming relies on a checkpoint location to track which offsets it has already processed. If the checkpoint is missing, pointed to the wrong path, or was manually deleted, Spark has no memory of what it already wrote — and reprocesses from the beginning, creating duplicates.

🐍 PySpark
df.writeStream \
  .format("delta") \
  .option("checkpointLocation", "/mnt/checkpoints/orders_stream") \
  .start("/mnt/delta/orders")

Step 2 — Resolve existing duplicates in the table: If duplicates already exist, I clean them up first — keep the latest version per natural key using a window function, then overwrite the table or the affected partitions.

🐍 PySpark
from pyspark.sql.window import Window
from pyspark.sql.functions import row_number, col

window = Window.partitionBy("order_id").orderBy(col("event_time").desc())

deduped = (df.withColumn("rn", row_number().over(window))
             .filter(col("rn") == 1).drop("rn"))
deduped.write.format("delta").mode("overwrite").save("/mnt/delta/orders")

Step 3 — Prevent it going forward:

  • MERGE in foreachBatch: Instead of plain append, I switch to a MERGE pattern per micro-batch — match on the natural key, update if found, insert if not. This makes the write idempotent — even if the same batch runs twice, the result is identical.
  • Watermark + dropDuplicates: For dedup within a time window, I add withWatermark("event_time", "10 minutes").dropDuplicates(["event_id"]) — Spark maintains a state store of seen IDs within that window.
  • Never delete the checkpoint manually: If schema changes force a checkpoint reset, I always run a one-time deduplication pass immediately after restart to clean up the reprocessing overlap.
🐍 PySpark
def upsert_batch(batch_df, batch_id):
    batch_df.createOrReplaceTempView("updates")
    spark.sql("""
        MERGE INTO orders t
        USING updates s ON t.order_id = s.order_id
        WHEN MATCHED THEN UPDATE SET *
        WHEN NOT MATCHED THEN INSERT *
    """)

df.writeStream.foreachBatch(upsert_batch) \
  .option("checkpointLocation", "/mnt/checkpoints/orders_stream") \
  .start()
💡
"My rule with streaming pipelines — never use plain append for anything that can restart or reprocess. MERGE is slightly more expensive per batch, but it makes the entire pipeline idempotent, which means I sleep better at night."
7
Explain the Data Skipping mechanism in Delta Lake. How does it differ from traditional SQL indexing?
Optimization
▼

When you read from a Delta table with a filter (e.g., WHERE event_date = '2024-01-15'), Delta reads the transaction log's file-level statistics before scanning any data. For each Parquet file, the log stores: min value, max value, and null count per column.

If a file's event_date range is 2024-01-10 to 2024-01-12, Delta knows it cannot possibly contain 2024-01-15 and skips it entirely — no I/O, no task created.

vs. traditional SQL index: A B-tree index points to exact row locations — ideal for OLTP single-row lookups. Delta's data skipping is coarser (file-level, not row-level) but is designed for analytical scans across billions of rows in object storage where random I/O is impractical.

💡
Z-Ordering colocates related data within files: OPTIMIZE table ZORDER BY (customer_id, event_date). This makes min/max statistics tight and effective — maximizing data skipping hits on those columns.
8
How do you choose between Partitioning and Z-Ordering for a 10TB dataset?
Architecture Decision
▼

These solve different problems and work best together:

Partitioning creates physical directory splits. A query with WHERE year=2024 reads only that folder — extremely effective for the partition column. Problem: high-cardinality columns (user_id with millions of values) → millions of tiny directories.

Z-Ordering works within partitions. It reorders rows so rows sharing values in Z-ordered columns are stored together in the same files, making data skipping (min/max) effective for those columns.

  • Pick 1-2 low-cardinality columns that appear in almost every query filter → Partition by these (e.g., date, region).
  • Pick 2-3 higher-cardinality columns that appear in many filters but aren't suitable for partitioning → Z-Order by these (e.g., customer_id, product_id).
  • Never partition by a high-cardinality column — this creates the severe small files problem.
9
Explain Skew Join optimization in Spark 3.x and how to handle it manually when AQE is disabled.
Advanced
▼

With AQE enabled: AQE monitors shuffle statistics mid-query. If a partition is more than 5x the median size (and larger than 256MB), AQE splits that partition into sub-partitions and processes them in parallel. Completely transparent — no code changes needed.

Manually without AQE (Salting):

🐍 PySpark
from pyspark.sql.functions import rand, lit, concat, col, explode, array

N = 10  # salt factor
# Skewed (large) side - append random salt
big = big_df.withColumn("salt", (rand() * N).cast("int")) \
            .withColumn("key_s", concat(col("key"), lit("_"), col("salt")))

# Small side - replicate N times with each salt value
small = small_df.withColumn("salt", explode(array([lit(i) for i in range(N)]))) \
                .withColumn("key_s", concat(col("key"), lit("_"), col("salt")))

# Join on salted key, then drop salt columns
result = big.join(small, "key_s").drop("salt", "key_s")
10
How do you implement SCD (Slowly Changing Dimensions) in a streaming environment?
▼

"SCD in batch is straightforward — you have the full dataset, you can compare and merge. In streaming, you only see new events as they arrive, so you need a state store to remember what you've already written. The pattern I use is MERGE in foreachBatch — it gives you the control of batch SCD logic but runs on every micro-batch."

SCD Type 1 in Streaming (overwrite latest value):

🐍 PySpark
from delta.tables import DeltaTable

def upsert_scd1(batch_df, batch_id):
    """SCD Type 1 — just overwrite with latest value, no history"""
    delta_table = DeltaTable.forPath(spark, "/mnt/delta/dim_customer")

    delta_table.alias("target").merge(
        batch_df.alias("source"),
        "target.customer_id = source.customer_id"
    ).whenMatchedUpdateAll()    \
     .whenNotMatchedInsertAll() \
     .execute()

stream = (spark.readStream
    .format("kafka")
    .option("subscribe", "customer-updates")
    .load()
    .select(from_json(col("value").cast("string"), schema).alias("data"))
    .select("data.*")
)

stream.writeStream \
    .foreachBatch(upsert_scd1) \
    .option("checkpointLocation", "/mnt/checkpoints/dim_customer") \
    .start()

SCD Type 2 in Streaming (keep full history with effective dates):

This is more complex — when a record changes, I need to close the existing row (set end_date, is_current=False) and insert a new row with the new values. The MERGE handles this in one atomic operation per micro-batch.

🐍 PySpark
from pyspark.sql.functions import current_timestamp, lit, col

def upsert_scd2(batch_df, batch_id):
    """SCD Type 2 — expire old row, insert new row with history"""
    delta_table = DeltaTable.forPath(spark, "/mnt/delta/dim_customer_scd2")

    # Step 1 — close the existing current rows that have changed
    delta_table.alias("target").merge(
        batch_df.alias("source"),
        """target.customer_id = source.customer_id
           AND target.is_current = true
           AND (target.email != source.email
                OR target.region != source.region)"""
    ).whenMatchedUpdate(set={
        "is_current":  lit(False),
        "end_date":    current_timestamp()
    }).execute()

    # Step 2 — insert new rows for all incoming records
    (batch_df
        .withColumn("start_date",  current_timestamp())
        .withColumn("end_date",    lit(None).cast("timestamp"))
        .withColumn("is_current",  lit(True))
        .write
        .format("delta")
        .mode("append")
        .save("/mnt/delta/dim_customer_scd2")
    )

stream.writeStream \
    .foreachBatch(upsert_scd2) \
    .option("checkpointLocation", "/mnt/checkpoints/dim_customer_scd2") \
    .start()
💡
"The key insight for streaming SCD: foreachBatch converts each micro-batch into a static DataFrame, giving you full access to Delta MERGE semantics. You write batch SCD logic — it just runs automatically every few seconds or minutes."
11
How does Delta Lake handle schema evolution and versioning?
▼

"Delta Lake gives you two complementary controls — schema enforcement (reject changes you didn't ask for) and schema evolution (accept changes you explicitly allow). Both are managed through the transaction log, so every schema change is versioned and auditable."

Schema Enforcement (default):

By default, Delta rejects any write where the DataFrame schema doesn't match the table schema. This protects the table from silent corruption — a wrong data type or an unexpected column causes a hard failure at write time, not a downstream analytics bug days later.

Schema Evolution — three scenarios:

🐍 PySpark
# ── 1. Adding new columns — mergeSchema ───────────────
# Source now has a new column 'loyalty_score' not in target
df_with_new_col.write \
    .format("delta") \
    .mode("append") \
    .option("mergeSchema", "true") \  # new column gets added to table schema
    .save("/mnt/delta/customers")

# Or set globally for the session
spark.conf.set("spark.databricks.delta.schema.autoMerge.enabled", "true")

# ── 2. Schema changes via ALTER TABLE (SQL) ────────────
# Add a column
spark.sql("""
    ALTER TABLE customers
    ADD COLUMNS (loyalty_score DOUBLE COMMENT 'Loyalty program score')
""")

# Change column type (widening only — e.g. INT → BIGINT)
spark.sql("""
    ALTER TABLE customers
    ALTER COLUMN order_count TYPE BIGINT
""")

# Rename a column (requires column mapping mode)
spark.sql("""
    ALTER TABLE customers
    RENAME COLUMN cust_name TO customer_name
""")

# Drop a column (requires column mapping mode)
spark.sql("""
    ALTER TABLE customers
    DROP COLUMN legacy_field
""")

# ── 3. Enable Column Mapping (for rename/drop) ─────────
spark.sql("""
    ALTER TABLE customers SET TBLPROPERTIES (
        'delta.columnMapping.mode' = 'name',
        'delta.minReaderVersion'   = '2',
        'delta.minWriterVersion'   = '5'
    )
""")

Schema Versioning: Every schema change is recorded in the transaction log as a separate commit with a new version number. You can see the full history:

🐍 PySpark
# See full history including schema changes
spark.sql("DESCRIBE HISTORY customers").show(truncate=False)
# Shows: version, timestamp, operation, operationParameters

# Read the table as it was before a schema change
old_df = spark.read \
    .format("delta") \
    .option("versionAsOf", 5) \
    .load("/mnt/delta/customers")

# Old version had the old schema — the new column will be null
# for rows written before the column was added
💡
"Schema enforcement is your safety net, schema evolution is your escape hatch. In production, I keep enforcement on (default) and only use mergeSchema or ALTER TABLE consciously when a source genuinely adds a new field — not as a way to bypass schema validation on bad data."
12
How do you implement SCD Type 2 using PySpark or Databricks SQL?
▼

"SCD Type 2 keeps the full history of changes — when an attribute changes, the old row is 'closed' with an end date, and a new row is inserted with the new values and a new start date. Each row has start_date, end_date, and is_current columns."

Target table — dim_customer_scd2:
customer_id, name, region, email, start_date, end_date, is_current, surrogate_key

Method 1 — PySpark with Delta MERGE:

🐍 PySpark
from delta.tables import DeltaTable
from pyspark.sql.functions import (
    current_timestamp, lit, col, sha2, concat_ws
)

# Incoming updates
updates = spark.read.format("delta").load("/mnt/silver/customer_updates")

# Add a surrogate key based on content
updates = updates.withColumn(
    "surrogate_key",
    sha2(concat_ws("||", "customer_id", "region", "email"), 256)
)

target = DeltaTable.forPath(spark, "/mnt/delta/dim_customer_scd2")

# Step 1 — expire rows where tracked columns changed
target.alias("t").merge(
    updates.alias("s"),
    """t.customer_id = s.customer_id
       AND t.is_current = true
       AND t.surrogate_key != s.surrogate_key"""
).whenMatchedUpdate(set={
    "is_current": lit(False),
    "end_date":   current_timestamp()
}).execute()

# Step 2 — insert new rows for changed records + brand new records
new_rows = updates.alias("s").join(
    target.toDF().filter(col("is_current") == True).alias("t"),
    (col("s.customer_id") == col("t.customer_id")) &
    (col("s.surrogate_key") == col("t.surrogate_key")),
    "left_anti"   # only rows NOT already current
)

(new_rows
    .withColumn("start_date",  current_timestamp())
    .withColumn("end_date",    lit(None).cast("timestamp"))
    .withColumn("is_current",  lit(True))
    .write.format("delta").mode("append")
    .save("/mnt/delta/dim_customer_scd2")
)

Method 2 — Databricks SQL (cleaner for SQL-first teams):

🗄 SQL
-- Step 1: expire changed rows
MERGE INTO dim_customer_scd2 AS target
USING customer_updates AS source
ON target.customer_id = source.customer_id
   AND target.is_current = true
   AND (target.region != source.region
        OR target.email != source.email)
WHEN MATCHED THEN UPDATE SET
    target.is_current = false,
    target.end_date   = current_timestamp();

-- Step 2: insert new rows (changed + new customers)
INSERT INTO dim_customer_scd2
SELECT
    source.customer_id,
    source.name,
    source.region,
    source.email,
    current_timestamp()  AS start_date,
    NULL                 AS end_date,
    true                 AS is_current,
    sha2(concat_ws('||', source.customer_id,
                   source.region, source.email), 256) AS surrogate_key
FROM customer_updates AS source
LEFT JOIN dim_customer_scd2 AS target
    ON source.customer_id = target.customer_id
   AND target.is_current = true
   AND target.region  = source.region
   AND target.email   = source.email
WHERE target.customer_id IS NULL;  -- no matching current row = new or changed
💡
"The surrogate key trick (sha2 hash of tracked columns) is the most efficient way to detect changes without comparing every column individually. If the hash matches, nothing changed — skip it. If it doesn't match, close the old row and insert a new one."
🗄
SQL
Query optimization, indexing, MERGE, duplicates, consecutive logins, masking
1
SQL query execution order & Types of Joins
SQL FundamentalsCore Concept
▼

1. SQL Execution Order

Even though we write a query as SELECT → FROM → WHERE → GROUP BY → HAVING → ORDER BY, that's not how the database actually runs it. The database executes it almost in the reverse order:

Logical execution order:
FROM → JOIN → WHERE → GROUP BY → HAVING → SELECT → DISTINCT → ORDER BY → LIMIT

First the database figures out which tables to read and joins them (FROM/JOIN), then it filters rows before grouping (WHERE), then it groups the data (GROUP BY), then it filters the groups (HAVING — that's why HAVING can use aggregate functions but WHERE can't), then it picks the columns to display (SELECT), and finally sorts and limits the result (ORDER BY, LIMIT).

💡
This is exactly why you can't use a column alias defined in SELECT inside a WHERE clause — WHERE runs before SELECT even exists yet.

2. Types of Joins

Joins combine rows from two tables based on a related column. Here's how I'd explain each one:

  • INNER JOIN: Returns only the matching rows from both tables. If a customer has no orders, that customer won't show up at all.
  • LEFT JOIN (LEFT OUTER): Returns all rows from the left table, plus matching rows from the right table. If there's no match, right-side columns come back as NULL. I use this when I want "all customers, and their orders if they have any."
  • RIGHT JOIN (RIGHT OUTER): Same as LEFT JOIN but flipped — all rows from the right table, matched with the left. In practice I almost never use this — I just swap the table order and use LEFT JOIN, it's easier to read.
  • FULL OUTER JOIN: Returns everything from both tables — matched rows, plus unmatched rows from either side filled with NULLs. Useful when I want to find mismatches in both directions, like reconciling two datasets.
  • CROSS JOIN: Produces the Cartesian product — every row from table A combined with every row from table B. I rarely use this intentionally; usually it shows up by accident when a join condition is missing, and it can blow up your row count fast.
  • SELF JOIN: A table joined with itself, using aliases.

    Sometimes all the information you need is already in one table, but you need to compare one row with another row from the same table.

    Self Join is Not Just Table joined with itself, Self Join is One row needs information from another row in the same table

    "I have a value in one row, and I need to use that value to find information from another row in the same table."

    I've used this for hierarchical data — like finding an employee's manager, where both employee and manager are in the same Employees table.
  • LEFT SEMI JOIN: This is a Spark/PySpark concept — it returns only the columns from the left table where a match exists in the right table, but it never duplicates rows even if there are multiple matches on the right. It's basically an "EXISTS" check. I'd use it when I want to filter customers who placed at least one order, but I don't care about order details.
    🗄 SQL
    SELECT *
FROM customers c
WHERE EXISTS (
    SELECT 1
    FROM orders o
    WHERE c.customer_id = o.customer_id);
  • LEFT ANTI JOIN: The opposite of semi join — returns rows from the left table that have no match in the right table. I use this a lot for finding "customers who never placed an order" or "records in source but missing in target" — a classic data validation check.
    🗄 SQL
    SELECT *
FROM customers c
WHERE NOT EXISTS (
    SELECT 1
    FROM orders o
    WHERE c.customer_id = o.customer_id);
📌
In plain SQL, semi/anti joins are written using EXISTS / NOT EXISTS or IN / NOT IN. In PySpark, they're literal join types: df1.join(df2, "id", "left_semi") and df1.join(df2, "id", "left_anti").
2
CTE, CTAS, Views, Stored Procedures & Indexes
Core Concept
▼

These all help organize and reuse SQL logic, but they solve different problems — I usually explain them by when I'd reach for each one.

CTE (Common Table Expression) — a temporary, named result set(virtual table) in SQL that can be used multiple times within a single query. Defined using the WITH keyword, it allows us to break down large queries into smaller, manageable logical sections that can be referenced multiple times within the same statement. I use this to break a complex query into readable steps, especially before a final SELECT or before a window function.

🗄 SQL
WITH high_value_customers AS (
    SELECT customer_id, SUM(amount) AS total_spent
    FROM orders
    GROUP BY customer_id
    HAVING SUM(amount) > 10000
)
SELECT c.customer_id, c.name, h.total_spent
FROM customers c
JOIN high_value_customers h ON c.customer_id = h.customer_id;

When I'd use it: When my query is getting deeply nested with subqueries and hard to read, or when I need to reference the same derived result multiple times in one query (recursive CTEs are also great for hierarchies like org charts).

CTAS (Create Table As Select) — physically creates a new table and populates it with the result of a query. Unlike a CTE, this data is persisted on disk.

🗄 SQL
CREATE TABLE high_value_customers AS
SELECT customer_id, SUM(amount) AS total_spent
FROM orders
GROUP BY customer_id
HAVING SUM(amount) > 10000;

When I'd use it: When a calculation is expensive and I don't want to recompute it every time — like building a Gold layer summary table in a lakehouse. I've used CTAS in Synapse to materialize aggregated tables for Power BI so the dashboard doesn't hit raw data every time.

View — is a virtual table created from a SQL query. It does not store data itself but provides a simplified and reusable way to access data from one or more underlying tables.

A table physically stores data, whereas a view stores only the SQL definition and retrieves data from underlying tables when queried.

Views are useful for encapsulating complex logic, providing a consistent interface, and controlling access to data.

🗄 SQL
CREATE VIEW vw_high_value_customers AS
SELECT customer_id, SUM(amount) AS total_spent
FROM orders
GROUP BY customer_id
HAVING SUM(amount) > 10000;

-- usage
SELECT * FROM vw_high_value_customers WHERE total_spent > 50000;

When I'd use it: When I want to give analysts or BI tools a simplified, reusable, always-up-to-date interface to complex logic, without duplicating storage. The tradeoff vs CTAS is — a view is always fresh but recomputes every time, while CTAS is fast to read but can go stale until refreshed.

Stored Procedure — a saved block of SQL code (can include logic, loops, conditions, multiple statements) that you call by name. It is a precompiled collection of one or more SQL statements stored in a database that can be executed as a single unit used to automate complex tasks.

🗄 SQL
CREATE PROCEDURE usp_refresh_customer_summary
AS
BEGIN
    TRUNCATE TABLE customer_summary;

    INSERT INTO customer_summary
    SELECT customer_id, SUM(amount) AS total_spent
    FROM orders
    GROUP BY customer_id;
END;

-- usage
EXEC usp_refresh_customer_summary;

When I'd use it: When I need to encapsulate a multi-step process — like truncate-and-reload logic, or a batch job that does validation, then transformation, then logging — and trigger it as a single callable unit, often from an ADF pipeline via a Stored Procedure activity.

📌
Quick way to remember the difference: CTE = temporary, query-scoped shortcut. View = saved query, no storage. CTAS = saved query, with storage (a real table). Stored Procedure = saved multi-step logic/actions, can do more than just SELECT.

4. Indexes

An index is a database structure that improves the query performance by allowing database to locate requried rows quickly instead of scanning the entire table.

  • Clustered Index: Determines the physical order of data in the table. There can be only one per table (because data can only be physically sorted one way). In SQL Server, the primary key creates a clustered index by default.
  • Non-Clustered Index: A separate structure that holds the indexed column(s) plus a pointer back to the actual row. You can have many of these on one table. I'd add these on columns that are frequently used in WHERE clauses or JOIN conditions but aren't the primary key.
  • Composite Index: An index on multiple columns together — useful when queries always filter on the same combination of columns, like (customer_id, order_date).
  • Unique Index: Enforces that no two rows have the same value in that column, while also speeding up lookups.

When I'd use indexes: On columns I frequently filter, join, or sort by — especially foreign keys and date columns in large fact tables. The tradeoff is that indexes speed up reads but slow down writes (every INSERT/UPDATE/DELETE has to update the index too), so I avoid over-indexing staging or heavily-written tables.

⚠️
In Synapse Dedicated Pools, the equivalent concept is the Clustered Columnstore Index (CCI) — used by default on large fact tables for analytical workloads, since it compresses data column-wise and speeds up aggregations rather than single-row lookups.
3
Indexes in SQL — types, internals, when to use / avoid
IndexesCore Concept
▼

An index is a separate data structure (B-tree or other) that stores a sorted copy of one or more columns with pointers to original rows. It converts O(n) table scans into O(log n) lookups.

Types:

  • Clustered Index: Determines the physical order of rows in the table. One per table. Rows are stored in the B-tree leaf nodes. Range queries on the clustered key are extremely fast. SQL Server PKs are clustered by default.
  • Non-Clustered Index: Separate structure with key + pointer (row locator) back to the table. Multiple allowed. Best for columns frequently in WHERE, JOIN ON, ORDER BY that aren't the PK.
  • Covering Index (INCLUDE): A non-clustered index that includes extra columns (via INCLUDE clause) beyond the key. Query can be satisfied entirely from the index — no lookup to the main table ("index-covered query"). Fastest reads for specific query patterns.
  • Composite Index: Index on multiple columns. Column order matters — the index can only be used for queries that filter on a left-prefix of the index columns. INDEX(a, b, c) can serve WHERE a=?, WHERE a=? AND b=?, but NOT WHERE b=?.
  • Columnstore Index: Stores data column-by-column (vertically), highly compressed. Optimal for analytics — aggregating millions of rows on a few columns. Used by default in Synapse Dedicated Pool (CCI = Clustered Columnstore Index). Not suited for row-level lookups.
  • Filtered Index: Index with a WHERE clause — only indexes rows matching the filter. Smaller, faster for specific query patterns (e.g., index only status = 'ACTIVE' rows).

When NOT to index: Columns with very low cardinality (e.g., gender — only 2 values, a scan is faster than an index on 50% of a table). Tables with very frequent writes — every INSERT/UPDATE/DELETE maintains all indexes. Staging/temp tables — not worth the overhead.

⚠️
Common pitfalls: functions on indexed columns prevent index use (WHERE YEAR(order_date)=2024 → full scan). Leading wildcards in LIKE prevent index use (LIKE '%keyword' → full scan, but LIKE 'keyword%' uses the index).
4
How would you optimize a slow SQL query? What steps and tools do you use?
Performance
▼
  1. Read the execution plan first: EXPLAIN ANALYZE SELECT ... — look for full table scans, sort operations, nested loops, and large gaps between estimated and actual row counts.
  2. Check indexes: Is there an index on the filter columns (WHERE, JOIN ON)? Are existing indexes being used or bypassed by the optimizer?
  3. Reduce the dataset early: Push WHERE filters as early as possible. Avoid SELECT * — select only the columns needed.
  4. Avoid functions on indexed columns: WHERE YEAR(created_at) = 2024 prevents index use. Rewrite as: WHERE created_at >= '2024-01-01' AND created_at < '2025-01-01'
  5. Check join order and type: Join smaller tables first. Ensure join columns are indexed on both sides.
  6. Update statistics: UPDATE STATISTICS (SQL Server) or ANALYZE (PostgreSQL). Stale stats produce suboptimal execution plans.
  7. Avoid correlated subqueries: Replace them with JOINs or CTEs — correlated subqueries execute once per row in the outer query.
  8. In Synapse: Check distribution skew with DBCC PDW_SHOWSPACEUSED, update statistics, consider BROADCAST hints on small dimension tables.
5
Explain database indexing and its impact on performance.
Fundamentals
▼

An index is a separate data structure (usually a B-tree) that stores a sorted copy of one or more columns along with pointers to the original rows. Instead of scanning every row, the database finds matching rows in O(log n) time.

  • Clustered index: The table's physical row order matches the index. One per table. Best for range queries on the clustered key.
  • Non-clustered index: A separate structure pointing into the table. Multiple allowed. Best for frequently-filtered non-PK columns.
  • Covering index: Includes all columns needed by a query (using INCLUDE). The query is satisfied from the index alone — no table lookup required.
  • Columnstore index: Stores data column by column, highly compressed. Ideal for analytical aggregations over millions of rows.
⚖️
Indexes speed up reads but slow down writes — the index must be maintained on every INSERT, UPDATE, and DELETE. Index only columns that appear in WHERE, JOIN ON, and ORDER BY clauses of slow queries.
6
Explain the MERGE statement. How is it different from UPSERT?
Code
▼

UPSERT is a concept: insert if a record doesn't exist, update if it does. MERGE is the SQL statement that implements this — and more. MERGE is a superset of UPSERT — it can handle UPDATE, INSERT, and DELETE in one atomic statement.

🗄 SQL
MERGE INTO target_table AS t
USING source_table AS s
ON t.order_id = s.order_id
WHEN MATCHED AND s.amount != t.amount THEN
    UPDATE SET t.amount = s.amount, t.updated_at = GETDATE()
WHEN NOT MATCHED BY TARGET THEN
    INSERT (order_id, amount, created_at)
    VALUES (s.order_id, s.amount, GETDATE())
WHEN NOT MATCHED BY SOURCE THEN
    DELETE;  -- rows in target not in source → delete

The WHEN NOT MATCHED BY SOURCE THEN DELETE clause goes beyond UPSERT — it's a full sync that removes rows from target that no longer exist in source. MERGE is atomic — either all three operations succeed or none do.

7
What are window functions in SQL and when would you use them?
ConceptWindow Functions
▼

A window function performs a calculation across a set of rows related to the current row — without collapsing them into a single output row like GROUP BY does. The "window" is defined by OVER (PARTITION BY ... ORDER BY ...).

Use window functions when you need both the individual row AND an aggregation — e.g., "show each employee's salary alongside their department average."

  • Ranking: ROW_NUMBER(), RANK(), DENSE_RANK() — rank rows within a group.
  • Running totals / moving averages: SUM() OVER, AVG() OVER with ORDER BY.
  • Lead/Lag: LAG(col, 1) — previous row's value; LEAD(col, 1) — next row's value. Great for month-over-month comparisons.
  • NTILE(n): Divide rows into n equal buckets (e.g., quartiles, deciles).
  • FIRST_VALUE / LAST_VALUE: First or last value in the window frame.
🗄 SQL — Window Function Examples
SELECT employee_id, department, salary,
  -- Rank within department
  RANK() OVER (PARTITION BY department ORDER BY salary DESC) AS dept_rank,
  -- Running total per department
  SUM(salary) OVER (PARTITION BY department ORDER BY employee_id
                  ROWS BETWEEN UNBOUNDED PRECEDING AND CURRENT ROW) AS running_total,
  -- Department average (no GROUP BY needed)
  AVG(salary) OVER (PARTITION BY department) AS dept_avg,
  -- Previous employee's salary
  LAG(salary, 1) OVER (PARTITION BY department ORDER BY salary DESC) AS prev_salary
FROM employees;
🐍 PySpark
from pyspark.sql.functions import (rank, avg, sum as _sum, lag, col)
from pyspark.sql.window import Window

dept_win  = Window.partitionBy("department")
order_win = Window.partitionBy("department").orderBy(col("salary").desc())
running   = Window.partitionBy("department").orderBy("employee_id") \
                  .rowsBetween(Window.unboundedPreceding, Window.currentRow)

df.select(
  "employee_id", "department", "salary",
  rank().over(order_win).alias("dept_rank"),
  _sum("salary").over(running).alias("running_total"),
  avg("salary").over(dept_win).alias("dept_avg"),
  lag("salary", 1).over(order_win).alias("prev_salary")
).show()
8
Normalization vs Denormalization — what's the difference and when do you use each?
Data ModellingFundamentals
▼

"These are opposite design philosophies, and the right choice depends entirely on what the table is used for — transactional writes or analytical reads."

Normalization: Breaking data into multiple related tables to eliminate redundancy. Each piece of information is stored in exactly one place. Following 1NF, 2NF, 3NF rules — atomic columns, no partial dependencies, no transitive dependencies.

  • Example: Instead of storing customer name and address directly in every order row, you store a customer_id in the orders table and keep name/address in a separate customers table.
  • Advantages: No data duplication, smaller storage footprint, no update anomalies — change a customer's address once, it's correct everywhere. Strong data integrity through foreign keys.
  • Disadvantages: Requires JOINs to get a complete picture — more joins means more query complexity and slower reads on large datasets.
  • Best for: OLTP systems — banking, order management, CRM — where you're doing frequent small writes and need strict consistency.

Denormalization: Intentionally introducing redundancy by combining related data into fewer, wider tables — to avoid joins and speed up reads.

  • Example: A Gold layer reporting table that includes customer name and address directly alongside every order row, even though that duplicates the same name/address across thousands of orders.
  • Advantages: Much faster reads — no joins needed, which matters a lot when scanning billions of rows for a dashboard. Simpler queries for BI tools and analysts.
  • Disadvantages: Storage redundancy, and update anomalies — if a customer's address changes, you'd need to update it across every duplicated row. Harder to maintain consistency.
  • Best for: OLAP/analytical systems — data warehouses, BI dashboards, Star Schema fact/dimension tables, Gold layer in a lakehouse.
💡
In my lakehouse projects, this maps directly to layers: Silver layer is normalized — clean, structured, source-of-truth, minimal redundancy. Gold layer is denormalized — pre-joined, flattened, optimized for fast BI reads. I normalize where I write, and denormalize where I read.
9
Explain LEAD, LAG, ROW_NUMBER, RANK & DENSE_RANK
▼

"All five of these are window functions — they run a calculation across a set of rows related to the current row, without collapsing rows like GROUP BY does. Each row keeps its own identity in the result."

1. ROW_NUMBER()

Assigns a unique sequential number to every row — no ties, no gaps. Even if two rows have identical values, they get different numbers. The order is determined by the ORDER BY inside the window.

🗄 SQL — Window Function Examples
SELECT
  employee_id, name, salary, department_id,
  ROW_NUMBER() OVER (PARTITION BY department_id ORDER BY salary DESC) AS row_num
FROM employees;

Use case: Picking exactly the top 1 row per group — like the highest paid employee per department. Since it never ties, you're always guaranteed one row per rank position.

2. RANK()

Assigns a rank based on ORDER BY — but if two rows tie, they get the same rank, and the next rank skips accordingly. So if two people are both rank 1, the next rank is 3 (not 2) — rank 2 is skipped.

🗄 SQL — Window Function Examples
SELECT
  employee_id, name, salary,
  RANK() OVER (PARTITION BY department_id ORDER BY salary DESC) AS rnk
FROM employees;

-- Result example:
-- Salary 9000 → Rank 1
-- Salary 9000 → Rank 1  (tie)
-- Salary 8000 → Rank 3  (rank 2 is SKIPPED)

Use case: Competition-style ranking where tied positions leave a gap — like sports standings. "There were two 1st place finishers, so there's no 2nd place."

3. DENSE_RANK()

Same as RANK but no gaps. Tied rows get the same rank, and the next rank is always consecutive — rank 1, 1, 2, 3 (not 1, 1, 3).

🗄 SQL — Window Function Examples
SELECT
  employee_id, name, salary,
  DENSE_RANK() OVER (PARTITION BY department_id ORDER BY salary DESC) AS dense_rnk
FROM employees;

-- Result example:
-- Salary 9000 → Dense Rank 1
-- Salary 9000 → Dense Rank 1  (tie)
-- Salary 8000 → Dense Rank 2  (NO gap — continues from 1)

Use case: When you need true rank levels without gaps — like "show me all employees at rank 2 salary level." With RANK, rank 2 might not exist; with DENSE_RANK, rank 2 always means "the second distinct salary value."

Comparison — salary 9000, 9000, 8000, 7000
ROW_NUMBER()
9000 → 1
9000 → 2
8000 → 3
7000 → 4
Always unique, no ties
RANK()
9000 → 1
9000 → 1
8000 → 3
7000 → 4
Ties allowed, gaps after tie
DENSE_RANK()
9000 → 1
9000 → 1
8000 → 2
7000 → 3
Ties allowed, no gaps

4. LAG()

Looks backwards — fetches the value from a previous row in the window. By default it looks 1 row back, but you can specify any offset.

🗄 SQL — Window Function Examples
SELECT
  sale_date, product_id, revenue,
  LAG(revenue, 1, 0) OVER (PARTITION BY product_id ORDER BY sale_date) AS prev_day_revenue,
  revenue - LAG(revenue, 1, 0) OVER (PARTITION BY product_id ORDER BY sale_date) AS revenue_change
FROM daily_sales;
-- LAG(column, offset, default_if_null)

Use case: Day-over-day comparisons, calculating the difference between current and previous value, detecting when a value changed from the last row. I've used this to calculate month-over-month revenue growth per product.

5. LEAD()

The opposite of LAG — looks forward and fetches the value from a future row. Same syntax, just looks ahead instead of behind.

🗄 SQL — Window Function Examples
SELECT
  employee_id, name, salary,
  LEAD(salary, 1) OVER (PARTITION BY department_id ORDER BY salary DESC) AS next_lower_salary,
  salary - LEAD(salary, 1, 0) OVER (PARTITION BY department_id ORDER BY salary DESC) AS salary_gap
FROM employees;

Use case: Calculating how far ahead the current row is from the next — like salary gap analysis, predicting next period's value, or finding the next event's timestamp in a sequence. Also useful for consecutive record analysis — comparing current login date against the next login date to find gaps.

💡
Quick way to remember: LAG = look back (previous row). LEAD = look ahead (next row). ROW_NUMBER = always 1,2,3,4 no matter what. RANK = gives same number to ties, then skips. DENSE_RANK = gives same number to ties, never skips. In interviews, the most commonly tested combination is ROW_NUMBER vs RANK vs DENSE_RANK — the colour table above is the fastest way to explain the difference.
10
How do you handle NULL values in SQL and PySpark?
▼

"NULL means the absence of a value — not zero, not empty string, just unknown. That distinction matters a lot because NULL behaves differently from any other value in comparisons and aggregations, and if you don't handle it explicitly, it can silently corrupt results."

Key things to understand about NULL first:

  • NULL = NULL returns NULL (not true) — you can never equality-check a NULL. Always use IS NULL or IS NOT NULL.
  • Any arithmetic with NULL returns NULL — 100 + NULL = NULL. So if a revenue column has NULLs and you SUM it, those rows are silently skipped.
  • NULL in a GROUP BY is treated as its own group — all NULL values get grouped together.
  • NULLs are excluded from aggregate functions like COUNT(column), SUM, AVG — but COUNT(*) counts them.

In SQL — key functions:

🗄 SQL — Window Function Examples
-- 1. Check for NULLs
SELECT * FROM orders WHERE amount IS NULL;
SELECT * FROM orders WHERE amount IS NOT NULL;

-- 2. COALESCE — returns first non-NULL value
-- Most used. Replace NULL with a default.
SELECT
  customer_id,
  COALESCE(phone, email, 'No contact') AS contact
FROM customers;

-- 3. ISNULL (SQL Server / Synapse) — simpler 2-arg version of COALESCE
SELECT ISNULL(amount, 0) AS amount FROM orders;

-- 4. NULLIF — returns NULL if two values are equal
-- Useful to avoid division by zero
SELECT
  total_revenue / NULLIF(total_units, 0) AS avg_price
FROM sales;
-- If total_units = 0, NULLIF returns NULL
-- so we get NULL instead of a division-by-zero error

-- 5. CASE WHEN for complex logic
SELECT
  order_id,
  CASE
    WHEN discount IS NULL THEN 'No discount applied'
    WHEN discount = 0    THEN 'Zero discount'
    ELSE CAST(discount AS VARCHAR)
  END AS discount_label
FROM orders;

-- 6. NULL in aggregations
SELECT
  COUNT(*)          AS total_rows,       -- counts NULLs
  COUNT(phone)      AS rows_with_phone,  -- skips NULLs
  AVG(salary)       AS avg_salary,       -- skips NULLs in avg calc
  SUM(COALESCE(amount, 0)) AS total_amount  -- treat NULL as 0
FROM employees;

In PySpark — key functions:

🐍 PySpark
from pyspark.sql.functions import (
  col, isnull, isnan, coalesce, lit, when, count, sum as _sum)

# 1. Check for NULLs
df.filter(col("amount").isNull()).show()
df.filter(col("amount").isNotNull()).show()

# 2. Count NULLs per column — quality check
from pyspark.sql.functions import count, when
df.select([
  count(when(col(c).isNull(), c)).alias(c)
  for c in df.columns]).show()

# 3. Fill NULLs — fillna
df.fillna(0)                          # fill ALL numeric NULLs with 0
df.fillna({"amount": 0, "name": "Unknown"})  # per column

# 4. COALESCE — first non-null across multiple columns
df.withColumn("contact", coalesce(col("phone"), col("email"), lit("No contact")))

# 5. Drop rows with NULLs
df.dropna()                        # drop if ANY column is NULL
df.dropna(subset=["customer_id"])  # drop only if this column is NULL
df.dropna(thresh=3)                # drop if fewer than 3 non-null values

# 6. Replace with WHEN / OTHERWISE — conditional null handling
df.withColumn(
  "discount_label",
  when(col("discount").isNull(), "No discount")
  .when(col("discount") == 0, "Zero discount")
  .otherwise(col("discount").cast("string"))
)

# 7. Avoid division by zero (equivalent of NULLIF)
df.withColumn("avg_price",
  when(col("total_units") == 0, None)
  .otherwise(col("total_revenue") / col("total_units")))

In production pipelines — how I handle NULLs:

  • At Bronze layer: Accept NULLs as-is — don't change raw data. Just log null counts per column as a quality metric so we can track if NULL rates change over time (a spike in NULLs usually means a source system issue).
  • At Silver layer: Enforce NULL rules based on business requirements. Primary keys and join keys must be non-null — I assert this and fail the pipeline loudly if violated. For optional fields, I fill NULLs with sensible defaults (0 for amounts, "Unknown" for categories) or leave them as NULL if "no value" is genuinely meaningful.
  • At Gold layer: All NULLs should be resolved before serving to BI. Reports with NULL amounts or NULL dimension values confuse analysts and break aggregations silently.
⚠️
Most common NULL trap: AVG(salary) silently skips NULL rows — so if 20% of salaries are NULL, your average is calculated on only 80% of employees and looks artificially high. Always check null counts before trusting any aggregate result.
💡
Good line for interviews: "I treat NULLs as a data quality signal, not just a value to replace. Before I fill or drop NULLs, I ask — why is this NULL? Is it missing data, optional field, or a source system bug? The answer changes what the right handling is."
11
Slow query — troubleshooting & Handling duplicate data from source in a pipeline
OptimizationPipeline Design
▼

Slow query — troubleshooting order:

  1. Run EXPLAIN / EXPLAIN ANALYZE — find the most expensive node (full table scan, sort, nested loop join).
  2. Check for missing indexes on filter and join columns.
  3. Check statistics are fresh — stale stats lead to bad execution plans. Run UPDATE STATISTICS or ANALYZE.
  4. Rewrite predicate — remove functions on indexed columns, use range conditions instead of BETWEEN on dates.
  5. Check JOIN order — filter the smallest set first before joining large tables.
  6. Add covering index if the query selects few columns from a heavily filtered table.
  7. Partition pruning — ensure the query's WHERE clause aligns with partition keys in Synapse or Delta.
  8. In Synapse: check for data skew with DBCC PDW_SHOWSPACEUSED, update statistics, add BROADCAST hints on small tables.

Handling duplicate data from source in a pipeline:

  • At ingestion (Bronze): Load raw data as-is — never deduplicate at Bronze. Log the duplicate count as an audit metric.
  • At Silver (cleansing): Deduplicate using ROW_NUMBER() or dropDuplicates() on natural keys. Define a clear "winner" rule (latest timestamp, highest sequence number).
  • Use Delta MERGE into Silver/Gold with WHEN MATCHED THEN UPDATE — naturally idempotent, handles re-delivered records.
  • Add a data quality check after dedup — assert uniqueness on the key column and alert if violation detected.
  • Alert source team — duplicate data from source is a data contract violation. Raise it so the root cause is fixed upstream.
🔒
Security & Secrets Management
Key Vault, Managed Identity, Column/Row-Level Security, schema evolution
1
How do you use Azure Key Vault and Managed Identities to eliminate hardcoded credentials?
Security
▼

Azure Key Vault stores all secrets (passwords, connection strings, API keys) securely. Access is controlled via RBAC.

Managed Identity (MI) is an Azure AD identity automatically assigned to an Azure resource (ADF, Databricks, App Service). The identity has no password — Azure manages the credentials internally. You grant this identity access to Key Vault, and the resource authenticates using its MI, fetches the secret, and uses it — all without any hardcoded credentials.

  • In ADF: ADF has a System-Assigned MI automatically. Grant it the "Key Vault Secrets User" role. In Linked Service, reference: @Microsoft.KeyVault(VaultName=myVault;SecretName=sqlPassword). ADF fetches the secret at runtime — no password in ADF config.
  • In Databricks: Create a Databricks Secret Scope backed by Azure Key Vault. In notebooks: dbutils.secrets.get(scope="kv-scope", key="storage-key"). The secret is never printed in logs or visible in notebook output.
  • For ADLS access: Configure Spark session to use OAuth with MsiTokenProvider. Grant the Databricks cluster's MI the "Storage Blob Data Contributor" role on ADLS. No storage keys needed anywhere.
2
What is the role of Managed Identity in Azure, and how do you use it in ADF or Databricks?
▼

"Managed Identity is Azure's way of giving a service its own identity in Azure Active Directory — so it can authenticate to other Azure resources without needing a username, password, or connection string stored anywhere. The identity is managed by Azure itself, so there's nothing to rotate, nothing to leak."

Two types:

  • System-assigned: Tied to a specific resource — when you delete the resource, the identity is also deleted. One-to-one relationship. This is what ADF and Databricks use by default.
  • User-assigned: Created independently and can be attached to multiple resources. Use this when you want the same identity shared across multiple services — like multiple ADF instances all accessing the same Key Vault.

How I use it in ADF:

  • When ADF connects to ADLS Gen2, instead of storing an access key or SAS token in the Linked Service, I select "Managed Identity" as the authentication method. ADF's system-assigned identity authenticates directly to ADLS via AAD token — no secret stored anywhere.
  • For the identity to work, I assign the ADF Managed Identity the Storage Blob Data Contributor role on the ADLS account via IAM. That's the only setup needed.
  • Same pattern for Key Vault — give ADF's Managed Identity "Key Vault Secrets User" role, and now ADF can read secrets from Key Vault without storing any credentials.

How I use it in Databricks:

  • Databricks clusters can be assigned a Managed Identity via the Instance Profile (on AWS) or the Access Connector for Azure Databricks. The cluster's identity is then granted roles on ADLS, Key Vault, and other Azure services.
  • In Unity Catalog, Storage Credentials are defined using a Managed Identity — Databricks uses it to access external ADLS locations without any key in the notebook code.
🐍 PySpark
# In Databricks — with Managed Identity via Unity Catalog Storage Credential
# No keys, no secrets in code — identity handles auth at the platform level
df = spark.read.format("delta").load("abfss://container@account.dfs.core.windows.net/path")

# In ADF Linked Service (JSON config)
# authType: ManagedServiceIdentity
# No accountKey or sasToken fields needed
💡
"Managed Identity is the Azure-native answer to 'how do we stop putting secrets in config files?' It eliminates secret rotation headaches, reduces attack surface, and is the recommended authentication pattern for any Azure service-to-service communication."
3
How do you manage secrets securely in a production pipeline?
Best Practice
▼
  • Never hardcode secrets in notebooks, config files, or pipeline definitions — even temporarily. They end up in version control and are extremely difficult to fully remove from git history.
  • Key Vault for all secrets. Rotate secrets in Key Vault without touching your code — services fetch the latest value on the next run automatically.
  • Prefer Managed Identity over Service Principals over passwords. MIs have no credentials to leak. SPs have client secrets that expire and must be rotated. SQL passwords are the least secure option.
  • Databricks secret scopes: dbutils.secrets.get() — Databricks automatically redacts these values from notebook output and cluster logs.
  • Environment-specific secrets: Separate Key Vaults (or secret versions) per environment — dev, staging, prod. Pipelines reference the environment-appropriate vault via a parameter.
  • Audit Key Vault access: Enable diagnostic logs → Log Analytics. Alert on unexpected access patterns or access from unexpected principals.
  • Least privilege: Grant each service identity only the specific secrets it needs — not blanket "Get All Secrets" on the entire vault.
4
How do you implement Column Level Security and Row Level Security in a Lakehouse?
Security
▼

Column-Level Security (CLS): Prevent specific users or roles from reading specific columns entirely.

-- Synapse Dedicated Pool
DENY SELECT ON dbo.customers(ssn, credit_card) TO analyst_role;
GRANT SELECT ON dbo.customers(customer_id, name, email) TO analyst_role;

-- Databricks Unity Catalog
GRANT SELECT ON TABLE customers TO analyst_role;
REVOKE SELECT (ssn, credit_card) ON TABLE customers FROM analyst_role;

Row-Level Security (RLS): Restrict which rows a user can see based on their identity. In Synapse, create a security predicate function that evaluates the current user's access context, then bind it to the table as a SECURITY POLICY with FILTER PREDICATE — every query on the table automatically runs through the predicate.

In Databricks Unity Catalog: Define a column masking function and attach it to a column. Any query on that column runs through the masking function based on the caller's group membership — fine-grained, centrally managed access control across all tools querying through Unity Catalog.

5
How do you handle Schema Evolution without breaking downstream Power BI reports?
Architecture
▼

Core principle: insulate consumers from upstream schema changes using a stable interface layer.

  • Views as a contract layer: Power BI connects to a view (Synapse serverless view or Databricks SQL view), not directly to the Delta table. When upstream schema changes, update the view to maintain the same output columns — Power BI sees no change.
  • Semantic model isolation: In Power BI, use a semantic model (dataset) as the interface. Reports connect to the model, not the underlying source directly. Update the model's queries when schema changes, without touching any reports.
  • Additive changes are safe: Adding new columns to a Delta table doesn't break existing queries. Treat column removal or renaming as breaking changes requiring a deprecation period.
  • Column aliasing: If a source column is renamed, add an alias in the view to maintain backward compatibility during the transition period.
  • Schema change alerts: Add a validation step in your pipeline that compares the incoming schema to the expected schema and sends an alert before writing — giving you time to update the interface layer before new schema lands in production.
6
How would you implement data masking in Azure SQL for sensitive columns?
Security
▼

Dynamic Data Masking (DDM) is applied at the column definition level. Users without the UNMASK permission see masked values. Data is not changed on disk — masking is applied at query time.

🗄 SQL
-- Add masking to existing columns
ALTER TABLE customers ALTER COLUMN email
    ADD MASKED WITH (FUNCTION = 'email()');            -- aXXX@XXXX.com

ALTER TABLE customers ALTER COLUMN phone
    ADD MASKED WITH (FUNCTION = 'partial(0,"XXX-XXX-",4)'); -- XXX-XXX-1234

ALTER TABLE customers ALTER COLUMN salary
    ADD MASKED WITH (FUNCTION = 'default()');          -- 0 for numbers

-- Grant unmask to privileged role
GRANT UNMASK ON customers TO data_engineer_role;
⚠️
DDM does not protect against WHERE clause inference attacks (WHERE salary = 75000 still works). Combine with Column-Level Security (DENY SELECT on specific columns) for complete protection.
💻
Coding — SQL & PySpark
Real interview problems grouped by concept. Each problem shows SQL + PySpark solution side-by-side.
🧹 Set 1 — Duplicate Detection & Removal
1
Find duplicate records and remove them efficiently — keep the latest by timestamp
DedupWindow Function
▼
Table: customers — columns: customer_id, name, email, updated_at (TIMESTAMP)

Strategy: Use ROW_NUMBER() partitioned by the natural key, ordered by timestamp DESC. Row 1 = latest. Delete all rows where rn > 1.

🗄 SQL
-- Step 1: Identify duplicates
WITH ranked AS (
  SELECT *,
    ROW_NUMBER() OVER (PARTITION BY customer_id ORDER BY updated_at DESC) AS rn
  FROM customers
)
-- Step 2: View duplicates
SELECT * FROM ranked WHERE rn > 1;

-- Step 3: Delete duplicates (keep latest)
DELETE FROM customers
WHERE id NOT IN (
  SELECT MIN(id)   -- keep one record per group
  FROM (
    SELECT id,
      ROW_NUMBER() OVER (PARTITION BY customer_id ORDER BY updated_at DESC) AS rn
    FROM customers
  ) t
  WHERE rn = 1
);

-- Alternative (faster on large tables):
WITH ranked AS (
  SELECT id,
    ROW_NUMBER() OVER (PARTITION BY customer_id ORDER BY updated_at DESC) AS rn
  FROM customers
)
DELETE FROM customers
WHERE id IN (SELECT id FROM ranked WHERE rn > 1);
🐍 PySpark
from pyspark.sql import SparkSession
from pyspark.sql.functions import col, row_number
from pyspark.sql.window import Window

spark = SparkSession.builder.getOrCreate()

df = spark.read.parquet("/mnt/data/customers")

# Define window: partition by business key, latest first
window = Window.partitionBy("customer_id").orderBy(col("updated_at").desc())

# Assign row numbers
ranked = df.withColumn("rn", row_number().over(window))

# Keep only the latest record per customer_id
deduped = ranked.filter(col("rn") == 1).drop("rn")

# Write back
deduped.write.mode("overwrite").format("delta").save("/mnt/delta/customers_clean")

# Verify
print(f"Before: {df.count()}, After: {deduped.count()}")
💡
In Delta Lake, use MERGE INTO with a unique key instead of delete + reinsert — it's atomic and handles concurrent writes safely. Always check counts before and after dedup.
2
Detect duplicate records based on multiple columns (e.g., name + email + phone)
DedupMulti-column
▼
Table: records — columns: id, first_name, last_name, email, phone, created_at
🗄 SQL
-- Find groups with duplicates
SELECT first_name, last_name, email,
       COUNT(*) AS cnt
FROM records
GROUP BY first_name, last_name, email
HAVING COUNT(*) > 1;

-- Tag each duplicate with a row number
WITH ranked AS (
  SELECT *,
    ROW_NUMBER() OVER (PARTITION BY first_name, last_name, email ORDER BY created_at DESC) AS rn
  FROM records
)
-- See all duplicates
SELECT * FROM ranked WHERE rn > 1;

-- Remove duplicates (keep latest per group)
WITH ranked AS (
  SELECT id,
    ROW_NUMBER() OVER (PARTITION BY first_name, last_name, email ORDER BY created_at DESC) AS rn
  FROM records
)
DELETE FROM records
WHERE id IN (SELECT id FROM ranked WHERE rn > 1);
🐍 PySpark
from pyspark.sql.functions import col, row_number, count
from pyspark.sql.window import Window

df = spark.read.parquet("/mnt/data/records")

# --- Approach 1: dropDuplicates (simple, no tie-breaking) ---
df_simple = df.dropDuplicates(["first_name", "last_name", "email"])

# --- Approach 2: Window with tie-breaking (recommended) ---
key_cols = ["first_name", "last_name", "email"]
win = Window.partitionBy(*key_cols).orderBy(col("created_at").desc())

deduped = (df.withColumn("rn", row_number().over(win))
             .filter(col("rn") == 1).drop("rn"))

# --- Approach 3: Show duplicate groups ---
dups = (df.groupBy(*key_cols).agg(count("*").alias("cnt"))
          .filter(col("cnt") > 1))
dups.show()
3
Remove duplicates from a table without using DISTINCT
SQLDedup
▼

DISTINCT works but forces a full sort. For large tables, ROW_NUMBER() with a CTE is more efficient and gives you control over which duplicate to keep.

🗄 SQL — 3 approaches
-- Method 1: ROW_NUMBER (preferred — keeps latest)
WITH cte AS (
  SELECT *,
    ROW_NUMBER() OVER (PARTITION BY employee_id ORDER BY updated_at DESC) AS rn
  FROM employees
)
DELETE FROM employees
WHERE id IN (SELECT id FROM cte WHERE rn > 1);

-- Method 2: Self-join (keep min rowid)
DELETE e1 FROM employees e1
INNER JOIN employees e2
  ON e1.employee_id = e2.employee_id
  AND e1.id > e2.id;  -- keep the lower id

-- Method 3: INSERT INTO new table + rename
SELECT DISTINCT * INTO employees_clean
FROM employees;

DROP TABLE employees;
EXEC sp_rename 'employees_clean', 'employees';
🐍 PySpark
from pyspark.sql.functions import col, row_number
from pyspark.sql.window import Window

df = spark.read.format("delta").load("/mnt/delta/employees")

# ROW_NUMBER approach — equivalent to SQL Method 1
win = Window.partitionBy("employee_id").orderBy(col("updated_at").desc())

clean = (df.withColumn("rn", row_number().over(win)).filter("rn = 1").drop("rn"))

# Overwrite the Delta table
clean.write.format("delta") \
    .mode("overwrite") \
    .option("overwriteSchema", "false") \
    .save("/mnt/delta/employees")
4
Approach to identify duplicate records using multiple columns.
Code
▼
🗄 SQL
-- Method 1: Find groups with duplicates
SELECT order_id, customer_id, product_id, COUNT(*) AS duplicate_count
FROM orders
GROUP BY order_id, customer_id, product_id
HAVING COUNT(*) > 1;

-- Method 2: ROW_NUMBER to tag and inspect duplicates
WITH ranked AS (
    SELECT *,
        ROW_NUMBER() OVER (PARTITION BY order_id, customer_id, product_id
            ORDER BY created_at DESC  -- keep the latest) AS rn
    FROM orders
)
SELECT * FROM ranked WHERE rn > 1;  -- these are the duplicates

-- Method 3: Delete duplicates, keep the latest
WITH ranked AS (
    SELECT id,
        ROW_NUMBER() OVER (PARTITION BY order_id, customer_id, product_id
            ORDER BY created_at DESC) AS rn
    FROM orders
)
DELETE FROM orders WHERE id IN (SELECT id FROM ranked WHERE rn > 1);
5
How would you remove duplicate records from a huge PySpark DataFrame efficiently?
▼

"On a huge DataFrame, the approach matters a lot — naive deduplication causes massive shuffles and can OOM. I choose the method based on what 'duplicate' means for that dataset."

🐍 PySpark
from pyspark.sql.functions import row_number, col, count
from pyspark.sql.window import Window

# ── Method 1: dropDuplicates — simplest ───────────────
# Keeps one row per duplicate group, arbitrary which one
df.dropDuplicates()                            # all columns must match
df.dropDuplicates(["order_id"])                # dedupe on specific key
df.dropDuplicates(["order_id", "product_id"]) # composite key

# Limitation: you can't control WHICH duplicate to keep (first, latest etc.)

# ── Method 2: Window ROW_NUMBER — most control ────────
# Keep the LATEST record per order_id (most recent updated_at)
window = Window.partitionBy("order_id").orderBy(col("updated_at").desc())

deduped = (df.withColumn("rn", row_number().over(window))
             .filter(col("rn") == 1)
             .drop("rn"))
# Best when: you need to keep a specific version (latest, earliest, highest)
# Trade-off: requires a shuffle to partition + sort by key

# ── Method 3: groupBy + agg — for aggregation-style dedup ──
from pyspark.sql.functions import max as _max, first

deduped = (df
    .groupBy("order_id")
    .agg(_max("updated_at").alias("updated_at"),
        first("customer_id").alias("customer_id"),
        first("amount").alias("amount")))
# Best when: you want to aggregate values across duplicates
# Trade-off: need to list every column explicitly

# ── Method 4: Delta MERGE — for dedup at write time ───
# Best for streaming — prevents duplicates from ever landing
delta_table.alias("t").merge(
    df.alias("s"),
    "t.order_id = s.order_id"
).whenMatchedUpdateAll() \
 .whenNotMatchedInsertAll() \
 .execute()

# ── Performance tip — filter before dedup ─────────────
# If duplicates only exist in recent data, partition-filter first
# to avoid shuffling the entire historical dataset
recent = df.filter(col("process_date") >= "2024-01-01")
historical = df.filter(col("process_date") < "2024-01-01")

deduped_recent = recent.dropDuplicates(["order_id"])
final = historical.union(deduped_recent)
💡
On truly huge DataFrames (billions of rows), deduplication is one of the most expensive operations because it requires a full shuffle. Always ask: "Can I narrow the dedup scope?" — deduplicating only today's partition is 100x cheaper than deduplicating the entire 5-year history table.
📊 Set 2 — Nth Salary
6
Top 3 highest-paid employees in each department
RankingWindow Function
▼
Table: employees — employee_id, name, department, salary
🗄 SQL
WITH ranked AS (
  SELECT
    employee_id, name, department, salary,
    DENSE_RANK() OVER (PARTITION BY department ORDER BY salary DESC) AS rnk
  FROM employees
)
SELECT employee_id, name, department, salary, rnk
FROM ranked
WHERE rnk <= 3
ORDER BY department, rnk;

-- Note: Use DENSE_RANK (not RANK) so ties don't
-- skip positions. If two people share rank 2,
-- RANK gives 2,2,4 — DENSE_RANK gives 2,2,3.
🐍 PySpark
from pyspark.sql.functions import dense_rank, col
from pyspark.sql.window import Window

win = Window.partitionBy("department").orderBy(col("salary").desc())

result = (df.withColumn("rnk", dense_rank().over(win))
            .filter(col("rnk") <= 3)
            .select("employee_id","name","department","salary","rnk")
            .orderBy("department", "rnk"))
result.show()
7
Second highest salary in each department
RankingWindow Function
▼
Table: employees — employee_id, name, department, salary
🗄 SQL
-- Method 1: DENSE_RANK (handles ties correctly)
WITH ranked AS (
  SELECT
    employee_id, name, department, salary,
    DENSE_RANK() OVER (PARTITION BY department ORDER BY salary DESC) AS rnk
  FROM employees
)
SELECT * FROM ranked WHERE rnk = 2;

-- Method 2: Subquery (no window function)
SELECT department,
  MAX(salary) AS second_highest
FROM employees
WHERE salary < (
  SELECT MAX(salary) FROM employees e2
  WHERE e2.department = employees.department
)
GROUP BY department;

-- Method 3: LEAD (peek at next row's salary)
WITH ordered AS (
  SELECT DISTINCT department, salary,
    LEAD(salary) OVER (PARTITION BY department ORDER BY salary DESC) AS next_salary
  FROM employees
)
SELECT department, next_salary AS second_highest
FROM ordered
WHERE next_salary IS NOT NULL
  AND salary = (SELECT MAX(salary)
                FROM employees e
                WHERE e.department = ordered.department);
🐍 PySpark
from pyspark.sql.functions import dense_rank, col
from pyspark.sql.window import Window


win = Window.partitionBy("department").orderBy(col("salary").desc())

result = (df.withColumn("rnk", dense_rank().over(win))
            .filter(col("rnk") == 2)
            .select("employee_id","name","department","salary"))
result.show()

# Alternative: filter salary < max, then take max
from pyspark.sql.functions import max as _max

dept_max = df.groupBy("department").agg(_max("salary").alias("max_sal"))

second = (df.join(dept_max, "department")
            .filter(col("salary") < col("max_sal"))
            .groupBy("department")
            .agg(_max("salary").alias("second_highest")))
second.show()
8
Find employees whose salary is higher than the average salary of their department
Window FunctionAggregation
▼
🗄 SQL
-- Method 1: Window function (clean, no subquery)
SELECT employee_id, name, department, salary,
  AVG(salary) OVER (PARTITION BY department) AS dept_avg
FROM employees
WHERE salary > AVG(salary) OVER (PARTITION BY department);
-- NOTE: WHERE can't use window functions directly.
-- Wrap in a CTE:

WITH avgs AS (
  SELECT *,
    AVG(salary) OVER (PARTITION BY department) AS dept_avg
  FROM employees
)
SELECT employee_id, name, department,
       salary, ROUND(dept_avg,2) AS dept_avg
FROM avgs
WHERE salary > dept_avg
ORDER BY department, salary DESC;

-- Method 2: Correlated subquery
SELECT employee_id, name, department, salary
FROM employees e1
WHERE salary > (
  SELECT AVG(salary)
  FROM employees e2
  WHERE e2.department = e1.department);
🐍 PySpark
from pyspark.sql.functions import avg, col
from pyspark.sql.window import Window

dept_win = Window.partitionBy("department")

result = (df.withColumn("dept_avg", avg("salary").over(dept_win))
            .filter(col("salary") > col("dept_avg"))
            .select("employee_id","name","department","salary","dept_avg")
            .orderBy("department", col("salary").desc()))
result.show()
9
Find employees whose salary is greater than the average salary of ALL OTHER departments. Exclude comparison with their own department.
SQLWindow Function
▼
employees: employee_id, name, department_id, salary

The key here is "average salary of ALL OTHER departments" — meaning for each employee, you calculate the average salary of everyone not in their department, then compare.

🗄 SQL
-- Step 1: get total sum and count across ALL employees
-- Step 2: subtract current dept's contribution to get "other depts" average

WITH dept_stats AS (
    SELECT
        department_id,
        SUM(salary)   AS dept_sum,
        COUNT(*)      AS dept_count
    FROM employees
    GROUP BY department_id
),
totals AS (
    SELECT
        SUM(salary) AS total_sum,
        COUNT(*)    AS total_count
    FROM employees
)
SELECT
    e.employee_id, e.name, e.department_id, e.salary,
    ROUND((t.total_sum  - d.dept_sum) / (t.total_count - d.dept_count), 2) AS avg_other_depts
FROM employees e
JOIN dept_stats d ON e.department_id = d.department_id
CROSS JOIN totals t
WHERE e.salary > (t.total_sum  - d.dept_sum) / (t.total_count - d.dept_count)
ORDER BY e.department_id, e.salary DESC;
🐍 PySpark
from pyspark.sql.functions import (sum as _sum, count, col, round as _round)

dept_stats = (df.groupBy("department_id")
                .agg(_sum("salary").alias("dept_sum"), 
                count("*").alias("dept_count")))

totals = df.agg(_sum("salary").alias("total_sum"), count("*").alias("total_count"))

result = (df.join(dept_stats, "department_id").crossJoin(totals)
            .withColumn("avg_other_depts", _round(
                (col("total_sum")   - col("dept_sum")) /
                (col("total_count") - col("dept_count")), 2))
            .filter(col("salary") > col("avg_other_depts"))
            .select("employee_id", "name", "department_id", "salary", "avg_other_depts")
            .orderBy("department_id", col("salary").desc()))
result.show()
💡
The trick is to avoid a correlated subquery that loops for every row. Instead, pre-aggregate department stats once, then do simple math: (total_sum − own_dept_sum) / (total_count − own_dept_count) = average of all other departments. Clean and efficient.
10
Rank the top 3 departments (with ≥10 employees) by % of staff earning more than 100K.
▼
employees: employee_id, department, salary
🗄 SQL
WITH dept_stats AS (
    SELECT
        department,
        COUNT(*)                                    AS total_employees,
        SUM(CASE WHEN salary > 100000 THEN 1 ELSE 0 END) AS above_100k
    FROM employees
    GROUP BY department
    HAVING COUNT(*) >= 10          -- only depts with 10+ employees
),
ranked AS (
    SELECT department, total_employees, above_100k,
        ROUND(100.0 * above_100k / total_employees, 2) AS pct_above_100k,
        RANK() OVER (ORDER BY 100.0 * above_100k / total_employees DESC) AS rnk
    FROM dept_stats
)
SELECT rnk, department, total_employees, above_100k, pct_above_100k
FROM ranked
WHERE rnk <= 3
ORDER BY rnk;
🐍 PySpark
from pyspark.sql.functions import (
    count, sum as _sum, when, col, round as _round, rank)
from pyspark.sql.window import Window

dept_stats = (df.groupBy("department")
                .agg(count("*").alias("total_employees"),
                _sum(when(col("salary") > 100000, 1).otherwise(0))
                .alias("above_100k"))
                .filter(col("total_employees") >= 10)
                .withColumn("pct_above_100k", _round(100.0 * col("above_100k") / col("total_employees"), 2)))

win = Window.orderBy(col("pct_above_100k").desc())

result = (dept_stats.withColumn("rnk", rank().over(win))
                    .filter(col("rnk") <= 3).orderBy("rnk"))
result.show()
💡
Key points: use HAVING COUNT(*) >= 10 (not WHERE) because the filter is on the aggregated count, not a raw column. Use RANK() not ROW_NUMBER() — if two departments tie on percentage, they should share the same rank.
📂 Set 3 — Consecutive Logins
11
Find the longest consecutive login streak for each employee.
Window FunctionAggregation
▼
🗄 SQL
WITH cte AS
(
SELECT emp_id,
       login_date,
       ROW_NUMBER() OVER(PARTITION BY emp_id ORDER BY login_date) rn
FROM employee_logins
)
SELECT emp_id,
       MIN(login_date) AS streak_start,
       MAX(login_date) AS streak_end,
       COUNT(*) AS streak_length
FROM
(
SELECT emp_id,
       login_date,
       DATEADD(day,-rn,login_date) grp
FROM cte
)x
GROUP BY emp_id,grp
ORDER BY emp_id,streak_start;

-- Find Employees With At Least 3 Consecutive Logins
-- Just Add ----> HAVING COUNT(*) >= 3

-- If They Ask Longest Streak
SELECT emp_id, MAX(streak_length)
FROM streaks
GROUP BY emp_id;
🐍 PySpark
from pyspark.sql.window import Window
from pyspark.sql.functions import row_number,col,date_sub,min,max,count

w = Window.partitionBy("emp_id").orderBy("login_date")
df1 = df.withColumn("rn",row_number().over(w))
df2 = df1.withColumn("grp",date_sub(col("login_date"),col("rn")))
result = df2.groupBy("emp_id","grp")
            .agg(min("login_date").alias("streak_start"),
            max("login_date").alias("streak_end"),count("*").alias("streak_length"))

# If They Ask Longest Streak
result.groupBy("emp_id").agg(max("streak_length").alias("longest_streak"))
12
Find consecutive login days for users.
Code
▼
💡
Key insight: if logins are consecutive, date - ROW_NUMBER() produces the same constant value for all days in a streak. That constant becomes the group key.
🗄 SQL
WITH deduped AS (
    -- Remove duplicate logins on the same day
    SELECT DISTINCT user_id, CAST(login_time AS DATE) AS login_date
    FROM user_logins
),
numbered AS (
    SELECT user_id, login_date,
        ROW_NUMBER() OVER (PARTITION BY user_id ORDER BY login_date) AS rn
    FROM deduped
),
grouped AS (
    SELECT user_id, login_date,
        DATEADD(DAY, -rn, login_date) AS streak_group  -- constant within a streak
    FROM numbered
)
SELECT
    user_id,
    MIN(login_date) AS streak_start,
    MAX(login_date) AS streak_end,
    COUNT(*) AS consecutive_days
FROM grouped
GROUP BY user_id, streak_group
HAVING COUNT(*) > 1  -- only streaks of 2+ days
ORDER BY consecutive_days DESC;
🐍 PySpark
from pyspark.sql import functions as F
from pyspark.sql.window import Window

# Step 1: Remove duplicate logins on the same day
deduped_df = (user_logins.select("user_id", F.to_date("login_time").alias("login_date")).distinct())

# Step 2: Assign row number per user ordered by login date
window_spec = Window.partitionBy("user_id").orderBy("login_date")

numbered_df = (deduped_df.withColumn("rn", F.row_number().over(window_spec)))

# Step 3: Create streak group
grouped_df = (numbered_df.withColumn("streak_group", F.date_sub(F.col("login_date"), F.col("rn"))))

# Step 4: Aggregate streaks
result_df = (grouped_df
            .groupBy("user_id", "streak_group")
            .agg(F.min("login_date").alias("streak_start"),
            F.max("login_date").alias("streak_end"),
            F.count("*").alias("consecutive_days"))
            .filter(F.col("consecutive_days") > 1)
            .orderBy(F.desc("consecutive_days")))

result_df.show(truncate=False)
13
Find consecutive login days for employees (the "streak" problem)
Consecutive RecordsWindow Function
▼
employee_logins: employee_id, login_date (DATE)

Key insight: for consecutive dates, login_date - ROW_NUMBER() gives the same constant value for all days in a streak. That constant is the "streak group key".

🗄 SQL
WITH deduped AS (
  -- Remove duplicate logins on same day
  SELECT DISTINCT employee_id,
    CAST(login_date AS DATE) AS login_date
  FROM employee_logins
),
numbered AS (
  SELECT employee_id, login_date,
    ROW_NUMBER() OVER (PARTITION BY employee_id ORDER BY login_date) AS rn
  FROM deduped
),
grouped AS (
  SELECT employee_id, login_date,
    -- For consecutive days, this value is constant
    DATEADD(DAY, -rn, login_date) AS streak_grp
  FROM numbered
)
SELECT
  employee_id,
  streak_grp,
  MIN(login_date) AS streak_start,
  MAX(login_date) AS streak_end,
  COUNT(*)        AS streak_length
FROM grouped
GROUP BY employee_id, streak_grp
HAVING COUNT(*) >= 2          -- only streaks of 2+ days
ORDER BY employee_id, streak_length DESC;

-- To find the LONGEST streak per employee:
