-- Wrap above in another CTE and use MAX(streak_length)
🐍 PySpark
from pyspark.sql.functions import (
  col, row_number, date_add, min as _min,
  max as _max, count, to_date, datediff, lit
)
from pyspark.sql.window import Window

df = spark.read.format("delta").load("/mnt/delta/logins")

# Step 1: Deduplicate (one login per employee per day)
deduped = df.select("employee_id", to_date("login_date").alias("login_date")).distinct()

# Step 2: Number rows per employee ordered by date
win = Window.partitionBy("employee_id").orderBy("login_date")
numbered = deduped.withColumn("rn", row_number().over(win))

# Step 3: Compute streak group = date - rn (days offset)
# Consecutive dates give the same offset → same group
from pyspark.sql.functions import expr
grouped = numbered.withColumn("streak_grp", expr("date_sub(login_date, rn)"))

# Step 4: Aggregate to find streaks
streaks = (grouped
  .groupBy("employee_id","streak_grp")
  .agg(
    _min("login_date").alias("streak_start"),
    _max("login_date").alias("streak_end"),
    count("*").alias("streak_length")
  )
  .filter(col("streak_length") >= 2)
  .orderBy("employee_id", col("streak_length").desc())
)
streaks.show()
14
Find the second most recent login for every user using Window functions
Window FunctionLAG / ROW_NUMBER
▼
🗄 SQL
-- Method 1: ROW_NUMBER (most readable)
WITH ranked AS (
  SELECT
    user_id, login_time,
    ROW_NUMBER() OVER (PARTITION BY user_id ORDER BY login_time DESC) AS rn
  FROM user_logins
)
SELECT user_id, login_time AS second_recent_login
FROM ranked WHERE rn = 2;

-- Method 2: LAG (elegant, one pass)
WITH with_prev AS (
  SELECT
    user_id, login_time,
    LAG(login_time, 1) OVER (PARTITION BY user_id ORDER BY login_time DESC) AS one_before  -- this is actually the 2nd most recent
  FROM user_logins
)
SELECT DISTINCT user_id,
  FIRST_VALUE(login_time) OVER (PARTITION BY user_id ORDER BY login_time ASC) AS second_recent_login
FROM with_prev
WHERE one_before IS NOT NULL;
-- Simpler: just use Method 1 (ROW_NUMBER = 2)
🐍 PySpark
from pyspark.sql.functions import row_number, col
from pyspark.sql.window import Window

win = Window.partitionBy("user_id").orderBy(col("login_time").desc())

result = (df.withColumn("rn", row_number().over(win))
            .filter(col("rn") == 2)
            .select("user_id", "login_time")
            .withColumnRenamed("login_time", "second_recent_login"))
result.show()
📈 Set 4 — Running Totals & Aggregations
15
Running total of salaries per department — and running total of sales per customer
Running TotalWindow Function
▼
Tables: employees(employee_id, name, department, salary, hire_date)  |  orders(order_id, customer_id, order_date, amount)
🗄 SQL — Running Total (department salaries)
-- Running total of salaries per department,
-- ordered by employee_id
SELECT
  employee_id, name, department, salary,
  SUM(salary) OVER (PARTITION BY department ORDER BY employee_id
              ROWS BETWEEN UNBOUNDED PRECEDING AND CURRENT ROW) AS running_salary_total
FROM employees
ORDER BY department, employee_id;

-- Running total of sales per customer
SELECT
  order_id, customer_id, order_date, amount,
  SUM(amount) OVER (PARTITION BY customer_id ORDER BY order_date
                ROWS BETWEEN UNBOUNDED PRECEDING AND CURRENT ROW) AS running_revenue
FROM orders
ORDER BY customer_id, order_date;
🐍 PySpark
from pyspark.sql.functions import sum as _sum, col
from pyspark.sql.window import Window

# Running total of salaries per department
dept_run = (Window.partitionBy("department").orderBy("employee_id")\
                  .rowsBetween(Window.unboundedPreceding, Window.currentRow))
emp_df.withColumn("running_salary_total",_sum("salary").over(dept_run)).show()

# Running total of sales per customer
cust_run = (Window.partitionBy("customer_id").orderBy("order_date")\
                  .rowsBetween(Window.unboundedPreceding, Window.currentRow))
orders_df.withColumn("running_revenue",_sum("amount").over(cust_run)).show()
16
Calculate total revenue per customer using Orders and Order_Items tables
Join + Aggregation
▼
orders: order_id, customer_id, order_date, status
order_items: item_id, order_id, product_id, quantity, unit_price
🗄 SQL
SELECT
  o.customer_id,
  SUM(oi.quantity * oi.unit_price) AS total_revenue,
  COUNT(DISTINCT o.order_id)       AS total_orders,
  MIN(o.order_date)                AS first_order,
  MAX(o.order_date)                AS latest_order
FROM orders o
JOIN order_items oi ON o.order_id = oi.order_id
WHERE o.status != 'CANCELLED'   -- exclude cancelled orders
GROUP BY o.customer_id
ORDER BY total_revenue DESC;
🐍 PySpark
from pyspark.sql.functions import (
  sum as _sum, count, countDistinct,
  col, min as _min, max as _max
)

orders = spark.read.format("delta").load("/mnt/delta/orders")
items  = spark.read.format("delta").load("/mnt/delta/order_items")

items = items.withColumn("line_total", col("quantity") * col("unit_price"))

result = (orders.filter(col("status") != "CANCELLED")
                .join(items, "order_id", "inner")
                .groupBy("customer_id")
              .agg(_sum("line_total").alias("total_revenue"),
                  countDistinct("order_id").alias("total_orders"),
                  _min("order_date").alias("first_order"),
                  _max("order_date").alias("latest_order"))
                  .orderBy(col("total_revenue").desc()))
result.show(20)
17
3-day moving average of units sold per product from a daily sales table
Moving AverageWindow Function
▼
daily_sales: sale_date (DATE), product_id, units_sold (INT)
🗄 SQL
SELECT
  sale_date,
  product_id,
  units_sold,
  AVG(units_sold) OVER (PARTITION BY product_id
    ORDER BY sale_date
    ROWS BETWEEN 2 PRECEDING AND CURRENT ROW
    -- "current row + 2 rows before it" = 3-day window
  ) AS moving_avg_3d,
  -- Also useful: rounded version
  ROUND(AVG(CAST(units_sold AS FLOAT)) OVER (
    PARTITION BY product_id
    ORDER BY sale_date
    ROWS BETWEEN 2 PRECEDING AND CURRENT ROW
  ), 2) AS moving_avg_3d_rounded
FROM daily_sales
ORDER BY product_id, sale_date;

-- Note: For the first two rows of each product,
-- the window is smaller (1-row and 2-row).
-- To require exactly 3 rows, add HAVING or filter:
-- WHERE ROW_NUMBER() OVER (...) >= 3
🐍 PySpark
from pyspark.sql.functions import avg, round as _round, col
from pyspark.sql.window import Window

# 3-day moving average window
win = (Window.partitionBy("product_id")
             .orderBy("sale_date")
             .rowsBetween(-2, 0))  # 2 preceding + current = 3 rows

result = (df.withColumn("moving_avg_3d",
    _round(avg("units_sold").over(win), 2))
   .orderBy("product_id", "sale_date"))
            
result.select("sale_date","product_id","units_sold","moving_avg_3d").show()

# 7-day moving average: rowsBetween(-6, 0)
# 30-day: rowsBetween(-29, 0)
18
Find departments with more than 5 employees — and employees who joined in the last 6 months
Filter + Aggregation
▼
🗄 SQL
-- Departments with more than 5 employees
SELECT department, COUNT(*) AS emp_count
FROM employees
GROUP BY department
HAVING COUNT(*) > 5
ORDER BY emp_count DESC;

-- Employees who joined in the last 6 months
SELECT employee_id, name, department, hire_date
FROM employees
WHERE hire_date >= DATEADD(MONTH, -6, GETDATE())
  -- PostgreSQL: hire_date >= NOW() - INTERVAL '6 months'
  -- BigQuery:   hire_date >= DATE_SUB(CURRENT_DATE(),
  --                                   INTERVAL 6 MONTH)
ORDER BY hire_date DESC;
🐍 PySpark
from pyspark.sql.functions import (count, col, add_months, current_date)

# Departments with more than 5 employees
dept_counts = (df.groupBy("department")
                .agg(count("*").alias("emp_count"))
                .filter(col("emp_count") > 5)
                .orderBy(col("emp_count").desc()))
dept_counts.show()

# Employees who joined in the last 6 months
six_months_ago = add_months(current_date(), -6)

recent = (df.filter(col("hire_date") >= six_months_ago)
            .orderBy(col("hire_date").desc()))
recent.show()
🛒 Set 5 — Customer & Sales Analytics
19
Customers who spent the most in the last month (based on order_date)
Date FilterTop-N
▼
orders: order_id, customer_id, order_date  |  order_items: order_id, quantity, unit_price
🗄 SQL
-- Top spenders last calendar month
SELECT
  o.customer_id,
  SUM(oi.quantity * oi.unit_price) AS total_spent
FROM orders o
JOIN order_items oi ON o.order_id = oi.order_id
WHERE o.order_date >= DATE_TRUNC('month', CURRENT_DATE - INTERVAL '1 month')
  AND o.order_date <  DATE_TRUNC('month', CURRENT_DATE)
GROUP BY o.customer_id
ORDER BY total_spent DESC
LIMIT 10;

-- SQL Server version:
-- WHERE order_date >= DATEADD(MONTH,
--         DATEDIFF(MONTH, 0, GETDATE())-1, 0)
--   AND order_date <  DATEADD(MONTH,
--         DATEDIFF(MONTH, 0, GETDATE()), 0)
🐍 PySpark
from pyspark.sql.functions import (sum as _sum, col, add_months, date_trunc, current_date)

# Last month start and end
this_month_start = date_trunc("month", current_date())
last_month_start = add_months(this_month_start, -1)

items = items.withColumn("line_total", col("quantity") * col("unit_price"))

result = (orders.filter((col("order_date") >= last_month_start) & (col("order_date") <  this_month_start))
                .join(items, "order_id")
                .groupBy("customer_id")
                .agg(_sum("line_total").alias("total_spent"))
                .orderBy(col("total_spent").desc()))
result.show(10)
20
Find customers whose monthly orders grew this month compared to last month
MoM GrowthLAG / Pivot
▼
🗄 SQL
WITH monthly AS (
  SELECT
    customer_id,
    DATE_TRUNC('month', order_date) AS month,
    COUNT(*)                        AS order_count,
    SUM(amount)                     AS total_spent
  FROM orders
  GROUP BY customer_id, DATE_TRUNC('month', order_date)
),
with_prev AS (
  SELECT *,
    LAG(order_count) OVER (PARTITION BY customer_id ORDER BY month) AS prev_order_count
  FROM monthly
)
SELECT customer_id, month,
       order_count AS this_month,
       prev_order_count AS last_month,
       order_count - prev_order_count AS growth
FROM with_prev
WHERE month = DATE_TRUNC('month', CURRENT_DATE)
  AND order_count > COALESCE(prev_order_count, 0)
ORDER BY growth DESC;
🐍 PySpark
from pyspark.sql.functions import (date_trunc, count, sum as _sum,
                                                          col, lag, current_date, add_months, coalesce, lit)
from pyspark.sql.window import Window

orders = spark.read.format("delta").load("/mnt/delta/orders")
orders = orders.withColumn("month", date_trunc("month", col("order_date")))

monthly = (orders.groupBy("customer_id", "month")
                .agg(count("*").alias("order_count"),
                  _sum("amount").alias("total_spent")))

cust_win = Window.partitionBy("customer_id").orderBy("month")

with_prev = monthly.withColumn("prev_count", lag("order_count", 1).over(cust_win))

this_month = date_trunc("month", current_date())
result = (with_prev.filter(col("month") == this_month)
                   .filter(col("order_count") > coalesce(col("prev_count"), lit(0)))
                   .withColumn("growth", col("order_count") - coalesce(col("prev_count"), lit(0)))
                   .orderBy(col("growth").desc()))
result.show()
21
PySpark: Customers who spent more than ₹1000 from store S1 in the last month (Customer + Store + Sales)
Multi-Table JoinPySpark
▼
customers: customer_id, name, city
stores: store_id, store_name, region
sales: sale_id, customer_id, store_id, sale_date, amount
🗄 SQL
SELECT
  c.customer_id,
  c.name,
  SUM(s.amount) AS total_spent
FROM sales s
JOIN customers c ON s.customer_id = c.customer_id
JOIN stores  st ON s.store_id     = st.store_id
WHERE st.store_name = 'S1'
  AND s.sale_date >= ADD_MONTHS(TRUNC(SYSDATE,'MM'),-1)
  AND s.sale_date <  TRUNC(SYSDATE,'MM')
GROUP BY c.customer_id, c.name
HAVING SUM(s.amount) > 1000
ORDER BY total_spent DESC;
🐍 PySpark
from pyspark.sql.functions import (
  sum as _sum, col, add_months, date_trunc, current_date)

customers = spark.read.format("delta").load("/mnt/delta/customers")
stores    = spark.read.format("delta").load("/mnt/delta/stores")
sales     = spark.read.format("delta").load("/mnt/delta/sales")

# Date range: last calendar month
this_month = date_trunc("month", current_date())
last_month = add_months(this_month, -1)

# Step 1: Filter to store S1
store_s1 = stores.filter(col("store_name") == "S1").select("store_id")

# Step 2: Filter sales to last month + store S1
filtered_sales = (sales.join(store_s1, "store_id", "inner")
                      .filter((col("sale_date") >= last_month) &
                      (col("sale_date") <  this_month)))

# Step 3: Aggregate + filter threshold + join customers
result = (filtered_sales.groupBy("customer_id")
                      .agg(_sum("amount").alias("total_spent"))
                      .filter(col("total_spent") > 1000)
                      .join(customers, "customer_id", "inner")
                      .select("customer_id","name","total_spent")
                      .orderBy(col("total_spent").desc()))
result.show()
22
Find employees and their department names — include employees with no department assigned (LEFT JOIN)
JOIN
▼
employees: employee_id, name, department_id
departments: department_id, department_name
🗄 SQL
-- LEFT JOIN: keeps ALL employees even without dept
SELECT
  e.employee_id,
  e.name,
  COALESCE(d.department_name, 'Not Assigned') AS department
FROM employees e
LEFT JOIN departments d
  ON e.department_id = d.department_id
ORDER BY department, e.name;

-- To see ONLY employees without a department:
SELECT e.*
FROM employees e
LEFT JOIN departments d ON e.department_id = d.department_id
WHERE d.department_id IS NULL;

-- INNER JOIN (comparison): only employees WITH a dept
SELECT e.employee_id, e.name, d.department_name
FROM employees e
INNER JOIN departments d ON e.department_id = d.department_id;
🐍 PySpark
from pyspark.sql.functions import coalesce, lit

emp  = spark.read.format("delta").load("/mnt/delta/employees")
dept = spark.read.format("delta").load("/mnt/delta/departments")

# LEFT JOIN - keep all employees
result = (emp.join(dept, "department_id", "left")
             .withColumn("department", coalesce("department_name", lit("Not Assigned")))
             .select("employee_id","name","department")
             .orderBy("department","name"))
result.show()

# Employees with NO department
no_dept = (emp.join(dept, "department_id", "left_anti"))
no_dept.show()
📂 Set 6 — Processing Large Files & Nested Data
23
Read and process a large CSV file efficiently in Python / PySpark
PythonLarge Files
▼

For truly large files, PySpark is almost always the right answer. But sometimes you need Python-only (memory-efficient chunking). Here are both approaches:

🐍 Python (Pandas — chunked, memory-safe)
import pandas as pd
from typing import Iterator

# --- Approach 1: chunked reading ---
CHUNK_SIZE = 100_000  # rows per chunk
results = []

for chunk in pd.read_csv(
    "large_file.csv",
    chunksize=CHUNK_SIZE,
    dtype={"amount": float, "customer_id": str},
    parse_dates=["order_date"],
    usecols=["customer_id","amount","order_date"],
    na_values=["", "NULL", "N/A"]
):
    # Filter and aggregate per chunk
    filtered = chunk[chunk["amount"] > 0]
    agg = (filtered
        .groupby("customer_id")["amount"]
        .sum()
        .reset_index()
    )
    results.append(agg)

# Combine all chunks
final = (pd.concat(results)
    .groupby("customer_id")["amount"]
    .sum()
    .reset_index()
    .sort_values("amount", ascending=False)
)
print(final.head(20))

# --- Approach 2: Polars (faster than Pandas) ---
import polars as pl

df = (pl.scan_csv("large_file.csv")   # lazy scan
        .filter(pl.col("amount") > 0)
        .groupby("customer_id")
        .agg(pl.col("amount").sum())
        .sort("amount", descending=True)
        .collect()                     # execute
)
print(df.head(20))
🐍 PySpark (recommended for large scale)
from pyspark.sql import SparkSession
from pyspark.sql.functions import col, sum as _sum
from pyspark.sql.types import (
  StructType, StructField,
  StringType, DoubleType, DateType
)

spark = (SparkSession.builder
  .appName("LargeCSVProcessing")
  .config("spark.sql.shuffle.partitions", "400")
  .getOrCreate()
)

# Always define schema explicitly in production
schema = StructType([
  StructField("customer_id", StringType(), False),
  StructField("order_id",    StringType(), False),
  StructField("order_date",  DateType(),   True),
  StructField("amount",      DoubleType(), True),
  StructField("status",      StringType(), True),
])

df = (spark.read
  .option("header", "true")
  .option("dateFormat", "yyyy-MM-dd")
  .option("mode", "PERMISSIVE")  # handle bad rows
  .option("columnNameOfCorruptRecord", "_corrupt_record")
  .schema(schema)
  .csv("/mnt/data/large_file.csv")
)

# Cache if used multiple times
df.cache()

# Process: filter, aggregate
result = (df.filter(col("amount") > 0)
            .filter(col("status") != "CANCELLED")
            .groupBy("customer_id")
            .agg(_sum("amount").alias("total_spent"))
            .orderBy(col("total_spent").desc()))

result.write.format("delta") \
    .mode("overwrite") \
    .save("/mnt/delta/customer_totals")
💡
Key efficiency tips: define schema (no inferSchema), pushdown filters early (filter before join), use Parquet not CSV in production (10x faster reads), select only columns you need before any transformation.
24
PySpark: Process nested JSON trip data (real-world scenario)
Nested JSONPySpark
▼
Sample JSON structure:
{ "trip_id": "T1", "driver": {"id": "D1", "name": "Raj"}, "waypoints": [{"seq": 1, "lat": 12.9, "lng": 77.6, "ts": "2024-01-15T10:00:00"}, ...], "fare": {"base": 50, "surcharge": 10, "total": 60} }
🐍 PySpark — Full Nested JSON Processing
from pyspark.sql import SparkSession
from pyspark.sql.functions import (col, explode_outer, to_timestamp, round as _round)

spark = SparkSession.builder.getOrCreate()

# Read raw JSON (multiline for pretty-printed files)
raw = spark.read \
    .option("multiline", "true") \
    .json("/mnt/raw/trips/*.json")

raw.printSchema()
# root
#  |-- trip_id:    string
#  |-- driver:     struct (id, name)
#  |-- waypoints:  array  (seq, lat, lng, ts)
#  |-- fare:       struct (base, surcharge, total)

# ── Step 1: Flatten top-level structs ────────────────
trips = raw.select(
  col("trip_id"),
  col("driver.id").alias("driver_id"),
  col("driver.name").alias("driver_name"),
  col("fare.base").alias("fare_base"),
  col("fare.surcharge").alias("fare_surcharge"),
  col("fare.total").alias("fare_total"),
  col("waypoints")   # keep array for next step
)

# ── Step 2: Explode waypoints array ──────────────────
waypoints = trips.select(
  col("trip_id"),
  col("driver_id"),
  col("fare_total"),
  explode_outer(col("waypoints")).alias("wp")
).select(
  col("trip_id"),
  col("driver_id"),
  col("fare_total"),
  col("wp.seq").alias("waypoint_seq"),
  col("wp.lat").alias("latitude"),
  col("wp.lng").alias("longitude"),
  to_timestamp(col("wp.ts")).alias("waypoint_ts")
)

waypoints.show(5)

# ── Step 3: Aggregations on trip level ───────────────
from pyspark.sql.functions import (count, sum as _sum, min as _min, max as _max)

trip_summary = (waypoints
  .groupBy("trip_id", "driver_id", "fare_total")
  .agg(
    count("waypoint_seq").alias("total_waypoints"),
    _min("waypoint_ts").alias("trip_start"),
    _max("waypoint_ts").alias("trip_end")
  )
)
trip_summary.show()

# ── Step 4: Write to Delta ────────────────────────────
waypoints.write.format("delta") \
    .partitionBy("driver_id") \
    .mode("overwrite") \
    .save("/mnt/delta/trip_waypoints")

trip_summary.write.format("delta") \
    .mode("overwrite") \
    .save("/mnt/delta/trip_summary")
⭐ Bonus — Essential Patterns Every Data Engineer Must Know
25
Slowly Changing Dimension (SCD Type 2) — track historical changes in dimension records
SCD Type 2MERGE
▼
dim_customers: surrogate_key, customer_id, name, city, effective_from, effective_to, is_current
🗄 SQL — SCD Type 2 MERGE
-- Expire old records that changed
UPDATE dim_customers
SET effective_to = CAST(GETDATE() AS DATE),
    is_current = 0
WHERE customer_id IN (
  SELECT s.customer_id
  FROM stg_customers s
  JOIN dim_customers d
    ON s.customer_id = d.customer_id
    AND d.is_current = 1
  WHERE s.city <> d.city   -- attribute changed
);

-- Insert new version for changed records
INSERT INTO dim_customers
  (customer_id, name, city,
   effective_from, effective_to, is_current)
SELECT s.customer_id, s.name, s.city,
  CAST(GETDATE() AS DATE),
  '9999-12-31',
  1
FROM stg_customers s
JOIN dim_customers d
  ON s.customer_id = d.customer_id
WHERE d.is_current = 0
  AND d.effective_to = CAST(GETDATE() AS DATE);

-- Insert brand new customers
INSERT INTO dim_customers
  (customer_id, name, city,
   effective_from, effective_to, is_current)
SELECT s.customer_id, s.name, s.city,
  CAST(GETDATE() AS DATE), '9999-12-31', 1
FROM stg_customers s
WHERE s.customer_id NOT IN (
  SELECT customer_id FROM dim_customers
);
🐍 PySpark — SCD Type 2 with Delta MERGE
from delta.tables import DeltaTable
from pyspark.sql.functions import (lit, current_date, col)

dim = DeltaTable.forPath(spark, "/mnt/delta/dim_customers")
stg = spark.read.format("delta").load("/mnt/delta/stg_customers")

# Step 1: Expire records where attribute changed
(dim.alias("d").merge(stg.alias("s"), "d.customer_id = s.customer_id AND d.is_current = 1")
  .whenMatchedUpdate(condition="s.city != d.city",
    set={"effective_to": "current_date()",
         "is_current":   "0"}).execute())

# Step 2: Insert new versions for changed + new customers
dim_df = dim.toDF()
existing = dim_df.filter("is_current = 1").select("customer_id","city")

# Find changed customers
changed = (stg.join(existing, "customer_id")
  .filter(stg["city"] != existing["city"])
  .select(stg["*"])
)

# Find brand new customers
new_custs = stg.join(dim_df.select("customer_id").distinct(), "customer_id", "left_anti")

to_insert = (changed.union(new_custs)
  .withColumn("effective_from", current_date())
  .withColumn("effective_to",   lit("9999-12-31").cast("date"))
  .withColumn("is_current",     lit(1))
)

to_insert.write.format("delta") \
    .mode("append") \
    .save("/mnt/delta/dim_customers")
26
Pivot and Unpivot — transform rows to columns and back
PivotReshape
▼
sales: customer_id, month (Jan/Feb/Mar…), revenue
🗄 SQL — PIVOT
-- SQL Server PIVOT
SELECT customer_id, [Jan], [Feb], [Mar]
FROM (
  SELECT customer_id, month, revenue FROM sales
) src
PIVOT (
  SUM(revenue)
  FOR month IN ([Jan], [Feb], [Mar])
) pvt;

-- Standard SQL (CASE WHEN approach, works everywhere)
SELECT customer_id,
  SUM(CASE WHEN month='Jan' THEN revenue ELSE 0 END) AS Jan,
  SUM(CASE WHEN month='Feb' THEN revenue ELSE 0 END) AS Feb,
  SUM(CASE WHEN month='Mar' THEN revenue ELSE 0 END) AS Mar
FROM sales
GROUP BY customer_id;
🐍 PySpark — groupBy + pivot
from pyspark.sql.functions import sum as _sum

# Pivot: rows (customer_id, month, revenue)
# → columns (customer_id, Jan, Feb, Mar)
pivoted = (sales_df.groupBy("customer_id")
                   .pivot("month", ["Jan", "Feb", "Mar"])
                   .agg(_sum("revenue")))
pivoted.show()

# Unpivot (stack columns back to rows)
from pyspark.sql.functions import expr, stack

unpivoted = pivoted.select("customer_id",
  expr("""stack(3,
      'Jan', Jan,
      'Feb', Feb,
      'Mar', Mar) AS (month, revenue)"""))
unpivoted.show()
27
NULL handling — COALESCE, NULLIF, ISNULL — and common traps
NULL HandlingSQL
▼
🗄 SQL — NULL Functions
-- COALESCE: return first non-null value
SELECT COALESCE(phone, mobile, 'No Contact') AS contact
FROM customers;

-- NULLIF: return NULL if two values are equal
-- (avoid divide-by-zero)
SELECT revenue / NULLIF(units, 0) AS revenue_per_unit
FROM sales;

-- IS NULL / IS NOT NULL (never use = NULL)
SELECT * FROM orders WHERE discount IS NULL;

-- NULL in aggregations (NULL is ignored by SUM, AVG)
-- but COUNT(*) counts NULLs, COUNT(col) does not!
SELECT
  COUNT(*)          AS total_rows,      -- includes NULLs
  COUNT(discount)   AS rows_with_disc,  -- excludes NULLs
  AVG(discount)     AS avg_disc,        -- ignores NULLs
  AVG(COALESCE(discount, 0)) AS avg_disc_with_zero
FROM orders;

-- NULL in JOIN: NULL != NULL, so NULLs never join
-- Use IS NULL check after a LEFT JOIN to find unmatched
SELECT o.*, c.name
FROM orders o
LEFT JOIN customers c ON o.customer_id = c.customer_id
WHERE c.customer_id IS NULL;  -- orders with no customer!
🐍 PySpark — NULL handling
from pyspark.sql.functions import (coalesce, lit, isnull, when, col, count, isnan)

# COALESCE equivalent
df.withColumn("contact", coalesce(col("phone"), col("mobile"), lit("No Contact")))

# Replace NULLs
df.fillna({"discount": 0, "city": "Unknown"})

# Drop rows where key columns are null
df.dropna(subset=["customer_id", "order_date"])

# Count NULLs per column (data quality check)
from pyspark.sql.functions import sum as _sum
null_counts = df.select([_sum(isnull(c).cast("int")).alias(c) for c in df.columns])
null_counts.show()

# Filter NULLs (use isNull(), not == None)
df.filter(col("discount").isNull())
df.filter(col("discount").isNotNull())

# Safe division (avoid divide by zero)
df.withColumn("rev_per_unit", when(col("units") != 0, col("revenue") / col("units")).otherwise(None))
28
Date functions cheat-sheet — date filtering, truncation, add_months, datediff
Date FunctionsEssential
▼
🗄 SQL Date Functions
-- Current date/time
SELECT GETDATE(), CURRENT_DATE, NOW();

-- Truncate to month/year start
SELECT DATE_TRUNC('month', order_date)  -- PostgreSQL/Synapse
SELECT DATEADD(MONTH,
  DATEDIFF(MONTH, 0, order_date), 0)    -- SQL Server

-- Add/subtract time
SELECT DATEADD(DAY,   -7,  GETDATE())   -- 7 days ago
SELECT DATEADD(MONTH, -1,  GETDATE())   -- 1 month ago
SELECT DATEADD(YEAR,  -1,  GETDATE())   -- 1 year ago

-- Difference between dates
SELECT DATEDIFF(DAY, hire_date, GETDATE()) AS days_employed
SELECT DATEDIFF(MONTH, order_date, GETDATE()) AS months_ago

-- Extract parts
SELECT YEAR(order_date), MONTH(order_date),
       DAY(order_date),  DATEPART(QUARTER, order_date)

-- Format
SELECT FORMAT(order_date, 'yyyy-MM-dd')
SELECT CONVERT(VARCHAR, order_date, 23)

-- Last day of month
SELECT EOMONTH(GETDATE())
SELECT EOMONTH(GETDATE(), -1)  -- last day of prev month
🐍 PySpark Date Functions
from pyspark.sql.functions import (
  current_date, current_timestamp,
  date_trunc, add_months, date_add,
  datediff, months_between,
  year, month, dayofmonth, quarter,
  date_format, last_day,
  to_date, to_timestamp
)

df = df.withColumn("today", current_date())

# Truncate to month start
df.withColumn("month_start", date_trunc("month", col("order_date")))

# Add / subtract
df.withColumn("next_month",  add_months(col("order_date"),  1))
df.withColumn("prev_month",  add_months(col("order_date"), -1))
df.withColumn("week_ago",    date_add(col("order_date"),   -7))

# Differences
df.withColumn("days_diff", datediff(current_date(), col("order_date")))
df.withColumn("months_diff", months_between(current_date(), col("order_date")).cast("int"))

# Extract parts
df.withColumn("yr",  year(col("order_date")))
df.withColumn("mth", month(col("order_date")))
df.withColumn("day", dayofmonth(col("order_date")))
df.withColumn("qtr", quarter(col("order_date")))

# Format
df.withColumn("fmt_date", date_format(col("order_date"), "yyyy-MM-dd"))

# Parse strings to date
df.withColumn("parsed_date", to_date(col("date_str"), "dd/MM/yyyy"))

# Last day of month
df.withColumn("month_end", last_day(col("order_date")))
1
What is Apache Airflow?
Overview
▼

Airflow is an open-source framework which can be used as an Orchestrator. Orchestration means managing execution order, timing, retries, dependencies and monitoring workflows.

So Airflow is an open-source platform to programmatically author, schedule, and monitor workflows. We write workflows using Python code — this concept is called DAG-as-Code.

  • History: Created at Airbnb in 2014. Later donated to Apache Software Foundation.
  • Airflow is based on DAG (Directed Acyclic Graph). Everything in Airflow is a DAG.

What Airflow is NOT:

  • Not a Data Processing / Real-Time Processing framework.
  • Not an ETL framework.
  • Doesn't store / process big data.
💡
It only triggers, monitors & manages order. It triggers Spark jobs, Python scripts, SQL, Bash commands, API calls.

Why companies prefer DAG-as-Code instead of Drag/Drop tools:

  • Version Control (Git integration) — changes can be tracked
  • Code review process — checking pull requests, reviewing workflow logic
  • Reusability & Modularity — dynamically generate multiple pipelines
  • Testing — code can be tested
  • Complex logic handling
  • Infrastructure as Code philosophy
2
What is a DAG in Airflow?
Core Concept
▼

DAG stands for Directed Acyclic Graph.

  • Directed: Edges have direction → Task A → Task B. This means A must complete before B starts.
  • Acyclic: No loops allowed. Airflow forbids cycles.
  • Graph: It is Nodes (tasks) & Edges (dependencies).

It is a Python object that defines a workflow structure & schedule. DAG doesn't execute tasks — it defines structure.

Ex: Architectural drawing of a building, not the building. Blueprint of a pipeline, not the pipeline itself.

Real Example:
start → extract-A → Transform-A → Load
start → extract-B → Transform-B → Load
(2 parallel branches merge into 1 task — a valid DAG)

DAG vs Task:

  • DAG is a full workflow — contains tasks & has a schedule.
  • Task is a single step — executes logic & has execution logic.

Execution Flow — when scheduler sees a DAG:

  1. Creates DAG Run.
  2. Creates Task Instance.
  3. Sends Task to executor.
  4. Worker executes.
📌
DAG defines structure. Task instance represents one execution of a task.
3
What are the Core Components of Airflow?
Architecture
▼
  • ① Metadata DB: Airflow needs to store all the metadata about the DAGs — like DAG runs, schedule, status, task instances.
  • ② DAG File Processor: It will go to the DAGs folder, parse it, and store the serialized DAG into the DB for the scheduler to use.
  • ③ API Server (Web Server): API is the middleman for every task. It serves the Airflow UI and provides REST API for interacting with Airflow — including for the UI, workers & external clients.
  • ④ Scheduler: The heart of Airflow. Responsible for monitoring DAGs, scheduling task instances based on dependencies and timing, and submitting tasks to the executor.
  • ⑤ Executor: It determines how & where tasks need to run. It is a configuration of the scheduler (not a separate component). Common executors: Local Executor (for dev), Celery Executor (for distributed workers), Kubernetes Executor (for dynamic pod-based execution).
  • ⑥ Workers: A process that runs tasks as defined by the executor. Workers are the real layer for execution.
  • ⑦ Queue: Executor prioritizes the tasks to which worker will have to work.
  • ⑧ Triggerer: Designed to improve efficiency of long-running tasks, particularly those that depend on external sources. Instead of blocking workers, the triggerer handles tasks asynchronously — improving resource usage & scalability. Allows Airflow to scale more effectively when workflows have tasks involving waiting for external events.
4
Airflow Architecture — end-to-end flow
Architecture
▼
Flow:
Dev → DAGs folder → DAG Processor → Metadata DB → API → Scheduler → Execution Queue → Executor → Worker → Execute → Redis Queue → Triggerer
  • Dev writes the .py files in the dags folder.
  • DAG Processor parses the DAG files, extracts task definitions, and stores metadata in the DB. Creates task instances & creates execution queue.
  • Scheduler decides when DAG runs are runnable tasks, and pushes them into the metadata DB. The DAG runs are read from the metadata DB.
  • Executor decides the strategy — it will put all the tasks in the Redis queue. Executor pulls tasks from Redis queue, executes items & updates status in metadata DB.
  • Triggerer is used for Deferrable Operators. Handles tasks waiting asynchronously for file, trigger etc. Logs metadata, stores DAG runs, task instances, status. If metadata DB goes down — XCom, connections, variables, Airflow stop functioning.
5
What are Operators and Tasks in Airflow?
Core Concept
▼

An Operator is a pre-defined way to execute some type of work.

Ex: Bash, Python, Empty, Email, BranchPythonOperator etc.

  • Operator → Template / Class
  • Task → instance of that operator inside a DAG
  • Task instance → one execution of that task for one DAG run

Task: It is a node in a DAG that wraps an operator and becomes executable at runtime.

DAG Versioning: Whenever a new change is applied, after parsing, it will create another version of the DAG like V1, V2, V3... All the versions can be accessed from options.

6
What is XCom in Airflow?
Communication
▼

XCom = Cross Communication. It allows one task to send small pieces of data to another task.

  • XCom is NOT for larger data.
  • Used for: IDs, filenames, status flags, small JSON etc.
  • Not for: Large DataFrames, Big CSVs, Binary files.

How XCom works internally: When a task returns a value, Airflow automatically stores the return value in XCom, saves it in Metadata DB — which is associated with task_id + DAG Run. Downstream task can pull it.

  • ti → Task Instance Object. It allows to pull data from previous task → ti.xcom_pull()
⚠️
If too much data is stored/shared through XCom: DB will bloat, Scheduler slows down, Web server becomes unstable, System performance degrades.

What is Context: Runtime metadata passed to task. It contains dag_id, task_id, run_id, execution_date, logical_date, previous_task_state.

Two ways to use XCom:

  • Automatic XCom: Task uses @task.python decorator — simply return a value from first task and pass it as argument to second task. Airflow automatically handles the push/pull.
  • Manual XCom (kwargs): Extract ti from kwargs. Push using ti.xcom_push(key='return_result', value=fetch_data). Pull using ti.xcom_pull(task_id='first_id', key='return_result').
7
How do you write your first DAG in Airflow?
Code
▼

We will create a simple flow with 3 tasks: Task-1 → Task-2 → Task-3. Task-1 is completed when the above task is done.

🐍 PySpark
from airflow.sdk import dag, task

@dag(dag_id = "first-dag")
def first_dag():

    @task.python
    def first_task():
        print("This is 1st task")

    @task.python
    def second_task():
        print("This is end task")

    @task.python
    def third_task():
        print("This is 3rd task")

    # Defining Task Dependencies
    first  = first_task()
    second = second_task()
    third  = third_task()

    first >> second >> third  # first → second → third

first_dag()
💡
dag_id is unique id for the dag. Specify it — if we don't, it will take the function name by default. The DAGs are stored in the serialized_dag table in Postgres DB.
8
How does Airflow compare to ADF for orchestration?
Comparison
▼
  • Airflow: Open-source, code-first (Python DAGs), platform-agnostic, excellent for complex logic, branching, dynamic pipeline generation, strong community. Self-hosted or managed (MWAA, Astronomer, Cloud Composer).
  • ADF: Azure-native, GUI-based (low-code), tightly integrated with Azure services (ADLS, Synapse, SQL). Best for Azure-to-Azure pipelines, no coding needed for simple ETL.
  • Use Airflow when: cross-cloud, complex branching logic, dynamic DAG generation, code review workflow, non-Azure sources.
  • Use ADF when: fully Azure ecosystem, team is not Python-heavy, quick Azure service integration needed.
📌
In interviews: "Both are orchestrators — they trigger and monitor, not process data. Airflow gives more flexibility and testability through Python code. ADF gives tighter Azure integration with a visual interface."
🧩
Data Modelling
Star schema, SCD, normalization, PII masking, Delta Lake skipping, M:N relationships, monitoring
📐 Fundamentals
1
What is Data Modelling?
Fundamentals
▼

Data Modelling is the Process of Creating a Visual and logical blueprint that defines how data is Structured, organized and Stored & retrieved in a System.

  • It makes data easier to understand & use.
  • Improves query Performance.
  • Helps build Scalable System.

Types of Data Models:

1. Conceptual Data Model: It focuses on high-level business concepts and how data is used in an organization. This model defines the Scope of a data System by identifying the type and kind of data, the attributes & the Relationships between them.

2. Logical Data Model: This model builds on the Conceptual data model by adding more detailed & technical information, such as a defined Structure, organization & relationship of the data, tables, Columns & Constraints without specifying technical Storage.

3. Physical Data Model: It represents the implementation of the data model in a specific DBMS. It focuses on Actual implementation of the database using queries and the Chosen DBMS features.

2
OLTP vs OLAP in Data Modelling
Fundamentals
▼
  • OLTP (Online Transaction Processing): Designed for day-to-day transactional operations. Highly normalized (3NF), optimized for fast reads and writes of individual rows. Low data volume per query. Examples: banking systems, e-commerce order systems, CRM systems. Uses row-oriented storage.
  • OLAP (Online Analytical Processing): Designed for analytical queries and reporting. Denormalized (star/snowflake schema), optimized for aggregations and scans across large volumes. High data volume per query. Examples: data warehouses, BI dashboards, Synapse, Redshift. Uses columnar storage.
  • Key differences: OLTP — many small transactions, normalized, row store, millisecond response. OLAP — few large queries, denormalized, columnar store, seconds to minutes response.
  • In Azure: OLTP → Azure SQL Database, Cosmos DB. OLAP → Synapse Dedicated Pool, Databricks SQL, Synapse Serverless SQL.
3
Star Schema vs Snowflake Schema vs One Big Table (OBT)
Schema Design
▼

Star Schema: One central fact table (measurements/events/transactions) surrounded by denormalized dimension tables. Each dimension table connects directly to the fact table. Simple JOIN path, fast queries, easy for BI tools to understand. Best for Synapse, Redshift, traditional DW.

Snowflake Schema: Like star schema, but dimension tables are normalized — dimensions reference sub-dimension tables (e.g., dim_product → dim_category → dim_subcategory). Less redundancy, smaller storage, but more JOINs required. Harder for BI tools to auto-generate queries.

One Big Table (OBT): All dimensions pre-joined into the fact table — one massive denormalized table. No JOINs needed for queries = maximum read speed. Best for Lakehouse Gold layer serving BI dashboards. Downside: massive storage redundancy, difficult to maintain when dimensions change.

📌
Modern lakehouse pattern: Silver = normalized Star Schema (Delta tables, ACID, flexible). Gold = OBT or Star Schema (pre-joined, optimized for Power BI / Tableau reads). Serverless SQL views bridge Silver → Gold without duplicating data.
4
Fact Tables vs Dimension Tables — types and design rules
Star Schema
▼

Fact Tables: Store measurements, events, or transactions. Rows are additive (you can SUM them). Very wide (many metrics) and very tall (billions of rows). Always contain foreign keys to dimension tables + metric columns (amount, quantity, duration).

Types of fact tables:

  • Transaction fact: One row per event (each order, each payment, each click). Most common. Grain = one transaction.
  • Periodic snapshot: One row per time period per entity (monthly account balance, weekly inventory level). Good for trend analysis.
  • Accumulating snapshot: One row per pipeline/process instance that gets updated as milestones are hit (order placed → shipped → delivered → returned). One row follows the entity's entire lifecycle.
  • Factless fact: No numeric measures — records that an event happened (student attended class, product was eligible for promotion). Useful for counting relationships.

Dimension Tables: Describe the "who, what, where, when, why" context of facts. Smaller, slowly changing. Contain surrogate key + natural key + descriptive attributes. Types: Conformed (shared across multiple fact tables), Degenerate (dimension key in fact table with no separate dim table, e.g., order number), Junk (low-cardinality flags grouped into one table).

5
Normalization (1NF, 2NF, 3NF) — explain each with an example
Normalization
▼

1NF (First Normal Form): Every column must contain atomic (indivisible) values. No repeating groups. Each row must be unique.

  • Violation: customer(id, name, phone1, phone2, phone3) — repeating groups for phone numbers.
  • Fix: customer(id, name) + customer_phone(customer_id, phone)

2NF (Second Normal Form): Must be in 1NF, AND every non-key column must depend on the WHOLE primary key (no partial dependencies). Only matters when PK is composite.

  • Violation: order_item(order_id, product_id, quantity, product_name) — product_name depends only on product_id, not on the full composite key.
  • Fix: Move product_name to a product(product_id, product_name) table.

3NF (Third Normal Form): Must be in 2NF, AND no transitive dependencies (non-key columns must not depend on other non-key columns).

  • Violation: employee(id, name, zip_code, city) — city depends on zip_code, not on id.
  • Fix: employee(id, name, zip_code) + zip_code(zip_code, city)
📌
In data engineering: normalize for OLTP (transactional systems — 3NF minimizes update anomalies). Denormalize for OLAP (analytical — fewer JOINs = faster aggregations on billions of rows).
6
Slowly Changing Dimensions (SCD Types 0, 1, 2, 3, 4, 6)
SCD
▼

SCDs handle how dimension attributes change over time. Choosing the right type is a business decision about history preservation vs complexity.

  • Type 0: Never change. Ignore updates. Used for static reference data (country codes, currency codes).
  • Type 1: Overwrite the old value. No history. Simple. Use when historical accuracy doesn't matter (correcting typos, fixing data errors).
  • Type 2 (most common): Add a new row for each change. Preserve full history. Columns: effective_from, effective_to, is_current. Fact tables join on surrogate key — they point to the exact dimension row that was current at transaction time. Historical analysis is fully accurate.
  • Type 3: Add a new column for the previous value (e.g., current_region, prev_region). Preserves only one level of history. Simple but limited — only one change back.
  • Type 4: Separate history table — current table has only current values, history table has all previous versions. Fast reads on current, full history still available separately.
  • Type 6 (Hybrid 1+2+3): SCD2 row versioning + a current_value column on all rows. Supports "what was the value then" (Type 2) AND "what is the current value" (Type 1) in a single query without self-joining.
7
Difference between Data Lake vs Data Warehouse vs Lakehouse architecture
▼

"These three represent the evolution of how organizations store and serve data. Each solved the problems of the previous one — but each introduced new trade-offs."

Data Warehouse:

  • Structured, schema-enforced storage optimized for SQL queries and BI reporting. Data is cleaned and modelled before it lands here — ETL (Extract, Transform, Load).
  • Examples: Synapse Dedicated Pool, Redshift, Snowflake, BigQuery.
  • Strengths: Fast SQL performance, strong governance, well-understood by BI tools, ACID transactions.
  • Weaknesses: Expensive at scale, can't store unstructured data (images, logs, JSON), transformations must happen before loading — if you didn't anticipate a query pattern, you may not have the raw data anymore to re-derive it. Schema changes are painful and slow.

Data Lake:

  • Store everything raw, in any format (structured, semi-structured, unstructured), at low cost on object storage (ADLS, S3). Schema is applied on read — ELT (Extract, Load, Transform).
  • Examples: ADLS Gen2, S3, GCS with raw Parquet/JSON/CSV files.
  • Strengths: Cheap, flexible, stores any data type, supports ML and data science workloads, never discard raw data.
  • Weaknesses: No ACID transactions — partial writes create corrupted state. No schema enforcement — bad data silently lands. No update/delete support on Parquet. Performance is poor without careful partitioning. Can become a "data swamp" — data lands but nobody trusts or uses it.

Lakehouse:

  • Combines the best of both. Cheap object storage like a data lake, but with a transactional metadata layer (Delta Lake, Apache Iceberg, Apache Hudi) that adds ACID transactions, schema enforcement, time travel, and update/delete support.
  • Examples: Databricks Lakehouse (Delta Lake on ADLS), AWS Lakehouse (Iceberg on S3).
  • Strengths: Low cost storage of a data lake + reliability and performance of a data warehouse. One system for BI, ML, and streaming. Schema enforcement without losing raw data. MERGE, UPDATE, DELETE on object storage. Time travel for audit and recovery.
  • Weaknesses: More complex than a pure data warehouse for simple BI use cases. Requires more engineering expertise. Ecosystem is still maturing vs traditional DW tooling.
Quick comparison
Data Warehouse
✅ Fast SQL
✅ ACID
✅ Schema enforced
❌ Expensive
❌ No unstructured
❌ Schema rigid
Data Lake
✅ Cheap
✅ Any format
✅ Flexible
❌ No ACID
❌ No updates
❌ Data swamp risk
Lakehouse
✅ Cheap storage
✅ ACID
✅ Any format
✅ Updates/deletes
✅ Time travel
✅ One system
💡
"I always frame it this way in interviews: Data Warehouse = reliability but expensive and rigid. Data Lake = cheap and flexible but unreliable. Lakehouse = the transaction log on top of cheap object storage is what bridges the gap — you get cheap, flexible storage with warehouse-grade reliability. That's what Delta Lake gives us."
🎯 Scenario-Based & Advanced
8
How do you handle Many-to-Many relationships in a Star Schema without creating a Cartesian product?
Advanced ModellingScenario
▼

A many-to-many relationship (e.g., one Order can have many Promotions, one Promotion can apply to many Orders) creates a Cartesian product if you JOIN directly — every order row multiplied by every matching promotion row = duplicated facts (inflated revenue totals).

Solution: Bridge Table (associative entity)

Create a bridge table between the fact table and the many-side dimension:

  • fact_orders → bridge_order_promotions(order_id, promotion_id, weight_factor) → dim_promotions
  • The weight_factor column (e.g., 1/count_of_promotions) allows proportional allocation — when aggregating, multiply the metric by weight_factor to avoid double-counting.

Example: Order worth ₹1000 has 2 promotions applied. Bridge table has 2 rows with weight_factor = 0.5 each. When summing revenue by promotion: ₹1000 × 0.5 = ₹500 per promotion. Total = ₹1000 ✓ (not ₹2000).

Alternative — Multi-valued dimension degenerate approach: If cardinality is low (e.g., max 3 promotions per order), store as a delimited string in the fact table and use string-splitting functions in queries. Simpler but less elegant.

⚠️
Never JOIN a fact table directly to a many-side dimension without a bridge table. The fan-out will inflate all numeric measures and produce silently wrong analytics.
9
How do you implement Dynamic Data Masking in a Lakehouse to protect PII data?
PII / PrivacyGovernance
▼

PII (Personally Identifiable Information) masking ensures sensitive data (SSN, email, phone, credit card) is visible only to authorized users, even when the underlying data is stored in plain text.

Azure SQL / Synapse — Dynamic Data Masking (DDM):

🗄 SQL
-- Apply masking rule to column
ALTER TABLE customers ALTER COLUMN email
  ADD MASKED WITH (FUNCTION = 'email()');      -- aXXX@XXXX.com
ALTER TABLE customers ALTER COLUMN phone
  ADD MASKED WITH (FUNCTION = 'partial(0,"XXX-XXX-",4)');  -- XXX-XXX-1234
ALTER TABLE customers ALTER COLUMN ssn
  ADD MASKED WITH (FUNCTION = 'default()');   -- XXXX

-- Privileged users see real data
GRANT UNMASK ON customers TO data_engineer_role;

Databricks Unity Catalog — Column Masks (GA in DBR 12.2+):

🗄 SQL
-- Create a masking function
CREATE FUNCTION mask_email(email STRING, groups ARRAY<STRING>)
RETURNS STRING
RETURN CASE
  WHEN array_contains(groups, 'data_engineer') THEN email
  ELSE CONCAT(SUBSTRING(email, 1, 1), 'XXX@XXXX.com')
END;

-- Attach to column
ALTER TABLE customers ALTER COLUMN email
  SET MASK mask_email USING COLUMNS (current_user_groups());

Lakehouse approach (encryption + masking):

  • Bronze: store PII as-is (raw landing, restricted access via ADLS ACLs).
  • Silver: hash non-queryable PII (e.g., SHA-256 of SSN for joining), mask display fields. Store encrypted PII in a separate secured table.
  • Gold: serve only masked or tokenized PII. BI users never see raw PII.
  • Audit all access to PII columns via Unity Catalog audit logs.
10
Explain the Data Skipping mechanism in Delta Lake vs traditional SQL indexing
Delta LakePerformance
▼

Delta Lake Data Skipping: For each Parquet file in a Delta table, the transaction log stores file-level statistics: minValues, maxValues, and nullCount per column (for the first 32 columns by default). When you run a query with a filter, Delta checks these stats before opening any file. If a file's min/max range doesn't contain the filter value, the file is skipped entirely — no I/O, no task created.

Example: WHERE order_date = '2024-03-15' on a table with 1,000 files. If 950 files' date ranges don't include March 15, only 50 files are read. 95% I/O reduction.

Z-Ordering amplifies this: OPTIMIZE table ZORDER BY (customer_id, order_date) physically colocates rows with similar values in the same files — making min/max ranges tight, maximizing skip hit rate.

Traditional SQL B-Tree Index comparison:

  • B-Tree → row-level precision, O(log n) lookup, great for OLTP point queries. Requires random I/O to fetch scattered rows — catastrophic on object storage (ADLS) where random reads are expensive.
  • Delta Data Skipping → file-level granularity (less precise), but avoids all I/O for skipped files — perfect for analytical scans on object storage where sequential reads are cheap.
  • B-Tree requires maintenance (INSERT/UPDATE/DELETE updates the index). Delta stats are written automatically on each write — zero maintenance.
11
What is the impact of Broadcast Hints on worker memory? When should you avoid them?
MemoryJoin Strategy
▼

A broadcast hint sends a full copy of one DataFrame to every executor in the cluster. Memory impact = table size × number of executors.

Example: 500MB table, 200 executors → 100GB of total executor memory consumed just for that broadcast. If executors have 8GB each, this exceeds their capacity → OOM → executor crashes.

When to AVOID broadcasting:

  • Table is larger than ~200-400MB (depends on executor memory).
  • Cluster has many executors — even a modest-sized table becomes a memory problem at scale.
  • The "small" table has variable size — today it's 50MB, next month 2GB. Hardcoded broadcast hints become time bombs.
  • Already under OOM pressure — broadcasting adds fixed memory overhead regardless of the query.

Best practice: Rely on AQE's automatic broadcast decision (spark.sql.adaptive.enabled=true) which uses actual runtime statistics rather than your estimate. Only use explicit broadcast hints when you're certain the table fits and AQE's size estimate is wrong (e.g., it doesn't know about the filtered size after a predicate).

⚠️
Check spark.sql.autoBroadcastJoinThreshold (default 10MB). If you're using explicit broadcast() hint on a 200MB table and the default threshold is 10MB, you're overriding Spark's safety check intentionally — make sure your executors can handle it.
12
How do you monitor pipeline failures and set up alerting using Azure Monitor and Log Analytics?
MonitoringObservability
▼

Layer 1 — ADF Native Alerts: In ADF Monitor → Alerts → New Alert Rule. Trigger on: Pipeline run failed, Activity run failed, Trigger run failed. Notification: Email, SMS, Teams webhook, Azure Action Group.

Layer 2 — Azure Monitor + Log Analytics:

  1. Enable Diagnostic Settings on ADF → send logs (Pipeline runs, Activity runs, Trigger runs) to a Log Analytics Workspace.
  2. Same for Databricks cluster logs and Synapse diagnostics.
  3. Query with KQL (Kusto Query Language) in Log Analytics to build custom queries — e.g., pipelines that ran > 2x their normal duration, activity error rate > 5%.
  4. Create Alert Rules from KQL queries — trigger when query returns results.
📄 SQL
// KQL: Find all failed ADF pipeline runs in last 24h
ADFPipelineRun
| where TimeGenerated > ago(24h)
| where Status == "Failed"
| project PipelineName, Start, End, ErrorMessage
| order by Start desc

Layer 3 — Custom application-level metrics: Write audit rows from each pipeline (pipeline name, run ID, row count in/out, duration, status) to a SQL audit table. Build Power BI dashboard on top. Set row-count anomaly alerts (e.g., today's count < 80% of 7-day average).

Layer 4 — Escalation path: Azure Action Group → email (low severity) → SMS (medium) → PagerDuty/OpsGenie (high/production SLA breach). Define on-call rotation and escalation rules clearly.

13
What is a Data Vault and how does it differ from Star Schema?
Advanced Modelling
▼

Data Vault is a modelling methodology designed for enterprise data warehouses where sources are many, change frequently, and history must be preserved. It has three entity types:

  • Hub: Stores unique business keys (e.g., hub_customer with customer_id, hash key, load date, source). No attributes — just the key and metadata.
  • Link: Stores relationships between hubs (e.g., link_order_customer connects hub_order and hub_customer). Many-to-many relationships handled naturally.
  • Satellite: Stores descriptive attributes and their history (e.g., sat_customer_details with name, address, load date). Full history maintained automatically — every change adds a new row.

Data Vault vs Star Schema:

  • Agility: Data Vault is additive — add new sources by adding new satellites, never modifying existing structure. Star Schema requires redesign when sources change.
  • Query simplicity: Star Schema is much simpler to query (few JOINs, business-friendly). Data Vault requires complex JOINs across Hubs, Links, and Satellites — analysts never query it directly. You build a Business Vault or Information Mart (star schema) on top.
  • Use case: Data Vault for complex enterprise DW with 50+ sources. Star Schema for straightforward BI reporting with well-understood sources.
14
How do you design a data model for a multi-tenant SaaS application?
ScenarioDesign
▼

Multi-tenant data modelling requires balancing isolation, cost, and query performance across tenants:

Option 1 — Shared database, shared schema (most common): All tenants in the same tables, with a tenant_id column everywhere. Cheapest, easiest to scale. Requires Row-Level Security to ensure tenants can't see each other's data. Risk: one large tenant's queries can impact others (noisy neighbor).

Option 2 — Shared database, separate schema: Each tenant gets their own schema (tenant_a.orders, tenant_b.orders). Good isolation, same database. Works well up to ~100 tenants. Gets unwieldy at thousands.

Option 3 — Separate database per tenant: Maximum isolation, compliance friendly (GDPR — delete one database = delete one tenant's data). Most expensive to maintain at scale.

In a Lakehouse context: Use ADLS folder structure: /tenants/{tenant_id}/silver/, /tenants/{tenant_id}/gold/. ADLS POSIX ACLs restrict each tenant's service principal to their folder. Shared Databricks cluster reads all tenants but filters by tenant_id at the query layer. Synapse Serverless SQL external tables use folder-level access control.

💡
Always include tenant_id in partition keys and distribution keys. Queries filtered by tenant_id will prune entire partitions/distributions — critical for performance when you have 10,000 tenants.
15
How would you model a time-series dataset for efficient querying?
ScenarioTime-Series
▼

Time-series data (IoT sensor readings, stock prices, website events) has specific patterns: always appended (rarely updated), queried by time range and entity, aggregated over windows.

Partitioning: Partition by date (or date + hour for high-frequency data). partitionBy("date") in Delta/Parquet means time-range queries read only relevant partitions — critical for performance on years of data.

Ordering within partitions: Sort within partitions by (entity_id, timestamp). Combined with data skipping, this allows per-entity range queries to read very few files.

Z-Ordering: OPTIMIZE sensor_readings ZORDER BY (sensor_id, timestamp) colocates readings for the same sensor in the same files. Queries like WHERE sensor_id = 'S123' AND timestamp BETWEEN X AND Y become extremely fast.

Aggregation tables: Pre-compute hourly, daily, monthly aggregates as separate Delta tables. BI tools query the pre-aggregated table — not the raw 1-trillion-row table.

Retention policies: Delete/archive data older than the retention window using Delta VACUUM + ADLS lifecycle policies. Don't let the table grow unboundedly.

16
How do you enforce data quality checks in a large-scale pipeline?
Data QualityGovernance
▼

Four dimensions of data quality: Completeness, Accuracy, Consistency, Timeliness.

Checks by layer:

  • Bronze → Silver: Schema validation (expected columns present + correct types), null checks on required fields (PK, FK, business keys), row count > 0, no corrupt records.
  • Silver → Gold: Uniqueness on business keys, referential integrity (every FK has a matching PK in dimension), business rule validation (amount >= 0, status in allowed set, dates in valid range), count reconciliation with source system.

Implementation options:

  • Delta Lake CHECK constraints: ALTER TABLE orders ADD CONSTRAINT amount_positive CHECK (amount >= 0) — enforced on every write, any violation fails the write immediately.
  • Great Expectations (Python): Define expectations as code, validate DataFrames, generate HTML quality reports. Integrates with Databricks notebooks.
  • Apache Deequ (Scala/PySpark): Amazon's data quality library built on Spark. Runs constraint checks on DataFrames at scale, computes metrics, stores results.
  • Custom PySpark checks: After each transformation, assert expected conditions — fail loudly with clear error messages rather than silently writing bad data.
🐍 PySpark
# PySpark data quality check pattern
def validate_df(df, name):
    checks = {
        "zero_rows": df.count() > 0,
        "no_null_id": df.filter(col("customer_id").isNull()).count() == 0,
        "no_neg_amount": df.filter(col("amount") < 0).count() == 0,
    }
    failures = [k for k, v in checks.items() if not v]
    if failures:
        raise ValueError(f"{name} failed checks: {failures}")
17
How do you handle stakeholder requirements and changing needs as a data engineer?
CollaborationSoft Skills
▼

This is both a technical and a people problem. Key approaches:

  • Clarify the "why" before the "what": Stakeholders often request specific solutions when what they need is an outcome. Ask "What business decision will this data support?" before designing any pipeline.
  • Design for flexibility: Parameterize pipelines (new table = new config row, not new code). Use generic transformation patterns. Build a Silver layer that's schema-flexible so Gold layer changes don't require Bronze re-ingestion.
  • Version your data and schemas: Delta time travel means "we need the data as it was last Tuesday" is always answerable. Schema evolution support means adding a column doesn't break existing consumers.
  • Document data contracts: Publish a data catalog (Purview or Databricks Unity Catalog). When consumers know exactly what columns mean and what quality guarantees exist, requirement changes are more structured conversations.
  • Communicate impact of changes: When a stakeholder wants to change source frequency from daily to hourly, explain the infrastructure cost, pipeline complexity, and testing time required. Give options with tradeoffs, not just "yes" or "no".
  • Prioritize with a backlog: Competing requirements from multiple stakeholders need a prioritized backlog managed in Jira/Azure DevOps. Escalate conflicts to a data product owner, not to individual engineers.

Tell me about a challenging data engineering project you worked on.
Behavioral
▼

"I worked on migrating a legacy SAS-based data processing system to an Azure cloud platform for a Citibank project at TCS. The SAS scripts ran sequentially, generated large Excel reports, and were expensive to maintain due to licensing costs. My task was to re-architect this as a scalable, cloud-native pipeline.

I designed an end-to-end solution using ADF for ingestion from on-prem SQL Server into ADLS Gen2, Databricks PySpark for transformations, Synapse for analytical queries, and Power BI for reporting. The trickiest part was rewriting complex SAS business logic into distributed PySpark jobs while ensuring the output matched the legacy system exactly — we did row-level reconciliation to validate every figure.

The result was a pipeline that now runs in parallel across Spark executors, completing in a fraction of the time, with zero SAS licensing costs and a fully automated weekly schedule with alerting."

2
Have you ever faced a production failure? How did you handle it?
Behavioral
▼

"Yes. One Monday evening, our ADF pipeline that loads source tables into ADLS Gen2 failed mid-run. The Databricks transformation job downstream had no data to process, which would have impacted the Monday 6 PM scheduled delivery.

I immediately checked ADF Monitor and found a timeout error on the Copy Activity connected to SQL Server via the SHIR. I SSH'd to the SHIR machine and found the disk was 97% full — accumulated ADF diagnostic logs had filled it up over weeks. I cleared the logs, freed disk space, and manually re-triggered the pipeline. It completed successfully within 40 minutes.

For prevention, I set up a disk usage alert on the SHIR VM through Azure Monitor, added a log rotation scheduled task, and increased the Copy Activity retry count to 3 with exponential backoff so transient issues wouldn't cause immediate failures. I also documented the incident in our team runbook."

💡
The prevention steps are what separate a senior engineer from a junior one — always include what you did to stop it from happening again.
3
What would you do if your pipeline fails just before a client demo?
Situational
▼

"The first thing I would do is immediately alert the demo lead — not hide it and scramble quietly. I'd say: 'Pipeline X failed 30 minutes ago, here's the estimated impact, and here's what I'm doing.' Stakeholders always prefer early warning over late surprises.

Simultaneously, I'd triage: is this a full data failure, or is most of the data intact? If the previous successful run is clean, I'd point the demo to that dataset using Delta time travel — SELECT * FROM table VERSION AS OF N. That buys time. If I have 20 minutes, I'd attempt a quick fix on the specific failing activity rather than re-running the full pipeline.

After the demo, I'd do a full root cause analysis and add a pre-demo health check to our runbook — verifying pipeline status 2 hours before any scheduled demo so there's buffer time to react."

💡
Key message: communicate early, have a fallback plan, and prevent it next time. Delta time travel is a genuine technical answer that shows real expertise.

How do you handle multiple stakeholders with conflicting requirements?
Stakeholder Management
▼

"First, I try to understand whether the conflict is a priority conflict, a technical conflict, or a resource conflict — each needs a different resolution. If Team A wants daily data and Team B wants it hourly from the same source, that's a technical feasibility question, not just a priority question.

I bring both stakeholders together (or their representatives) and lay out the options with clear tradeoffs: 'Daily processing costs X and takes Y time. Hourly costs 3X and requires Z infrastructure changes. Here are the tradeoffs for your use cases.' Then I let them make the decision with full information — not me deciding for them.

If they still can't agree, I escalate to the project manager with the options document, not the conflict. I always document the agreed decision in writing — this prevents 'you said you'd do X' conversations three months later."

How do you explain a technical bottleneck to a non-technical client or stakeholder?
Communication
▼

"I never lead with the technical cause — I lead with the business impact. Not 'executor OOM due to shuffle skew' but 'our pipeline is taking 4 hours instead of 1, which means the morning report won't be ready until noon.'

Then I use an analogy: 'Think of it like a single checkout lane at a supermarket — when all 1,000 customers arrive at once, everyone waits. We need to open more lanes — that's what we're doing by adding more processing capacity.'

Then I give them two concrete options: 'Option A: I can apply a quick configuration fix today that buys us 2 more months before we need infrastructure changes. Option B: A proper fix takes 3 days but permanently solves it.' I let them choose. And I give a concrete timeline — 'I'll have Option A done by 3 PM today and will confirm over email.'"

💡
Impact first, analogy second, options third, timeline always. Never over-explain — stop once they have what they need to make a decision.

10
How do you ensure reliability and scalability in your data solutions?
Technical Behavioral
▼

"Reliability starts at design time, not after something breaks. Every pipeline I build is idempotent — it's safe to re-run without creating duplicates. I use watermarks, Delta MERGE operations, and partition overwrites to achieve this, so a failed and re-triggered run always produces the same correct output.

For monitoring, every pipeline I build writes an audit row — pipeline name, run ID, start/end time, rows in, rows out, status, error message. Azure Monitor alerts fire within 5 minutes of any failure. I've never had a business team discover a data outage before I knew about it.

For scalability, I design config-driven pipelines. Adding a new source table is a configuration row, not a code change. The same pipeline handles 1 table and 50 tables identically. On the Databricks side, I use partitioning and Z-ordering on Delta tables upfront based on the expected query patterns, not as an afterthought."

💡
Mention specific techniques you actually use — idempotency, audit tables, config-driven design. These show real engineering depth, not just vocabulary.
11
How do you ensure data quality and reliability in your projects?
Technical Behavioral
▼

"I treat data quality as a pipeline responsibility, not a downstream problem. In every pipeline I build, I add quality gates at each layer boundary. At Bronze, I check that the row count is greater than zero and the schema matches expectation. At Silver, I validate that primary key columns have no nulls, no duplicates, and that business rules hold — amounts are non-negative, dates are within valid ranges. At Gold, I reconcile aggregated totals with source system counts.

On the NYC Taxi project, I built a validation framework in Databricks that ran checks as assertions — if any check failed, the job raised an exception with a clear message about exactly which check failed and for which records. That meant we never silently wrote bad data to the Gold layer.

I also define a data contract with source teams upfront — expected schema, delivery SLA, and what happens if they breach it. When the source sends unexpected data, there's a process for it, not a surprised pipeline failure."

💡
Use the NYC Taxi validation framework as a concrete example — it shows you've actually built quality into a real project, not just talked about it.
