📋
Handy Reference
Handy reference — PySpark & Spark internals for quick revision
1
RDD vs DataFrame vs Dataset
▼

① RDD (Resilient Distributed Dataset) — Low-level distributed collection of objects in Spark.

  • Characteristics: Immutable, Distributed, Fault tolerant, Lazy evaluation, Supports functional programming.
  • Features: No Schema, No optimization, Full control to dev, Can store Structured/Unstructured data.
  • Advantages: Very flexible, Fine-grained transformations, Suitable for low-level operations.
  • Disadvantages: Slower, Higher memory consumption, No Catalyst optimization, No Tungsten optimization.

② DataFrames — Distributed collection of data organized into named columns. Similar to SQL table, Pandas DataFrame.

  • Characteristics: Schema based, Distributed, Optimized, Immutable.
  • Major optimizations: Catalyst Optimizer & Tungsten Engine.
  • Advantages: Faster than RDD, Less memory usage, Easy SQL integration, Easier syntax.
  • Disadvantages: Less control than RDD, No compile-time type safety.

③ Dataset — Strongly typed version of DataFrame ⇒ (DF + Safety). Available mainly in Scala & Java. Not supported in PySpark.

  • Characteristics: Type-Safe, Object-Oriented, Optimized.
  • Advantages: Compile-time type checking, Better error detection, Combines RDD + DF benefits.
  • Disadvantages: More complex, Not available in Python API.
🔄
Internal Architecture:
RDD → User Code → JVM Objects → Execution (No optimization layer)
DF/Dataset → User Query → Catalyst Optimizer → Tungsten Engine → Execution (Highly optimized)
📌
Use RDD for low-level transformations & flexibility. Use DataFrames for structured data and SQL-like operations. Use Datasets when type safety & performance are critical — Scala/Java only.
2
How Fault Tolerance is Achieved in Spark
▼

If a machine, executor, or task fails during distributed processing, Spark can recover the lost computation automatically without restarting the entire application.

  • ① RDD Lineage — Core fault tolerance mechanism. Every RDD maintains a lineage graph. Spark remembers where data came from, what transformations were applied, and the dependency chain.
  • ② DAG — Spark converts transformations into a DAG. The DAG scheduler tracks dependencies, identifies failed stages & reruns failed tasks.
  • ③ Task Re-execution: Spark divides work into stages & tasks. If a task fails, Spark retries it automatically.
  • ④ Lazy Evaluation helps recovery: Spark builds the execution plan first. Because Spark knows the full DAG before execution, it can efficiently recover failed computations.
  • ⑤ Checkpointing: Lineage chains can become very long — recomputation becomes expensive. Spark saves RDD physically to HDFS. On failure: Spark loads checkpointed data instead of recomputing entire lineage.
  • ⑥ Data Replication: When persisting RDDs, Spark supports replication — stores 2 copies across nodes. If one executor fails, another copy exists.
  • ⑦ Speculative Execution: Sometimes one executor becomes slow. Spark can run duplicate copies of slow tasks. Whichever finishes first is accepted — improves reliability & execution stability.
💡
Spark achieves fault tolerance mainly through RDD lineage, allowing lost partitions to be recomputed automatically if an executor or node fails. Also uses DAG scheduling, task retries, checkpointing, storage replication & speculative execution.
3
OOMs - Out of Memory Errors
▼

Out of Memory happens when JVM heap memory gets exhausted and the process cannot allocate more memory.

  • ① Driver OOM → Driver JVM
  • ② Executor OOM → Executor JVM
  • ③ Overhead OOM → Non-heap / Container memory

Understanding Driver Memory:

  • Driver JVM has limited heap memory (Ex → 4GB). If driver tries to hold too much metadata, huge collected data, or massive query plans, the memory exhausts.
  • Driver is not meant for large-scale data processing. Executors should process data. Driver should only co-ordinate, manage metadata & receive small summaries.
  • Total Driver Container Memory = Driver Heap Memory + Driver Overhead Memory (10% or min 384MB of total memory)

Why Driver OOM?

  • ① df.collect() → Entire distributed dataset comes to driver.
  • ② toPandas() → Very dangerous for big data — all distributed data converted into local Pandas df inside driver memory.
  • ③ Large Broadcast Variables — Driver creates broadcast object first → driver memory spike.
  • ④ Huge Number of Partitions → Driver stores task metadata. Millions of tiny partitions → massive metadata load.
  • ⑤ Complex Transformations — Nested joins, Unions, CTEs, Wide transformations → increase driver memory.
⚠️
spark.driver.memoryOverhead: Default → max(384MB, driverMemory × 0.10). Ex: driver memory = 4GB → driver overhead = 1GB. If driver starts behaving like executor, OOM risk becomes huge.

Why Executor OOM? Occurs when executor JVM heap/off-heap memory exhausts.

  • ① Data Skew → one executor processes too heavy partitions.
  • ② Large Shuffle → groupByKey, join, orderBy.
  • ③ Excessive Cache/Persist → storage memory full.
  • ④ Too many Concurrent Tasks → less memory per task.
  • ⑤ Using UDF → serializes data, uses Python worker memory heavily.
  • ⑥ Improper Partitioning → too few partitions, huge task memory load.

Task Execution Inside Executor:

  • If executor has 4 cores, then up to 4 tasks can run simultaneously.
  • Each task gets execution memory, thread, CPU slot.
  • One Partition = One Task. Tasks are distributed across executors.

Why Spill to Disk Exists:

  • Memory is limited. During sort, aggregation, and shuffle — data may exceed available memory.
  • Spark spills intermediate data to disk instead of failing immediately — trades speed for stability.
⚠️
Spill to disk is the second-worst outcome after OOM crashes — disk I/O is 100–1000× slower than RAM. Check Spark UI Stages tab for non-zero "Shuffle Spill (Disk)" values.
4
Narrow vs Wide & Pyspark Job & Memory Optimization
▼

Transformations define how data is processed in Spark. They do not execute immediately, They build the execution Plan.

Actions trigger actual execution. They return results to the driver. They execute the full pipeline. Examples are show(), count(), write()

Narrow Transformations: When one child partition depends on only one parent partition. No data movement across executors — no shuffle. Processing stays local to executor — Fast.

  • No Shuffle, No redistribution, No network transfer.
  • Ex: map(), filter(), flatMap()

Wide Transformations: When one child partition depends on multiple parent partitions. Requires Shuffle — data moves across partitions, executors & networks.

  • Ex: groupByKey(), join(), reduceByKey(), distinct(), orderBy(), repartition()
⚡
How to Optimize a PySpark Job:
① DataFrame/Dataset API instead of RDD  |  ② Filter early — Predicate Pushdown  |  ③ Select only required columns — Column Pruning  |  ④ Avoid unnecessary Shuffles  |  ⑤ Use AQE for dynamic optimization  |  ⑥ Partition data properly  |  ⑦ Cache/Persist if reused  |  ⑧ Avoid UDFs — use built-in functions  |  ⑨ Handle data Skew (Salting, repartition)  |  ⑩ Monitor & Tune Spark Configuration.
💡
Memory Optimization Techniques:
① Use DataFrames instead of RDD  |  ② Avoid huge collect()  |  ③ Use broadcast join for small tables  |  ④ Reduce Shuffle  |  ⑤ Use efficient file formats (Parquet, Delta)  |  ⑥ Avoid excessive caching  |  ⑦ Partition properly
5
SparkContext vs SparkSession & SparkSubmit
▼

SparkContext (Spark 1.x) — Only RDDs. The core entry point for Spark Core. Used for RDD operations, cluster communication, and task scheduling.

  • Responsibilities: Connect to cluster manager, Create RDDs, Schedule tasks, Manage executors.
  • from pyspark import SparkContext as SC → sc = SC("local", "APP")

SparkSession (from Spark 2.0) — It is a unified entry point for all Spark functionalities. Combines SparkContext, SQLContext, HiveContext.

  • SparkSession internally contains SparkContext.
  • Supports DF, SQL, Hive, Streaming, MLlib.
  • spark = SparkSession.builder.appName("Demo").getOrCreate()
💡
Why SparkSession was introduced: Earlier Spark had SparkContext, SQLContext, HiveContext as too many entry points. SparkSession unified all of the above into one.

SparkSubmit — The command-line tool used to submit Spark apps to a cluster. Standard way to launch Spark jobs, allocate resources, specify configurations, and deploy applications.

  • Why needed: Spark apps need executor, memory & cluster resources. spark-submit tells the cluster manager: how much memory, how many executors, which deploy mode, which application to run.
  • Basic Syntax: spark-submit [options] app.py
  • it is the standard command-line utility used to deploy Spark applications on a cluster. It handles resource allocation, app deployment, executor configuration, driver initialization & Communication with the cluster manager.
🔄
Flow: spark-submit → Cluster manager → Driver Created → Executors Allocated → Tasks Executed
6
Spark Join Strategies
▼

Based on table size, cluster resources, and Spark config — Spark chooses one of several join strategies.

  • ① Broadcast Join — 1 big table & 1 small table (≤10MB). Spark broadcasts the small table to every executor, builds an in-memory hash table from it, and joins locally with partitions of the larger dataset — avoiding expensive shuffles.
    Default: spark.sql.autoBroadcastJoinThreshold  |  Force: df1.join(broadcast(df2), "id")
  • ② Shuffle Hash Join (medium datasets) — When no table is small enough for broadcasting. Spark shuffles both tables by join key. After shuffle → matching keys reach same executor & hash table built for smaller side. No sorting required. Needs sufficient executor memory.
  • ③ Sort Merge Join (Default) — Both tables shuffled, sorted by join key & merged. Highly scalable & preferred for large distributed joins.
7
Distributed Variables in Spark — Broadcast Variables & Accumulators
▼

Normally, any variable used inside a transformation is serialized and sent to each task (once per task, potentially thousands of copies).

Broadcast Variables: is used to cache a large, read-only variable in memory across all executors. This Prevents to the need to ship a copy of data with every single task, so that data can be processed faster.

Data is sent once Per Executor & Cached locally, instead of once per task drastically reducing data transfer overhead.

🐍 Python
lookup_dict = {"NY": "New York", "CA": "California"}
broadcast_dict = spark.sparkContext.broadcast(lookup_dict)

# Inside transformation - all tasks on same executor share one copy
df.rdd.map(lambda row: broadcast_dict.value.get(row["state"], "Unknown")).toDF()
✅
Use broadcast variables for large lookup dictionaries, config maps, or ML models that need to be available on every executor. For DataFrames, use the broadcast() function hint instead.

Accumulators: is a shared variable used to aggregate values from all tasks across a cluster back to the driver.

These will be processed row-by-row in a distributed fashion in each of the executor & that will be updated once a row is processed.

🐍 Python
bad_records = spark.sparkContext.accumulator(0)

def process_row(row):
    if row["amount"] is None:
        bad_records.add(1)
        return None
    return row

df.foreach(process_row)
print(f"Bad records found: {bad_records.value}")
⚠️
Accumulators can be double-counted if a task is retried (Spark re-runs failed tasks). Use them for approximate monitoring metrics, not for business-critical counting. For exact counts, use DataFrame aggregations.
8
What is Checkpointing?
▼
⚡
What is Checkpointing?
Mechanism used by Spark to store progress of streaming pipelines so they can resume after failure.
it stores -- Progress information, offset tracking, State Store Data, Commit logs, metadata etc.,

Batch checkpointing: For very long lineage chains (>~20 chained transformations), rdd.checkpoint() materializes the RDD to ADLS/HDFS, breaking the lineage so recomputations start from the checkpoint.

Structured Streaming checkpointing: The checkpoint directory stores the streaming state — offset tracking (which Kafka messages have been processed), aggregation state (running totals, windowed counts), and query progress metadata. It is how exactly-once processing is guaranteed.

🚨
If the checkpoint directory is deleted: The streaming query loses all state. On restart it begins fresh — it may re-read old data, aggregations reset to zero, and exactly-once guarantees are broken. For stateful aggregations this constitutes data loss. Never delete the checkpoint directory of a running or paused streaming query.
🐍 Python
df.writeStream \
  .format("delta") \
  .option("checkpointLocation", "/mnt/checkpoints/mystream") \
  .start("/mnt/delta/output")
9
Serialization & Large CSV reading
▼

The process of converting an object into a stream of bytes so it can be stored, transferred, or transmitted.

  • Ex: Cust = {"id":101, "name":"Salman"} → Inside memory it exists as a complex object. To send it to network, write to disk, or shuffle across executors, Spark must convert it into bytes. This conversion is called Serialization.
  • During Shuffle, Broadcast Joins, Cache, N/W transfer — objects cannot travel directly.
📌
Read large CSV file & process it efficiently:
① Don't use inferSchema on huge files  |  ② Repartition if required  |  ③ Cache the df if reused  |  ④ Column Pruning  |  ⑤ Predicate Pushdown
10
What is DAG, Stage & Task?
▼

DAG (Directed Acyclic Graph) — The logical execution plan that Spark builds to determine how to execute transformations and actions efficiently across a distributed cluster. Nodes are transformations, edges define data dependencies — no circular loops.

  • Spark doesn't execute immediately (lazy evaluation) — it first creates a logical execution plan represented as a DAG.
  • DAG Scheduler: builds DAG → identifies shuffle boundaries → divides into stages → schedules tasks.

What is a Stage?

  • A collection of tasks that can be executed together without requiring a shuffle.
  • Spark breaks a DAG into stages whenever it encounters a shuffle operation.
  • ⇒ Wide Transformation = New Stage. groupBy, join(), distinct() → shuffle → stage boundary.
  • Ex 1: map → filter → flatMap → all narrow → only 1 stage.
  • Ex 2: map → filter → reduceByKey → Stage 1: map, filter | Shuffle | Stage 2: reduceByKey

Task — Smallest unit of execution in Spark. Each task processes exactly one partition.

📊
Spark hierarchy: Application → Job → Stage → Task
11
Partitioning, Z-Ordering, OPTIMIZE & VACUUM
▼

"These are all Delta Lake optimization features — each solves a different problem. Let me go through them one by one."

1. Partitioning

Physically splits the data into separate folders based on a column's values. When we write with partitionBy("year", "month"), Delta creates subfolders like /year=2024/month=01/. A query filtering on WHERE year=2024 AND month=01 only opens that one folder and skips everything else — this is called partition pruning.

--> Spark Partition is used for parallel processing (in memory)

--> Delta Partition is used for storage layout (folder structure)

  • Use on low cardinality columns — date, region, status. Never partition by user_id or order_id — that creates millions of tiny folders, which is the small file problem at the folder level.

Core goal of data partitioning is to - Reduce I/O cost, Reduced data scan & improve query performance.

🐍 PySpark
df.write.format("delta") \
  .partitionBy("year", "month") \
  .save("/mnt/delta/orders")

2. Z-Ordering

Partitioning only helps when you filter on the partition column. Z-Ordering helps for other high-cardinality columns you filter on frequently — like customer_id or order_id. It physically reorganizes data so related values are stored closer together — this improves file-level clustering and data skipping efficiency.

📌
How Data Skipping works underneath: Delta Lake stores file-level statistics for every data file — min/max value of each column, null count, record count (for the first 32 columns). So for a query like SELECT * FROM sales WHERE id = 250, if File 1 has id range 1–100 and File 2 has 101–200, Delta skips both files entirely without opening them — only File 3 (201–300) gets read. This happens automatically based on metadata, without needing partitioning.

Z-Ordering makes data skipping far more effective. When we Z-Order by order_id, Spark first sorts all the data by that column in the background, then redistributes it across files — so each file ends up with a tight, narrow min/max range instead of overlapping ranges. That means a filter on order_id can skip almost all files instead of just a few.

OPTIMIZE orders ZORDER BY (customer_id, order_date);
  • Z-ORDER is applied during OPTIMIZE — it rewrites files to cluster related rows together.
  • Best for columns that are not our partition column but are frequently in WHERE clauses or JOIN conditions.
  • Z-Ordering on multiple columns: If we Z-Order by (country, order_id), Spark sorts by the first column first — so it groups AUS, IND, USA together — then within each country group, it sorts by order_id. Rows with similar values across both columns end up stored together in the same files.

3. OPTIMIZE

Compacts many small files into fewer large files (target size ~1GB). Also applies Z-ORDER if specified. Run this as a scheduled maintenance job — weekly on large tables, or after heavy write operations.

It Improves - Query Performance, Scan Speed, Cluster Efficiency

-- Compact files only
OPTIMIZE orders; 
--Before: 1000 files of 10MB each → After: 10 files of 1GB each

-- Compact + Z-Order
OPTIMIZE orders ZORDER BY (customer_id);

4. VACUUM

Delta keeps old file versions for time travel. VACUUM deletes files that are older than the retention threshold and no longer needed. Without VACUUM, the storage keeps growing even as data is marked as "deleted."

-- Default retention is 7 days — keeps last 7 days of history
VACUUM orders;

-- Or specify retention explicitly
VACUUM orders RETAIN 168 HOURS;  -- 7 days
  • Never run VACUUM with 0 hours retention unless we're absolutely sure no query is reading old versions — it can break time travel and active streaming jobs reading from that table.
💡
Quick decision guide: Use Partitioning for date/region columns with low cardinality. Use Z-Ordering for high-cardinality columns you filter on. Run OPTIMIZE regularly for compaction. Run VACUUM to control storage costs.
12
Deletion Vectors & Liquid Clustering
▼

1. Deletion Vectors

In a normal scenario, whenever we modify a row in a Parquet file, the whole file gets re-written — even if you're only deleting one row out of a million. This is a real optimization issue at scale.

Deletion Vectors solve this. If deletion vectors are enabled on the table and you delete a single row, instead of rewriting that file, a deletion vector/flag gets added to that row — the data is not rewritten, the row in that file is just flagged as deleted. Next time the file is read, only the rows that are not flagged will be read.

When you delete or update rows in Delta without deletion vectors, Delta has to rewrite the entire Parquet file containing those rows — expensive. Deletion vectors fix this by writing a tiny bitmap file that marks deleted rows, leaving the original Parquet file untouched. The file is only rewritten physically when OPTIMIZE runs.

ALTER TABLE orders
SET TBLPROPERTIES ('delta.enableDeletionVectors' = 'true');

-- Now DELETE just flags the row — no file rewrite
DELETE FROM orders WHERE customer_id = 'C1001';
  • Big benefit for GDPR deletion requests or frequent small updates — you get the logical delete immediately without the write amplification of rewriting an entire file.

2. Liquid Clustering

This is the newest approach — it improves on both Partitioning and Z-Ordering by simplifying data layout decisions to optimize query performance. Instead of upfront partitioning decisions that are hard to change, Liquid Clustering gives you the flexibility to redefine clustering columns without rewriting existing data — the data layout can evolve alongside analytics needs over time.

  • Clustering is applied incrementally — only newly written or changed files get clustered. You don't have to rewrite the whole table to change your clustering strategy.
  • It avoids the main problem with partitioning (choosing the wrong column early and being stuck with it) and Z-Ordering (full table rewrite needed to change columns).

When to use Liquid Clustering:

Tables often filtered by high-cardinality columns.

Tables with significant skew in data.

Tables that grow quickly & require frequent maintenance and tuning.

Tables with access patterns that change over time.

Tables where a typical partition column could leave the table with too many or too few partitions.

-- Create a new table with Liquid Clustering
CREATE TABLE orders CLUSTER BY (customer_id, order_date)
USING DELTA AS
SELECT ...;

-- Enable / change clustering on an existing table — no rewrite needed
ALTER TABLE orders CLUSTER BY (region, order_date);

-- Revert back to a normal table
ALTER TABLE orders CLUSTER BY NONE;

-- Apply clustering to existing data
OPTIMIZE orders;
⚠️
Limitation: The clustering column should be within the range of the first 32 columns index of the table.
💡
Think of it this way: Partitioning locks you into a folder structure decided upfront, and changing it later means rewriting the whole table. Z-Ordering needs a full OPTIMIZE rewrite every time you want to change the sort columns. Liquid Clustering avoids both problems — you can redefine clustering columns anytime, and only future writes get the new layout.
💡
Quick decision guide: Use Liquid Clustering when you want flexibility to change your strategy later without pain. Enable Deletion Vectors for tables with frequent deletes/updates.
13
Time Travel and Versioning - when would you use them?
▼

"Delta Lake records every write, update, delete, and schema change as a new version in the transaction log. Time Travel lets you query the table as it looked at any previous version or timestamp — without any extra setup. It's automatic."

🐍 PySpark
# ── View version history ───────────────────────────────
spark.sql("DESCRIBE HISTORY orders").show(20, truncate=False)
# Shows: version | timestamp | operation | operationParameters | numOutputRows

# ── Query by version number ────────────────────────────
df_v5 = spark.read \
    .format("delta") \
    .option("versionAsOf", 5) \
    .load("/mnt/delta/orders")

# ── Query by timestamp ─────────────────────────────────
df_yesterday = spark.read \
    .format("delta") \
    .option("timestampAsOf", "2024-01-14 18:00:00") \
    .load("/mnt/delta/orders")

# SQL syntax
spark.sql("SELECT * FROM orders VERSION AS OF 5")
spark.sql("SELECT * FROM orders TIMESTAMP AS OF '2024-01-14 18:00:00'")

# ── Restore a table to a previous version ─────────────
spark.sql("RESTORE TABLE orders TO VERSION AS OF 5")
spark.sql("RESTORE TABLE orders TO TIMESTAMP AS OF '2024-01-14'")

# ── Compare two versions — useful for debugging ────────
v_now  = spark.sql("SELECT * FROM orders VERSION AS OF 10")
v_prev = spark.sql("SELECT * FROM orders VERSION AS OF 9")

changed = v_now.exceptAll(v_prev)
print(f"Rows that changed: {changed.count()}")

When I actually use it:

  • Bad transformation run: A Gold layer mart gets overwritten with wrong aggregation logic. I RESTORE the table to the last good version — done in seconds, no re-ingestion from Bronze needed. I've used this on the NYC Taxi project after a faulty groupBy produced wrong borough totals.
  • Audit and compliance: "What did the revenue table look like on the day the quarterly report was signed?" Query by timestamp — exact answer, no guessing.
  • Debugging incremental logic: If my watermark-based pipeline loaded wrong data for a specific window, I can query that exact version, compare with the previous version, and see exactly what changed.
  • Rollback after accidental DELETE: Someone runs DELETE without a WHERE clause. RESTORE gets the data back atomically. No backup restore process, no downtime.

How long is history kept? Delta retains old file versions until you run VACUUM. Default retention is 7 days — you can time travel up to 7 days back. Beyond that, VACUUM has cleaned up the old Parquet files even though the log entries still exist.

⚠️
Never run VACUUM orders RETAIN 0 HOURS — this deletes all old files immediately, breaking any active streaming readers and eliminating all time travel history. Always keep at least 7 days of retention.
14
CDC (Change Data Capture) and CDF (Change Data Feed) in Databricks.
▼

What is CDC (Change Data Capture)?

CDC is a technique used to capture only the changed records (Inserts, Updates and Deletes) from a source system instead of reading the entire table every time.

Without CDC: Source database contains 50 million rows. Only 500 rows changed. Still we read all 50 million rows and overwrite the target table, resulting in long runtime, high database load, high network traffic and expensive compute cost.

With CDC: Source database contains 50 million rows. Only 500 rows changed. CDC captures only those 500 changed records, so only the changed rows are processed and updated. In Databricks, these changes are typically applied using MERGE INTO.

Why CDC?

CDC captures Inserts, Updates and Deletes from source systems and avoids full table loads.

Common CDC Methods

1. Timestamp Based: Uses last modified timestamp to identify changed records.

2. Trigger Based: Database triggers capture changes and store them in audit tables.

3. Log Based: Reads database transaction logs to identify inserts, updates and deletes.

Azure Data Factory (ADF) also provides built-in CDC capabilities.

Typical CDC Flow: Oracle DB → CDC → Bronze Delta → Silver Delta → Gold Delta

What is CDF (Change Data Feed)?

Change Data Feed (CDF) is a Delta Lake feature that exposes row-level changes between table versions.

It tracks Inserts, Updates and Deletes and is commonly used for incremental processing of Delta tables.

Without CDF

Gold layer reads the entire Silver table every time.

With CDF

Gold layer reads only the changed records since the previous run.

Enable CDF

ALTER TABLE silver.customer_silver
SET TBLPROPERTIES (
  delta.enableChangeDataFeed = true)

Reading CDF in PySpark

spark.read.format("delta")
.option("readChangeFeed", "true")
.table("customer")

Benefits of CDF

Incremental processing

Efficient downstream synchronization

Faster Gold layer processing

Reduced compute cost

Reduced data movement

CDC captures changes from source systems and avoids full table loads. It identifies Inserts, Updates and Deletes using techniques such as Timestamp Based CDC, Trigger Based CDC and Log Based CDC.
CDF is a Delta Lake feature that exposes row-level changes between Delta table versions. Instead of reading an entire table, downstream layers can process only changed records, making incremental pipelines much faster and more cost efficient.
15
You need to process 10 GB of data using Spark. How would you determine the number of partitions, executors, cores and memory required for maximum parallelism?
▼

"This is a cluster sizing question, and the key thing I always say first is — Spark parallelism depends on the number of partitions, not the raw data size. So my first job is to figure out how many partitions I need, then work backwards to executors and memory. One partition is processed by one task, and ideally one task runs on one core."

Number of Tasks = Number of Partitions
Maximum Parallelism = Number of Available Cores

Step 1 — Decide ideal partition size: I target 128MB–256MB per partition — that's the sweet spot Spark works best with. Let's take 128MB.

Step 2 — Calculate total number of partitions:

Number of Partitions = Total Data Size (MB) / Partition Size (MB)
(10 * 1024 MB) / 128 = 80 Partitions
📌
80 Partitions = 80 Tasks = 80 Cores required for maximum parallelism.

Step 3 — Decide cores per executor: Best practice is 3–5 cores per executor — going beyond 5 hurts HDFS/ADLS throughput due to too many concurrent connections from one JVM. I'll take 5 cores per executor.

Step 4 — Calculate number of executors:

Executors = Total Cores / Cores Per Executor
80 / 5 = 16 Executors

Step 5 — Estimate memory per executor: Each executor processes 5 partitions simultaneously.

5 cores * 128 MB (partition size) = 640 MB
-- This is the memory required for active task execution

Step 6 — Consider Spark's Unified Memory Model:

  • Execution Memory — used for shuffle, joins, sorts and aggregations
  • Storage Memory — used for cache and persisted DataFrames

To provide enough execution and storage space, I apply a buffer factor (~3.5x) to account for overhead, shuffle, and storage:

640 MB * 3.5 ≈ 2240 MB
≈ 2.6 GB per core

Step 7 — Calculate executor memory:

2.6 GB * 5 cores
≈ 13 GB per executor
Final Cluster Configuration
Total Partitions
80
Cores Per Executor
5
No. of Executors
16
Memory Per Executor
~13 GB

"Within that 13GB, Spark's unified memory model splits the spark.memory.fraction roughly 50/50 between storage (caching) and execution (shuffles, joins, aggregations) — and they can borrow from each other dynamically. Spark memory itself is typically around 60% of total executor memory, with the rest going to user memory and reserved memory."

💡
"Spark parallelism depends on partitions rather than data size. For a 10GB dataset, assuming a partition size of 128MB, we get around 80 partitions. Since one partition is processed by one task, we need approximately 80 cores for maximum parallelism. If we configure 5 cores per executor, we need 16 executors. Based on Spark's unified memory model and execution requirements, I'd allocate approximately 13GB memory per executor — so the final config is 16 executors, 5 cores per executor, 13GB executor memory. That said, this is a starting calculation — in practice I'd profile the actual job in Spark UI, check for skew and spill, and adjust from there. Static formulas give a good baseline; the real tuning happens by observing how the job actually runs."
🐍
Python
Python fundamentals, OOP, decorators, generators, ETL patterns & data engineering-specific usage
1
What are the key Python data structures? Which ones do you use most in data engineering?
▼

"In day-to-day data engineering work, I use a handful of Python structures constantly — the choice depends on what I'm doing with the data."

  • List: Ordered, mutable, allows duplicates. I use lists for sequences of items I need to iterate or transform — like a list of table names to loop through in a metadata-driven pipeline.
  • Dictionary: Key-value pairs. Probably my most-used structure in data engineering — config maps, schema definitions, column mappings, API responses (JSON), lookup tables for code-to-description translation.
  • Set: Unordered, unique values. I use sets for fast membership checks and deduplication — checking if a record ID has already been processed, finding the difference between two sets of column names (schema drift detection).
  • Tuple: Ordered, immutable. I use tuples for fixed data that shouldn't change — like coordinate pairs, column name + data type pairs, or return values from functions where I want to unpack multiple values.
  • List comprehension: Not a structure itself but a pattern I use constantly — cleaner and faster than a for loop for simple transformations.
🐍 Python
# Dictionary — most common in ETL config
table_config = {
    "orders":    {"watermark_col": "updated_at", "pk": "order_id"},
    "customers": {"watermark_col": "modified_date", "pk": "cust_id"},
}

# Set — schema drift detection
expected_cols = {"id", "name", "amount", "date"}
actual_cols   = {"id", "name", "amount", "date", "new_col"}
new_cols      = actual_cols - expected_cols
print(f"New columns detected: {new_cols}")  # {'new_col'}

# List comprehension — filter tables to process
tables_to_load = [t for t in table_config if t != "audit"]

# Tuple unpacking — return multiple values cleanly
def get_bounds(df):
    return df["id"].min(), df["id"].max()

min_id, max_id = get_bounds(df)
2
What is the difference between a list and a tuple? When would you use each?
▼
  • List — mutable (can change after creation), uses square brackets [], slightly more memory. Use when the collection will be modified — appending rows, removing items, updating values.
  • Tuple — immutable (cannot change after creation), uses parentheses (), slightly faster and less memory. Use for fixed data that should never be modified — like column schema definitions, coordinate pairs, or dictionary keys (lists can't be dict keys, tuples can).
🐍 Python
# List — mutable, use when data changes
pipeline_stages = ["ingest", "validate", "transform"]
pipeline_stages.append("load")   # works fine

# Tuple — immutable, use for fixed data
schema = ("customer_id", "INT", "NOT NULL")
# schema.append("something")  # would raise AttributeError

# Tuple as dictionary key (lists can't do this)
location_data = {
    (28.6139, 77.2090): "Delhi",
    (19.0760, 72.8777): "Mumbai"
}

# Function returning multiple values uses tuple under the hood
def min_max(values):
    return min(values), max(values)

lo, hi = min_max([10, 5, 8, 3, 15])
3
What are decorators in Python? Have you used them in your work?
▼

"A decorator is a function that wraps another function to add behaviour before or after it runs — without modifying the original function's code. It's Python's way of saying 'do this extra thing every time this function is called.'"

In data engineering, decorators are useful for: retry logic, logging execution time, validating inputs before a function runs, and catching exceptions consistently across multiple functions without repeating the same try/except block everywhere.

🐍 Python
import time
import functools

# 1. Simple timing decorator — useful for profiling ETL steps
def timer(func):
    @functools.wraps(func)
    def wrapper(*args, **kwargs):
        start = time.time()
        result = func(*args, **kwargs)
        end = time.time()
        print(f"{func.__name__} took {end - start:.2f}s")
        return result
    return wrapper

@timer
def load_table(table_name):
    # simulate loading
    time.sleep(1)
    return f"{table_name} loaded"

load_table("orders")  # prints: load_table took 1.00s

# 2. Retry decorator — for flaky API calls or DB connections
def retry(max_attempts=3, delay=2):
    def decorator(func):
        @functools.wraps(func)
        def wrapper(*args, **kwargs):
            for attempt in range(1, max_attempts + 1):
                try:
                    return func(*args, **kwargs)
                except Exception as e:
                    print(f"Attempt {attempt} failed: {e}")
                    if attempt < max_attempts:
                        time.sleep(delay)
            raise Exception(f"{func.__name__} failed after {max_attempts} attempts")
        return wrapper
    return decorator

@retry(max_attempts=3, delay=2)
def fetch_api_data(url):
    # if this raises, it will retry 3 times
    import requests
    return requests.get(url).json()
💡
The @functools.wraps(func) line is important — without it, the wrapped function loses its original name and docstring, which makes debugging harder.
4
What are generators in Python? How are they different from regular functions?
▼

"A generator is a function that uses yield instead of return. The key difference is — a regular function computes the entire result and returns it all at once. A generator produces values one at a time, on demand, and pauses between each one. It doesn't hold the entire result in memory."

This is extremely useful in data engineering when processing large files or datasets — you don't want to load 10 million rows into a Python list at once. A generator lets you process one chunk at a time.

🐍 Python
# Regular function — loads ALL rows into memory at once
def read_all_rows(filepath):
    rows = []
    with open(filepath) as f:
        for line in f:
            rows.append(line.strip())
    return rows  # entire file in memory

# Generator — yields one row at a time, memory efficient
def read_rows_lazily(filepath):
    with open(filepath) as f:
        for line in f:
            yield line.strip()  # pauses here, resumes on next()

# Usage — same interface, 1000x less memory for large files
for row in read_rows_lazily("large_file.csv"):
    process(row)

# Chunk-based reading for ETL — process 10k rows at a time
def read_in_chunks(filepath, chunk_size=10000):
    chunk = []
    with open(filepath) as f:
        for line in f:
            chunk.append(line.strip())
            if len(chunk) == chunk_size:
                yield chunk
                chunk = []
    if chunk:
        yield chunk  # last partial chunk

for chunk in read_in_chunks("orders.csv"):
    df = spark.createDataFrame(chunk)
    df.write.format("delta").mode("append").save("/mnt/delta/orders")
💡
Generator expressions also exist — like list comprehensions but lazy: (x*2 for x in range(1000000)) uses almost no memory, while [x*2 for x in range(1000000)] creates the full list in RAM immediately.
5
Explain OOP in Python — the 4 pillars: Encapsulation, Abstraction, Inheritance & Polymorphism
▼

"OOP has 4 pillars — and each one solves a specific design problem. Let me explain each with a data engineering angle."

1. Encapsulation

Bundling data (attributes) and the methods that work on that data together inside a class, and restricting direct access to internals. The idea is — hide the implementation details, expose only what's needed. In Python, we use a single underscore _ for "internal use" and double underscore __ for name-mangled private attributes.

🐍 Python
class PipelineConfig:
    def __init__(self, table_name, mode):
        self.table_name  = table_name   # public
        self._mode       = mode         # internal — don't access directly
        self.__secret_key = "abc123"    # private — name mangled

    # Controlled access via property
    @property
    def mode(self):
        return self._mode

    @mode.setter
    def mode(self, value):
        allowed = ["append", "overwrite", "merge"]
        if value not in allowed:
            raise ValueError(f"Mode must be one of {allowed}")
        self._mode = value

config = PipelineConfig("orders", "append")
config.mode = "overwrite"   # goes through setter — validated
# config.mode = "delete"   # raises ValueError
print(config.mode)          # "overwrite"

2. Abstraction

Hiding complex implementation details and exposing only a clean, simple interface. The user of a class shouldn't need to know HOW something works internally — just WHAT it does. In Python, abstraction is achieved using abstract base classes (ABC).

🐍 Python
from abc import ABC, abstractmethod

# Abstract class — defines WHAT must be done, not HOW
class BaseWriter(ABC):

    @abstractmethod
    def write(self, df, path):
        """Every writer must implement this"""
        pass

    @abstractmethod
    def validate(self, df):
        """Every writer must validate before writing"""
        pass

    # Concrete method — shared logic, no override needed
    def log(self, path, count):
        print(f"Written {count} rows to {path}")

# Cannot instantiate abstract class directly
# writer = BaseWriter()  # raises TypeError

# Must implement all abstract methods
class DeltaWriter(BaseWriter):
    def write(self, df, path):
        df.write.format("delta").mode("overwrite").save(path)
        self.log(path, df.count())

    def validate(self, df):
        if df.count() == 0:
            raise ValueError("Cannot write empty DataFrame")

class ParquetWriter(BaseWriter):
    def write(self, df, path):
        df.write.format("parquet").save(path)
        self.log(path, df.count())

    def validate(self, df):
        if df.count() == 0:
            raise ValueError("Cannot write empty DataFrame")

3. Inheritance

A child class inherits attributes and methods from a parent class, and can extend or override them. Avoids code duplication — common logic goes in the parent, specific logic in the child.

🐍 Python
class BaseReader:
    def __init__(self, source_name):
        self.source_name = source_name

    def validate(self, df):
        if df.count() == 0:
            raise ValueError(f"{self.source_name} returned 0 rows")
        return df

    def read(self):
        raise NotImplementedError

# SqlReader inherits validate() from BaseReader for free
class SqlReader(BaseReader):
    def __init__(self, table_name, jdbc_url):
        super().__init__(source_name=table_name)
        self.jdbc_url = jdbc_url

    def read(self):
        df = spark.read.jdbc(self.jdbc_url, self.table_name)
        return self.validate(df)   # inherited from BaseReader

class AdlsReader(BaseReader):
    def __init__(self, path):
        super().__init__(source_name=path)
        self.path = path

    def read(self):
        df = spark.read.parquet(self.path)
        return self.validate(df)   # same inherited method

4. Polymorphism

"Poly" = many, "morph" = forms. The same method name behaves differently depending on which class's object is calling it. This lets you write generic code that works with multiple types — you don't care which specific reader it is, you just call .read() and it does the right thing for its type.

🐍 Python
# Same method name — different behaviour per class
class SqlReader(BaseReader):
    def read(self):
        print(f"Reading from SQL: {self.source_name}")
        return spark.read.jdbc(...)

class AdlsReader(BaseReader):
    def read(self):
        print(f"Reading from ADLS: {self.source_name}")
        return spark.read.parquet(...)

class ApiReader(BaseReader):
    def read(self):
        print(f"Reading from API: {self.source_name}")
        return requests.get(self.source_name).json()

# Polymorphism in action — same loop, different behaviour
readers = [
    SqlReader("orders", jdbc_url),
    AdlsReader("/mnt/bronze/customers"),
    ApiReader("https://api.example.com/products"),
]

# We don't care what type each reader is
# Each .read() call does the right thing for its class
for reader in readers:
    data = reader.read()
    process(data)
💡
Quick way to remember all 4:
Encapsulation — hide the data, control access via methods.
Abstraction — hide the HOW, expose only the WHAT (interface).
Inheritance — child gets parent's features, avoids code duplication.
Polymorphism — same method name, different behaviour per class.

Key OOP concepts used here:

  • Class: Blueprint for creating objects — BaseReader, SqlReader
  • Inheritance: SqlReader inherits from BaseReader — gets validate() for free
  • __init__: Constructor — runs when object is created, sets up attributes
  • super(): Calls the parent class's __init__ — so I don't duplicate that setup code
  • Polymorphism: Both readers have a read() method — I can loop through them uniformly without caring which type each is
6
What is the difference between *args and **kwargs?
▼

*args — lets a function accept any number of positional arguments. They come in as a tuple.

**kwargs — lets a function accept any number of keyword arguments (name=value pairs). They come in as a dictionary.

🐍 Python
# *args — variable positional arguments
def log_tables(*args):
    for table in args:
        print(f"Processing: {table}")

log_tables("orders", "customers", "products")
# Processing: orders
# Processing: customers
# Processing: products

# **kwargs — variable keyword arguments
def run_pipeline(**kwargs):
    print(f"Source: {kwargs.get('source')}")
    print(f"Target: {kwargs.get('target')}")
    print(f"Mode:   {kwargs.get('mode', 'append')}")

run_pipeline(source="sql_server", target="/mnt/delta/orders", mode="overwrite")

# Common real use — passing config dict to a function
config = {"source": "adls", "target": "/mnt/gold", "mode": "merge"}
run_pipeline(**config)   # unpacks dict as keyword arguments

# Airflow tasks use **kwargs to receive context
def my_task(**kwargs):
    ti = kwargs["ti"]              # task instance
    execution_date = kwargs["execution_date"]
    print(f"Running for: {execution_date}")
💡
You've already seen **kwargs in Airflow tasks — when we extract ti = kwargs["ti"] to access XCom, that's **kwargs in action. Airflow passes the full task context as keyword arguments to every Python callable.
7
What is the difference between map(), filter() and reduce()?
▼
  • map() — applies a function to every item in an iterable, returns a new iterable of the same length. One-to-one transformation.
  • filter() — applies a function that returns True/False to every item, keeps only items where the result is True. Reduces the collection.
  • reduce() — applies a function cumulatively to items, reducing the whole collection to a single value. Needs functools.reduce.
🐍 Python
from functools import reduce

amounts = [100, 250, None, 400, None, 75]

# map() — transform every item
# Replace None with 0 across all amounts
cleaned = list(map(lambda x: x if x is not None else 0, amounts))
print(cleaned)  # [100, 250, 0, 400, 0, 75]

# filter() — keep only matching items
# Remove None values
valid = list(filter(lambda x: x is not None, amounts))
print(valid)  # [100, 250, 400, 75]

# reduce() — collapse to single value
# Sum all valid amounts
total = reduce(lambda acc, x: acc + x, valid)
print(total)  # 825

# In practice — list comprehensions are more Pythonic
# map equivalent:
cleaned = [x if x is not None else 0 for x in amounts]
# filter equivalent:
valid   = [x for x in amounts if x is not None]
💡
In data engineering, map() and filter() are more commonly replaced by list comprehensions for readability. But they're still useful when passing functions as arguments — and they're conceptually identical to Spark's .map() and .filter() on RDDs, so understanding them helps with Spark internals too.
8
What is exception handling in Python? How do you handle errors in ETL pipelines?
▼

"Exception handling lets us anticipate errors, handle them gracefully, and continue or fail with a clear message — instead of the program crashing with an unhelpful traceback."

🐍 Python
import logging

logging.basicConfig(level=logging.INFO)
logger = logging.getLogger(__name__)

# Basic try/except/finally
def load_table(table_name, jdbc_url):
    try:
        df = spark.read.jdbc(jdbc_url, table_name)
        row_count = df.count()
        logger.info(f"{table_name}: {row_count} rows loaded")
        return df

    except Exception as e:
        logger.error(f"{table_name} failed: {str(e)}")
        raise  # re-raise so ADF/Airflow knows it failed

    finally:
        # always runs — good for cleanup
        logger.info(f"Finished attempt for {table_name}")

# Multiple exception types
def read_config(path):
    try:
        with open(path) as f:
            return json.load(f)
    except FileNotFoundError:
        raise FileNotFoundError(f"Config not found at: {path}")
    except json.JSONDecodeError as e:
        raise ValueError(f"Config file is not valid JSON: {e}")

# Custom exception — useful for pipeline-specific errors
class DataQualityError(Exception):
    pass

def validate(df, table_name):
    if df.count() == 0:
        raise DataQualityError(f"{table_name} has 0 rows — aborting pipeline")
    null_ids = df.filter(col("id").isNull()).count()
    if null_ids > 0:
        raise DataQualityError(f"{table_name} has {null_ids} null primary keys")
💡
Always raise after logging in a pipeline function — if you catch the error and don't re-raise it, the orchestrator (ADF, Airflow) thinks the task succeeded and marks it green. Silent failures are the worst kind in production.
9
What is the difference between deepcopy and shallow copy?
▼

Shallow copy: Creates a new object, but the elements inside still reference the same memory locations as the original. Changing a nested object in the copy also changes it in the original.

Deep copy: Creates a completely independent copy — the new object and all objects nested inside it are new. Changes to the copy don't affect the original at all.

🐍 Python
import copy

original = {"tables": ["orders", "customers"], "config": {"mode": "append"}}

# Shallow copy — nested objects still shared
shallow = copy.copy(original)
shallow["tables"].append("products")
print(original["tables"])  # ['orders', 'customers', 'products'] — ALSO changed!

# Deep copy — fully independent
original2 = {"tables": ["orders", "customers"], "config": {"mode": "append"}}
deep = copy.deepcopy(original2)
deep["tables"].append("products")
print(original2["tables"])  # ['orders', 'customers'] — unchanged ✓

# In data engineering — relevant when copying pipeline configs
# If you reuse a config dict across multiple pipeline runs,
# always deepcopy it first so one run's changes don't bleed into another
base_config = {"mode": "append", "filters": []}

run1_config = copy.deepcopy(base_config)
run1_config["filters"].append("date = '2024-01-01'")

run2_config = copy.deepcopy(base_config)
run2_config["filters"].append("date = '2024-01-02'")

# Both are independent — no cross-contamination

How do you read and write files in Python? How do you work with JSON and CSV?
▼

"File I/O comes up constantly in data engineering — reading config files, writing audit logs, processing flat files before loading to a data lake."

🐍 Python
import json
import csv

# ── Reading / Writing text files ──────────────────────
with open("pipeline.log", "w") as f:
    f.write("Pipeline started\n")

with open("pipeline.log", "r") as f:
    content = f.read()

# Always use 'with' — auto-closes the file even if an error occurs

# ── JSON ───────────────────────────────────────────────
# Reading config files
with open("pipeline_config.json", "r") as f:
    config = json.load(f)        # file → dict
    table_name = config["table"]

# Writing JSON
audit_record = {"pipeline": "orders", "rows": 5000, "status": "success"}
with open("audit.json", "w") as f:
    json.dump(audit_record, f, indent=2)  # dict → file

# String ↔ JSON (common with API responses)
json_str = '{"id": 1, "name": "Salman"}'
data = json.loads(json_str)   # string → dict
back = json.dumps(data)       # dict → string

# ── CSV ────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Reading CSV
with open("orders.csv", "r") as f:
    reader = csv.DictReader(f)   # each row is a dict
    for row in reader:
        print(row["order_id"], row["amount"])

# Writing CSV
headers = ["order_id", "amount", "status"]
rows    = [["O001", 500, "completed"], ["O002", 300, "pending"]]

with open("output.csv", "w", newline="") as f:
    writer = csv.writer(f)
    writer.writerow(headers)
    writer.writerows(rows)
11
How do you implement parallel processing in Python for ETL? Explain threading vs multiprocessing.
▼

"Python has a GIL (Global Interpreter Lock) — only one thread executes Python bytecode at a time. This means threading is NOT truly parallel for CPU-bound tasks, but it IS effective for I/O-bound tasks where threads spend most of their time waiting."

  • Threading — best for I/O-bound tasks: API calls, database reads/writes, file uploads to ADLS. While one thread waits for a network response, another thread can run. Multiple source tables loading simultaneously is a great use case.
  • Multiprocessing — best for CPU-bound tasks: heavy data transformations, compression, parsing. Each process gets its own GIL so they genuinely run in parallel.
🐍 Python
from concurrent.futures import ThreadPoolExecutor, ProcessPoolExecutor, as_completed

tables = ["orders", "customers", "products", "inventory", "payments"]

# Threading — I/O bound (DB reads, API calls, file uploads)
def ingest_table(table_name):
    df = read_from_sql(table_name)
    write_to_adls(df, f"/mnt/bronze/{table_name}")
    return f"{table_name}: {df.count()} rows"

with ThreadPoolExecutor(max_workers=5) as executor:
    futures = {executor.submit(ingest_table, t): t for t in tables}
    for future in as_completed(futures):
        try:
            print(future.result())
        except Exception as e:
            print(f"{futures[future]} failed: {e}")

# Multiprocessing — CPU bound (heavy transformation)
def transform_chunk(chunk):
    # expensive CPU work — parsing, compression, aggregation
    return [process_row(row) for row in chunk]

chunks = split_into_chunks(large_dataset, n=8)

with ProcessPoolExecutor(max_workers=4) as executor:
    results = list(executor.map(transform_chunk, chunks))
💡
"In my ETL pipelines, I use ThreadPoolExecutor for parallel table ingestion — all tables connect and read from the source simultaneously. For heavy CPU transformation work I prefer Spark (which handles parallelism across a cluster) over Python multiprocessing."
12
What are lambda functions? When do you use them?
▼

"A lambda is an anonymous, single-expression function defined inline. It's not for complex logic — it's for simple, throwaway operations that don't need a full def block."

🐍 Python
# Regular function vs lambda — same result
def double(x): return x * 2
double_lambda = lambda x: x * 2

# Most common use — as argument to sorted(), map(), filter()
tables = [
    {"name": "orders",    "row_count": 5000},
    {"name": "customers", "row_count": 1200},
    {"name": "products",  "row_count": 300},
]

# Sort by row_count
sorted_tables = sorted(tables, key=lambda t: t["row_count"], reverse=True)

# Filter only large tables
large_tables = list(filter(lambda t: t["row_count"] > 1000, tables))

# With map — apply transformation to each item
table_names = list(map(lambda t: t["name"].upper(), tables))
# ['ORDERS', 'CUSTOMERS', 'PRODUCTS']

# Real ETL use — sorting columns for consistent schema comparison
schema_cols = ["amount", "id", "date", "name"]
schema_cols.sort(key=lambda c: c.lower())
💡
Don't overuse lambdas for complex logic — if it doesn't fit on one line readably, use a proper def function. Lambdas are for simplicity, not compression.
13
What are Python's built-in string methods? Which ones do you use most in data engineering?
▼

"String manipulation comes up constantly in data engineering — cleaning column names, parsing file paths, validating formats, building dynamic SQL queries."

🐍 Python
# Most used string methods in data engineering

# strip / lstrip / rstrip — remove whitespace or characters
"  orders  ".strip()          # "orders"
"/mnt/delta/".rstrip("/")     # "/mnt/delta"

# lower / upper — normalise before comparison
"Customer_ID".lower()         # "customer_id"
col_name = "  Order_Date  ".strip().lower().replace(" ", "_")
# "order_date"

# split / join — parse and rebuild strings
path = "/mnt/bronze/orders/2024/01"
parts = path.split("/")       # ['', 'mnt', 'bronze', 'orders', '2024', '01']
"/".join(parts[1:])           # "mnt/bronze/orders/2024/01"

# replace — clean values
"₹1,00,000".replace(",", "").replace("₹", "")  # "100000"

# startswith / endswith — check file type
filename = "orders_2024.parquet"
filename.endswith(".parquet")  # True
filename.startswith("orders")  # True

# in — membership check
"delta" in "/mnt/delta/orders"  # True

# format / f-strings — build dynamic paths and queries
table   = "orders"
schema  = "silver"
path    = f"/mnt/delta/{schema}/{table}"
query   = f"SELECT * FROM {schema}.{table} WHERE date = '2024-01-01'"

# zfill — pad numbers (useful for file naming)
f"part_{str(1).zfill(4)}.parquet"  # "part_0001.parquet"
14
What is the difference between is and == in Python?
▼

== checks value equality — are the two objects equal in value?

is checks identity — are the two variables pointing to the exact same object in memory?

🐍 Python
a = [1, 2, 3]
b = [1, 2, 3]
c = a

print(a == b)   # True  — same values
print(a is b)   # False — different objects in memory
print(a is c)   # True  — c points to the SAME object as a

# The classic trap
x = None
if x is None:    # correct — use 'is' for None checks
    print("x is None")
if x == None:    # works but not Pythonic — avoid this
    print("x is None")

# Why it matters in data engineering
config1 = {"mode": "append"}
config2 = config1            # same object — changing one changes both!
config3 = config1.copy()     # new object — independent

config2["mode"] = "overwrite"
print(config1["mode"])  # "overwrite" — config1 also changed (same object)
print(config3["mode"])  # "append"    — config3 unchanged (different object)
💡
Always use is when checking for None, True, or False. Use == for value comparisons. This is a common Python interview gotcha.
15
What are context managers in Python? Why is with important?
▼

"A context manager handles setup and teardown automatically — it guarantees that certain cleanup code always runs, even if an exception occurs. The with statement is Python's way of using them."

In data engineering, the most common use is file handling — but it's also critical for database connections, locks, and timing blocks.

🐍 Python
# Without context manager — risky
f = open("data.csv", "r")
data = f.read()
f.close()   # if an error happens before this, file stays open forever

# With context manager — always closes, even on error
with open("data.csv", "r") as f:
    data = f.read()
# file is automatically closed here, no matter what

# Building a custom context manager — using contextlib
from contextlib import contextmanager
import time

@contextmanager
def timer(label):
    start = time.time()
    try:
        yield    # code inside the 'with' block runs here
    finally:
        elapsed = time.time() - start
        print(f"{label} took {elapsed:.2f}s")

# Usage
with timer("Bronze ingestion"):
    df = spark.read.parquet("/mnt/bronze/orders")
    df.write.format("delta").save("/mnt/silver/orders")
# prints: Bronze ingestion took 12.45s

# Database connection context manager
@contextmanager
def get_db_connection(conn_string):
    conn = create_connection(conn_string)
    try:
        yield conn
    except Exception as e:
        conn.rollback()
        raise
    finally:
        conn.close()   # always closes

with get_db_connection(CONN_STRING) as conn:
    conn.execute("UPDATE watermark SET last_load = GETDATE()")
16
What are Python type hints? Why should you use them?
▼

"Type hints let you declare the expected data types of function arguments and return values. Python doesn't enforce them at runtime — but they make code much more readable, help IDEs give better autocomplete, and catch bugs early when used with tools like mypy."

🐍 Python
from typing import Optional, List, Dict, Tuple, Any
from pyspark.sql import DataFrame

# Without type hints — hard to know what goes in and what comes out
def process(table, config, mode):
    pass

# With type hints — immediately clear
def process(
    table_name : str,
    config     : Dict[str, Any],
    mode       : str = "append"
) -> DataFrame:
    pass

# Optional — value can be the type OR None
def get_watermark(table_name: str) -> Optional[str]:
    result = db.query(f"SELECT max_date FROM watermark WHERE table='{table_name}'")
    return result[0] if result else None

# List, Dict type hints
def load_tables(
    table_names : List[str],
    schema_map  : Dict[str, str]
) -> List[DataFrame]:
    return [spark.read.table(t) for t in table_names]

# Tuple with specific types
def get_row_stats(df: DataFrame) -> Tuple[int, int]:
    total    = df.count()
    non_null = df.dropna().count()
    return total, non_null

total, non_null = get_row_stats(orders_df)
💡
Type hints don't affect performance — they're purely for readability and tooling. In production ETL code with many functions, they're the difference between readable, self-documenting code and a codebase nobody wants to maintain.
17
How do you work with environment variables and secrets in Python? How do you manage configs across environments?
▼

"Never hardcode connection strings, passwords, or API keys in Python code — they end up in Git history and create security vulnerabilities. In data engineering, I manage secrets at multiple levels depending on the environment."

🐍 Python
import os
from dotenv import load_dotenv    # pip install python-dotenv

# ── Local development — .env file ──────────────────────
# .env file (never commit this to Git — add to .gitignore)
# DB_HOST=localhost
# DB_PASSWORD=mypassword
# ADLS_KEY=abc123

load_dotenv()   # loads .env into environment variables

db_host = os.getenv("DB_HOST")
db_pass = os.getenv("DB_PASSWORD")
adls_key = os.getenv("ADLS_KEY", "default_if_missing")

# ── Production — Azure Key Vault ───────────────────────
from azure.keyvault.secrets import SecretClient
from azure.identity import DefaultAzureCredential

def get_secret(secret_name: str) -> str:
    credential  = DefaultAzureCredential()
    client      = SecretClient(
        vault_url="https://my-keyvault.vault.azure.net/",
        credential=credential
    )
    return client.get_secret(secret_name).value

db_password = get_secret("sql-server-password")

# ── Databricks — dbutils.secrets ───────────────────────
# In a Databricks notebook, secrets come from secret scopes
storage_key = dbutils.secrets.get(scope="kv-scope", key="adls-key")
jdbc_pass   = dbutils.secrets.get(scope="kv-scope", key="sql-password")

# ── Config per environment ─────────────────────────────
# Avoid if/else env checks scattered through code
# Use a config class instead

class Config:
    ENV = os.getenv("ENVIRONMENT", "dev")

    STORAGE_PATH = {
        "dev" : "/mnt/dev/bronze",
        "test": "/mnt/test/bronze",
        "prod": "/mnt/prod/bronze",
    }[ENV]

    DB_SERVER = {
        "dev" : "dev-sql-server.database.windows.net",
        "prod": "prod-sql-server.database.windows.net",
    }.get(ENV, "dev-sql-server.database.windows.net")

# Usage anywhere in the codebase
from config import Config
print(Config.STORAGE_PATH)   # /mnt/dev/bronze or /mnt/prod/bronze
⚠️
Always add .env to your .gitignore. A leaked .env file with real Azure connection strings in a public GitHub repo is one of the most common cloud security incidents.
18
How do you write unit tests in Python? How do you test ETL logic?
▼

"Testing ETL code is harder than testing regular application code because the logic depends on DataFrames and external systems. But transformation logic — the business rules — absolutely should be tested. A bug in a transformation discovered in production is 10x more painful than one caught in a unit test."

🐍 Python
import pytest
from pyspark.sql import SparkSession
from pyspark.sql.types import StructType, StructField, StringType, IntegerType

# ── The function we want to test ───────────────────────
def clean_orders(df):
    """Remove nulls in order_id, filter negative amounts, uppercase status"""
    from pyspark.sql.functions import col, upper
    return (df
        .filter(col("order_id").isNotNull())
        .filter(col("amount") >= 0)
        .withColumn("status", upper(col("status")))
    )

# ── Test file: test_transformations.py ─────────────────
@pytest.fixture(scope="session")
def spark():
    return (SparkSession.builder
        .master("local[1]")
        .appName("unit_tests")
        .getOrCreate())

def test_clean_orders_removes_null_ids(spark):
    data = [
        (1,   500, "completed"),
        (None, 300, "pending"),   # should be removed
        (3,   200, "completed"),
    ]
    schema = ["order_id", "amount", "status"]
    df     = spark.createDataFrame(data, schema)
    result = clean_orders(df)
    assert result.count() == 2   # null row removed

def test_clean_orders_filters_negative_amounts(spark):
    data = [
        (1,  500, "completed"),
        (2,  -50, "refund"),    # should be removed
    ]
    df     = spark.createDataFrame(data, ["order_id", "amount", "status"])
    result = clean_orders(df)
    assert result.count() == 1

def test_clean_orders_uppercases_status(spark):
    data   = [(1, 500, "completed")]
    df     = spark.createDataFrame(data, ["order_id", "amount", "status"])
    result = clean_orders(df)
    assert result.first()["status"] == "COMPLETED"

# Run with: pytest test_transformations.py -v
💡
"I test transformation logic with small, controlled DataFrames — I create the input data explicitly, run the function, and assert the output. I never test against production data in unit tests. The goal is to test one behaviour per test so failures tell you exactly what broke."
19
What is the difference between mutable and immutable objects? Why does it matter in Python?
▼

Mutable — can be changed after creation. Lists, dictionaries, sets.

Immutable — cannot be changed after creation. Strings, integers, floats, tuples. Any "change" creates a new object.

🐍 Python
# Immutable — string
name = "salman"
name.upper()      # creates NEW string, doesn't change original
print(name)       # still "salman"
name = name.upper()  # must reassign to keep the change

# Mutable — list
tables = ["orders", "customers"]
tables.append("products")  # modifies IN PLACE
print(tables)     # ["orders", "customers", "products"]

# The most common trap — mutable default argument
# WRONG — the list is created once and shared across all calls
def add_table(table, tables=[]):
    tables.append(table)
    return tables

add_table("orders")    # ["orders"]
add_table("customers") # ["orders", "customers"] — NOT a fresh list!

# CORRECT — use None as default, create fresh list inside
def add_table(table, tables=None):
    if tables is None:
        tables = []
    tables.append(table)
    return tables

# Mutable objects passed to functions — changes affect original
def process(config):
    config["processed"] = True  # modifies the ORIGINAL dict

my_config = {"table": "orders"}
process(my_config)
print(my_config)  # {"table": "orders", "processed": True}
# If you don't want this — pass a copy: process(my_config.copy())
⚠️
The mutable default argument bug is one of Python's most infamous gotchas. It trips up even experienced developers — always use None as the default for mutable parameters.
20
How do you handle date and time in Python? What functions do you use most?
▼

"Dates come up everywhere in data engineering — watermark columns, partition paths, filename generation, filtering incremental loads. Python's datetime module handles most of this."

🐍 Python
from datetime import datetime, date, timedelta

# ── Get current date/time ──────────────────────────────
now       = datetime.now()           # datetime object — 2024-01-15 08:30:00
today     = date.today()             # date object — 2024-01-15
timestamp = datetime.now().strftime("%Y%m%d_%H%M%S")  # "20240115_083000"

# ── Format datetime to string ──────────────────────────
dt = datetime(2024, 1, 15, 8, 30)
dt.strftime("%Y-%m-%d")              # "2024-01-15"
dt.strftime("%d/%m/%Y %H:%M")        # "15/01/2024 08:30"
dt.strftime("%Y/%m/%d")              # "2024/01/15" — for partition paths

# ── Parse string to datetime ───────────────────────────
date_str = "2024-01-15"
dt = datetime.strptime(date_str, "%Y-%m-%d")

# ── Date arithmetic ────────────────────────────────────
yesterday   = date.today() - timedelta(days=1)
last_week   = date.today() - timedelta(weeks=1)
next_30_days = date.today() + timedelta(days=30)

# ── Watermark pattern — most common ETL use ────────────
def get_incremental_range(last_load_date: str) -> tuple:
    """Returns start and end dates for incremental extraction"""
    start = datetime.strptime(last_load_date, "%Y-%m-%d")
    end   = datetime.now()
    return start.strftime("%Y-%m-%d"), end.strftime("%Y-%m-%d")

start, end = get_incremental_range("2024-01-01")
query = f"SELECT * FROM orders WHERE updated_at BETWEEN '{start}' AND '{end}'"

# ── Partition path generation ──────────────────────────
run_date = datetime.now()
path = f"/mnt/bronze/orders/year={run_date.year}/month={run_date.month:02d}"
# /mnt/bronze/orders/year=2024/month=01

# ── Filename with timestamp ────────────────────────────
filename = f"audit_log_{datetime.now().strftime('%Y%m%d_%H%M%S')}.json"
# audit_log_20240115_083000.json
💡
strftime = datetime → string ("format time"). strptime = string → datetime ("parse time"). The "f" and "p" are the easiest way to remember which is which.
📊
Pandas & NumPy
Data manipulation, array operations, and numerical computing — from basics to interview-ready
Pandas
1
What is Pandas and why do we need it? How is it different from plain Python lists?
▼

"Pandas is a Python library built for data manipulation and analysis. The core reason we need it is that plain Python lists and dictionaries don't understand the concept of a table — rows, columns, data types per column, missing values, or aggregations. Pandas does all of that natively."

  • Plain Python list: One-dimensional, no column names, no data types, manual loops for any operation.
  • Pandas DataFrame: Two-dimensional table with named columns, mixed data types per column, built-in operations for filtering, grouping, merging, and reshaping — all without writing explicit loops.
  • Why it matters in data engineering: Before data reaches Spark or a data lake, a lot of config reading, small-file processing, audit log writing, and API response parsing happens in Pandas. It's also the closest Python equivalent to a SQL table, so it's the natural tool for small-to-medium data that doesn't need a distributed cluster.
🐍 Python
import pandas as pd

# Plain Python — manual and verbose
rows = [["O001", 500], ["O002", 300], ["O003", 700]]
total = sum(row[1] for row in rows)   # have to know index 1 = amount

# Pandas — expressive and readable
df = pd.DataFrame(rows, columns=["order_id", "amount"])
total = df["amount"].sum()            # clear, named column access
print(total)                          # 1500

# DataFrame gives you the table structure immediately
print(df)
#   order_id  amount
# 0     O001     500
# 1     O002     300
# 2     O003     700
2
What is the difference between a Series and a DataFrame?
▼

Series — a single one-dimensional column of data with an index. Think of it as a single column from a spreadsheet.

DataFrame — a two-dimensional table made up of multiple Series, each sharing the same index. Think of it as a full spreadsheet with rows and columns.

🐍 Python
import pandas as pd

# Series — 1D, like a single column
amounts = pd.Series([500, 300, 700], name="amount")
print(amounts)
# 0    500
# 1    300
# 2    700
# Name: amount, dtype: int64

print(type(amounts))  # 

# DataFrame — 2D table
df = pd.DataFrame({
    "order_id": ["O001", "O002", "O003"],
    "amount":   [500, 300, 700]
})
print(type(df))       # 

# Selecting a column from a DataFrame returns a Series
col = df["amount"]
print(type(col))      # 

# Selecting multiple columns returns a DataFrame
subset = df[["order_id", "amount"]]
print(type(subset))   #
3
How do you read and write data in Pandas? CSV, Excel, JSON, SQL.
▼
🐍 Python
import pandas as pd

# ── Reading ────────────────────────────────────────────
df = pd.read_csv("orders.csv")
df = pd.read_csv("orders.csv", sep="|", encoding="utf-8")
df = pd.read_csv("orders.csv", usecols=["order_id","amount"])  # specific cols only
df = pd.read_csv("orders.csv", chunksize=10000)                # read in chunks

df = pd.read_excel("report.xlsx", sheet_name="Orders")
df = pd.read_json("response.json")
df = pd.read_json("response.json", orient="records")

import sqlalchemy
engine = sqlalchemy.create_engine("mssql+pyodbc://...")
df = pd.read_sql("SELECT * FROM orders WHERE status='active'", con=engine)
df = pd.read_sql_table("orders", con=engine)

# ── Writing ────────────────────────────────────────────
df.to_csv("output.csv", index=False)           # index=False avoids extra index column
df.to_excel("output.xlsx", sheet_name="Result", index=False)
df.to_json("output.json", orient="records", indent=2)
df.to_sql("orders_staging", con=engine, if_exists="replace", index=False)
# if_exists: 'replace' drops & recreates, 'append' adds rows, 'fail' errors if exists
